Java Technical Reference

This page is designed for learning at an advanced level: each part not only has the concept, but also a production perspective, code/flow examples, common pitfalls, and how to answer in interviews. The goal is to help you relearn Java/Spring/Microservices/Kafka/DB/K8s in depth, not to learn generic definitions.

Deep Topics

Search by keywords such as OOP, HashMap, Transaction, Outbox, Kafka, N+1, Kubernetes. The content prioritizes how to use it in real projects and how to apply them in real-world engineering.

1. Java Core for Backend

Do not learn Java Core as definitions. Learn by being able to explain behavior, trade-off, production bugs and performance.

#1
Key focus
Real-world OOP in backend
Concept

OOP (object-oriented programming) is a way of organizing code around objects — bundling together data (state) and behavior (methods) that operate on that data, instead of leaving them scattered apart.

Why it exists: So that code reflects the business accurately, is easy to change and hard to put into an invalid state. The four pillars: Encapsulation, Abstraction, Inheritance, Polymorphism.

Analogy: Like an ATM: you press the "withdraw cash" button (method), you do not reach in and edit the balance (state) directly.

How it works
  1. Encapsulation: hide state inside, only allow changes through valid methods → the object stays correct (keeps the invariant).
  2. Abstraction: only expose "what it can do" (interface), hide "how it does it" (implementation).
  3. Inheritance: the child class reuses the state/behavior of the parent class — but creates tight coupling.
  4. Polymorphism: the same method call, different implementations run depending on the actual object → drop if/else by type.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Adding a new payment provider (VNPay, Momo, Stripe, ZaloPay): adding another if/else branch each time is a sign you need Strategy + registry Map<Type, Handler>.
  2. Two services both set order.status directly: conflicting state (A sets PAID, B sets CONFIRMED). Gather the invariant into business methods confirm/pay/cancel.
  3. A 500-line god service with a switch by type: nobody dares to change it. Split it into a registry of processors, each small class with its own test.
  4. Inheritance for a business type (PremiumUser extends User): when you need to change tier at runtime you must migrate data → switch to composition (User has-a Tier).
  5. One-impl interfaces everywhere: only create an interface when there are ≥2 real impls, you need a mock at a boundary, or it is an infrastructure port.

A strong engineer does not only talk about Encapsulation/Inheritance/Polymorphism/Abstraction. A strong engineer must know how to use OOP to reduce coupling, keep the domain model meaningful, avoid a service God class and avoid abusing inheritance.

What to understand deeply
  • Encapsulation = protecting the invariant, not getter/setter

    The real goal is for the object to always be in a valid state. Allowing setStatus(String) means the outside can set any value, including meaningless ones. Instead, expose business actions like confirm(), cancel(), pay() — each method checks the valid state itself before changing it.

    // Bad: invariant is not protected
order.setStatus("PAID");
order.setTotalAmount(BigDecimal.valueOf(-100));

// Good: the object protects itself
order.confirm();   // throw if order is empty or already CANCELLED
order.pay(paymentId); // throw if not yet CONFIRMED
  • Composition over Inheritance in business code

    Inheritance creates tight coupling: changing the parent can silently break the child. In the domain, prefer composition + interface to swap behavior freely. Inheritance is reasonable for frameworks/libraries, less reasonable for business types.

    // Bad: PremiumUser extends User
class PremiumUser extends User { ... }   // hard to add GoldUser, VipUser later

// Good: User has-a tier (composition)
class User {
    private UserTier tier; // BASIC, PREMIUM, GOLD, VIP
    public Money discountFor(Order o) {
        return tier.discountPolicy().calculate(o);
    }
}
  • Polymorphism replaces a long if/else

    Every switch/if-else by "type" is a sign of missing polymorphism. Split it into an interface + multiple implementations. When adding a new provider/rule, you only add a class — you do not edit old code (Open/Closed Principle).

    // Bad
if (type.equals("VNPAY")) { ... }
else if (type.equals("MOMO")) { ... }
else if (type.equals("STRIPE")) { ... }

// Good
interface PaymentGateway { PaymentResult charge(Payment p); }
class VnpayGateway implements PaymentGateway { ... }
class MomoGateway implements PaymentGateway { ... }
// The service only calls: gateways.get(type).charge(payment);
  • Abstraction = a domain capability, not mirroring infrastructure

    An interface must represent business meaning (a "port" in hexagonal), not a copy of the implementation. Don't create UserServiceImpl just because of a rule — only create an interface when you need a mock for testing, have multiple impls, or it is the boundary between domain & infrastructure.

    // Bad: interface only mirrors EmailClient
interface EmailService { void sendEmail(String to, String body); }

// Good: capability by domain
interface NotificationPort {
    void notify(Customer c, NotificationEvent e);
}
// Adapter implements it via email / SMS / push depending on context
Production issues / pitfalls
  • Anemic Domain Model — the entity is just a DTO

    The entity has only getters/setters, all rules scattered across services. Consequence: service A sets status = "PAID" while skipping the payment id check, service B resets it = "CONFIRMED" → data inconsistent. The bug is hard to debug because nowhere "owns" the invariant.

    // Symptom of anemic:
order.setStatus(OrderStatus.PAID);  // anyone can call it
order.setPaidAt(null);              // valid?
orderRepository.save(order);

// Fix: only expose meaningful actions
order.markAsPaid(paymentId, clock.now());
  • Inheritance for a business type → hard to refactor

    The class hierarchy BasicUser/PremiumUser/VipUser is rigid — when the business needs "a user can change tier", you have to create a new user and migrate data. Composition (User has-a Tier) lets you change tier with one line user.upgradeTo(VIP).

  • One-impl interfaces everywhere

    Every service has 1 interface + 1 impl just to "match the pattern" → the IDE navigates through 2 layers, code review is heavy, no real benefit. Only create an interface when: you need a mock for testing (Mockito can mock a concrete class, so this is not a mandatory reason), there are ≥2 real impls, or it is a port between layers.

  • God service: a 200-line method with a switch by type

    One OrderService.process() contains 20 if/else branches for each order type. Nobody dares to change it because testing is hard. Split it with a strategy registry: Map<OrderType, OrderProcessor>, each processor a small class with its own test.

Example
// Junior style: the object is just a data bag
class Order {
    private String status;
    public void setStatus(String status) { this.status = status; }
}

// Recommended style: the object keeps the invariant
class Order {
    private OrderStatus status;
    private Money totalAmount;

    public void confirm() {
        if (status != OrderStatus.DRAFT) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Only draft order can be confirmed");
        }
        if (totalAmount.isZeroOrNegative()) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Order total must be positive");
        }
        this.status = OrderStatus.CONFIRMED;
    }

    public void cancel() {
        if (status == OrderStatus.PAID) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Paid order cannot be cancelled directly");
        }
        this.status = OrderStatus.CANCELLED;
    }
}
Interview answer tip: When asked about OOP, don't answer with theory. Say: 'I use OOP to protect the invariant, express business behavior and reduce if/else with polymorphism. For example Order.confirm() checks the valid state instead of letting the service set the status directly.'
Key focus
HashMap, equals/hashCode, Collections internals
Concept

HashMap stores key→value pairs allowing average O(1) lookup. hashCode() returns an int to place an object into a "bucket"; equals() answers "are two objects equal".

Why it exists: To find a value by key extremely fast without scanning everything. You need both hashCode (to locate the bucket) and equals (to compare within the bucket) for the map to work correctly.

Analogy: hashCode = the shelf number of the filing cabinet; equals = reading the name on each folder on that shelf.

How it works
  1. put(key,value): compute hashCode(key) → derive the bucket index.
  2. Bucket empty → place the entry. Already has an entry (collision) → use equals to compare each key in the bucket.
  3. Java 8+: a bucket that is too crowded (>8 entries) switches from a linked list to a red-black tree for O(log n) lookup.
  4. get(key): repeat — hashCode finds the bucket, equals finds the right key.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Mutable key: using an object whose equals/hashCode depend on a field that gets changed → after changing the field, get() returns null even though the key "still exists". Use an immutable key (id, record).
  2. Forgetting to override equals/hashCode: using an object as a cache/dedup key → each instance is a different key → complete cache miss, memory leak.
  3. HashMap used across threads: multiple threads put concurrently → corruption / infinite loop (Java 7). Use ConcurrentHashMap.
  4. ConcurrentModificationException: modifying the map/list while iterating → use iterator.remove() or copy first.
  5. Cache with no size limit: using a HashMap as an unbounded cache → OOM. Use Caffeine / LinkedHashMap LRU with maxSize + TTL.

A strong engineer needs to be able to explain why bugs happen when using a mutable object as a key, why HashMap degrades performance, and when to use ConcurrentHashMap.

What to understand deeply
  • HashMap internals: hashCode → bucket index → equals

    On put(k,v): compute (n-1) & hash(k.hashCode()) to choose the bucket. On get(k): same bucket, then iterate the linked list/tree in the bucket, calling equals to find the right entry. So equals and hashCode must be consistent: a.equals(b) ⇒ a.hashCode() == b.hashCode().

    // Java 8 hash spread function
static final int hash(Object key) {
    int h;
    return (key == null) ? 0 : (h = key.hashCode()) ^ (h >>> 16);
}
  • Poorly distributed hashCode → degrades to O(n)

    If hashCode returns a few values (worst: a constant), many keys fall into the same bucket → get/put must scan the whole list. Real-world example: a large object overrides hashCode using only 1 categorical field (status enum) → at over 10k entries, performance is worse than an ArrayList.

  • Java 8 treeify — fixes the symptom, not the root cause

    When a bucket has > 8 entries and map size > 64, Java converts the linked list → a red-black tree (O(log n) instead of O(n)). This is a safety net, not permission to write a bad hashCode. Tree nodes cost more memory and Comparable helps make it more stable.

  • The key must be immutable with respect to the equals/hashCode fields

    After put, if a field changes → the new hashCode differs → map.get(key) will look in the wrong bucket, returning null even though the key still exists. Especially dangerous with a JPA entity that has no id yet (id = null at put time, after flush id != null).

    Map<User, Score> map = new HashMap<>();
User u = new User("alice@x.com");
map.put(u, score);
u.setEmail("alice@y.com"); // hashCode changes
map.get(u); // null — the entry is "orphaned" in the old bucket
Production issues / pitfalls
  • Override equals but forget hashCode (or vice versa)

    Two objects are equal but their hashCodes differ → a HashSet holds both, a HashMap cannot find the key. Lombok @EqualsAndHashCode or the IDE generates both. When writing by hand, always remember this pair.

  • A JPA entity with no id yet used as a key

    Before persist() id = null; after persist + flush, id ≠ null. If hashCode is based on id, the hashCode changes → the entity "disappears" from the Set. Fix: hashCode uses a business key (email, externalId), or use Objects.hash(getClass()) + equals based on id (accepting hashCode collisions for transient entities).

  • ArrayList.contains() in a large loop — O(n²)

    10k records × contains linear = 100 million comparisons. Convert to a HashSet before the loop to get O(1) lookup.

    // Bad: O(n*m)
for (Order o : orders) {
    if (blacklist.contains(o.getCustomerId())) skip(); // List linear scan
}

// Good: O(n+m)
Set<String> blacklistSet = new HashSet<>(blacklist);
for (Order o : orders) {
    if (blacklistSet.contains(o.getCustomerId())) skip();
}
  • HashMap multi-thread write — Java 7 infinite loop, Java 8 lost update

    On Java 7, a concurrent resize can create a circular linked list → CPU at 100% forever (there are many famous production bug histories). Java 8 does not loop but still has lost updates / null reads. Use ConcurrentHashMap for shared mutable state.

Example
class UserKey {
    private String email;

    // BAD: email is mutable but used in hashCode
    public void setEmail(String email) { this.email = email; }

    @Override
    public int hashCode() {
        return Objects.hash(email);
    }

    @Override
    public boolean equals(Object o) {
        if (!(o instanceof UserKey other)) return false;
        return Objects.equals(email, other.email);
    }
}

// If you change the email after putting it into the HashMap,
// map.get(key) may no longer find it.
Interview answer tip: Add something about production: the hard-to-debug bug usually is not HashMap being wrong, but the key being mutated, equals/hashCode being unstable, or the entity lifecycle making the id change.
Key focus
Concurrency, Thread Pool, CompletableFuture
Concept

Concurrency is running multiple tasks "at the same time". A Thread Pool is a group of reusable threads to run tasks; CompletableFuture is an API to run and compose asynchronous tasks.

Why it exists: Creating/destroying threads is very expensive and threads are a limited resource. A pool controls the number of threads, avoiding unbounded creation that crashes the machine. CompletableFuture helps call many I/O in parallel without blocking.

Analogy: A pool = a shop with 5 employees (threads); when customers (tasks) are many they queue, you do not hire unlimited employees.

How it works
  1. Submit a task to the pool → the task queues up.
  2. The pool takes an idle thread to run the task; when out of threads the task waits in the queue (backpressure).
  3. supplyAsync(task, executor) runs the task on the pool, returns a future immediately; thenCombine/thenCompose composes multiple futures.
  4. Always include a timeout + exception handling; more parallelism does not mean faster (the bottleneck is at the DB pool).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Using the common ForkJoinPool for I/O: a blocking call occupies the JVM-wide shared pool (parallel stream) → the whole app freezes. Define a dedicated executor.
  2. Forgetting a timeout on CompletableFuture: one slow downstream → the thread waits forever → the pool drains. Always orTimeout/completeOnTimeout.
  3. Parallelism exceeding the DB pool capacity: 100 parallel tasks but a pool of 10 connections → choke + timeout. Limit concurrency to match resources.
  4. Swallowing exceptions in async: an error in supplyAsync that is not handled simply disappears → always exceptionally/handle + log the correlation id.
  5. ThreadLocal leak in a pool: setting a ThreadLocal without clearing → a later request reads data from a previous request (a security flaw). Clear it in finally.

A strong engineer must know that concurrency is not only for running faster, but also to control resources: thread pool, timeout, backpressure, connection pool, DB pool and failure handling.

What to understand deeply
  • CompletableFuture for independent parallel I/O

    When building an "aggregator endpoint" that calls 3 downstream APIs (user, order, score), if called sequentially total latency = the sum. Called in parallel with CompletableFuture, the total ≈ the max. Suitable when the calls are truly independent (do not need each other's results).

    // Sequential: 100+150+200 = 450ms
User u = userClient.get(id);
List<Order> o = orderClient.list(id);
Score s = scoreClient.get(id);

// Parallel: max(100,150,200) ≈ 200ms
CompletableFuture<User> fu = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> userClient.get(id), exec);
CompletableFuture<List<Order>> fo = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> orderClient.list(id), exec);
CompletableFuture<Score> fs = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> scoreClient.get(id), exec);
CompletableFuture.allOf(fu, fo, fs).join();
  • Do not use commonPool — define a dedicated executor per task type

    ForkJoinPool.commonPool() is shared across the whole JVM, including parallel streams. One slow task can block another parallel stream. In production you should have a dedicated executor for each task type (DB, HTTP, CPU-bound) with appropriate configuration.

    @Bean("httpExecutor")
ExecutorService httpExecutor() {
    return new ThreadPoolExecutor(
        10, 50, 60L, TimeUnit.SECONDS,
        new ArrayBlockingQueue<>(200),
        new ThreadFactoryBuilder().setNameFormat("http-%d").build(),
        new ThreadPoolExecutor.CallerRunsPolicy() // backpressure
    );
}
  • Parallel ≠ Faster — you must measure the real bottleneck

    If the DB pool has 10 connections but you create 100 parallel DB tasks → 90 tasks wait. Worse than sequential due to thread thrashing and queue overhead. Always measure: connection pool, DB CPU, network throughput before increasing parallelism.

  • Timeout + Exception handling + Correlation id propagation

    orTimeout(2, SECONDS) for each future. exceptionally() or handle() to handle errors. The MDC (correlation id) does not propagate automatically to another thread — you must copy it manually.

    Map<String,String> mdc = MDC.getCopyOfContextMap();
CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> {
    MDC.setContextMap(mdc); // restore correlation id
    try {
        return externalClient.call();
    } finally {
        MDC.clear();
    }
}, executor).orTimeout(2, TimeUnit.SECONDS);
Production issues / pitfalls
  • Unbounded thread pool → OutOfMemoryError

    Executors.newCachedThreadPool() creates unbounded threads (queue = SynchronousQueue). A traffic burst → thousands of threads → OOM "unable to create new native thread". Always bound it: max pool size + bounded queue + rejection policy.

  • CompletableFuture with no exception handling → silent failure

    An exception in supplyAsync is not thrown out until you call .get()/.join(). Forgetting to chain exceptionally → the future hangs or the error is not logged. Log the exception in .handle() to know a task failed.

    future.handle((result, ex) -> {
    if (ex != null) {
        Log.errorf(ex, "Task failed");
        return defaultValue;
    }
    return result;
});
  • Parallel DB tasks exceeding the connection pool

    Especially in a Kafka consumer batch process: poll 500 messages, each one supplyAsync(() -> repo.save(...)) → 500 tasks waiting for 10 connections. Tail latency spikes, sometimes connection acquisition timeout.

  • No timeout on an external call → cascading failure

    Default RestTemplate / OkHttp has no timeout. When the downstream is slow, the thread pool fills up, the app looks "hung" and the HealthCheck still passes (it only pings HTTP). Set a connect timeout + read timeout for every external client, plus a circuit breaker (Resilience4j).

  • Synchronized too broad → low throughput

    synchronized on a 100-line method (including an HTTP call) → other threads wait on HTTP. The lock scope should be as small as possible, only wrapping the critical section of shared state.

    // Bad
public synchronized void process() {
    var data = externalClient.fetch();   // 500ms blocking
    cache.put(key, data);
}

// Good
public void process() {
    var data = externalClient.fetch();
    synchronized (cacheLock) { cache.put(key, data); }
}
Example
ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(10);

CompletableFuture<User> userFuture =
    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> userClient.getUser(userId), executor);

CompletableFuture<List<Order>> orderFuture =
    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> orderClient.getOrders(userId), executor);

CompletableFuture<CreditScore> scoreFuture =
    CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> creditClient.getScore(userId), executor);

CustomerView view = CompletableFuture
    .allOf(userFuture, orderFuture, scoreFuture)
    .thenApply(v -> new CustomerView(
        userFuture.join(),
        orderFuture.join(),
        scoreFuture.join()
    ))
    .orTimeout(2, TimeUnit.SECONDS)
    .exceptionally(ex -> {
        // log with correlation id
        throw new RuntimeException("Cannot build customer view", ex);
    })
    .join();
Interview answer tip: When talking about CompletableFuture, emphasize: 'I will bound the executor, set a timeout, measure the connection pool, and not parallelize tasks that depend on each other.'
Key focus
Java Memory Model, volatile, synchronized
Concept

The Java Memory Model (JMM) is a set of rules that determine when a change made by one thread becomes visible to another thread. volatile guarantees visibility; synchronized guarantees both mutual exclusion and visibility.

Why it exists: Each thread may cache a variable in a CPU register/cache; without rules, another thread could keep reading a stale value forever. The JMM defines the happens-before relationship so multithreaded code is correct.

How it works
  1. Visibility: writing a volatile variable → every thread that reads afterward sees the latest value.
  2. Atomicity: a multi-step operation like count++ (read-add-write) is NOT atomic even if volatile.
  3. synchronized: only one thread enters the critical section, and on exit it flushes changes for the next thread.
  4. Need an atomic counter → AtomicInteger (CAS); need complex exclusion → lock/synchronized.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Wrong counter even with volatile: count++ is not atomic → use AtomicInteger/LongAdder.
  2. Stop flag not seen by the thread: boolean running not volatile → the thread loops forever without seeing false. Mark it volatile.
  3. Double-checked locking missing volatile: a thread receives a half-initialized instance → declare volatile instance.
  4. check-then-act race: if(map.get==null) map.put → two threads both put. Use computeIfAbsent/putIfAbsent.

A strong engineer needs to understand visibility and atomicity. volatile does not replace a lock for a compound operation such as count++.

What to understand deeply
  • Visibility: volatile flushes cache → main memory

    Each CPU core has its own cache. Without volatile, thread B may read a stale value of a field that thread A just updated because the value is still in thread A's cache. volatile creates a memory barrier: a write goes straight to main memory, a read always reads from main memory.

    // Classic use case: a thread stop flag
class Worker implements Runnable {
    private volatile boolean running = true;
    public void stop() { running = false; }
    public void run() {
        while (running) { /* work */ }  // without volatile, it may loop forever
    }
}
  • volatile is NOT atomic for a compound operation

    count++ = 3 steps: read, add 1, write. Two threads can both read = 5, both write = 6 → lose 1 increment. volatile only guarantees each step sees the latest value, it does not prevent interleaving between the steps.

  • synchronized = mutex + visibility (both)

    A synchronized block: acquire lock (memory barrier in), execute, release lock (memory barrier out). Guarantees both mutual exclusion and the happens-before relationship. Higher cost than volatile because of lock contention.

  • AtomicInteger / Lock — choose the right tool

    AtomicInteger uses CAS (Compare-And-Swap), no lock — fast when contention is low. ReentrantLock has tryLock(timeout), fair lock, Condition — suitable when you need advanced features. LongAdder is better than AtomicLong when many threads increment together (high contention).

    // CAS-based, lock-free
AtomicInteger counter = new AtomicInteger();
counter.incrementAndGet();

// Lock with timeout — avoid deadlock
if (lock.tryLock(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
    try { /* critical */ } finally { lock.unlock(); }
} else {
    throw new BusyException();
}
Production issues / pitfalls
  • Thinking volatile = thread-safe

    volatile only fixes visibility. Every compound action (check-then-act, read-modify-write) still races. Example: if (instance == null) instance = new X(); with a volatile field is still not thread-safe — you need double-checked locking done correctly.

  • A synchronized method covering I/O too

    Seeing a method with a concurrency problem → public synchronized void doStuff(). The method calls HTTP/DB → other threads wait on the lock until the I/O is done. The lock scope must be as small as possible, only wrapping the truly shared mutable state.

  • Shared mutable state — the root cause of 90% of concurrency bugs

    A field private Map<String, Object> cache in a @Service singleton, not synchronized. Many threads put/get at the same time → corrupted structure. Better: use ConcurrentHashMap, or immutable + replace the whole thing with an AtomicReference.

    // Bad
class CacheService {
    private Map<String,User> cache = new HashMap<>();
    public User get(String k) { return cache.computeIfAbsent(k, this::load); }
}

// Good
class CacheService {
    private final ConcurrentMap<String,User> cache = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    public User get(String k) { return cache.computeIfAbsent(k, this::load); }
}
  • Race condition that does not show up locally (1 thread, low load)

    Unit test passes, manual test passes — production crashes because of 100 RPS. A race condition needs a concurrent stress test. Use JCStress, or create a test with CountDownLatch + 1000 threads calling the method at the same time.

Example
class Counter {
    private volatile int count = 0;

    // BAD: volatile does not make count++ atomic
    public void increment() {
        count++;
    }
}

class SafeCounter {
    private final AtomicInteger count = new AtomicInteger();

    public void increment() {
        count.incrementAndGet();
    }
}
Interview answer tip: A good answer: 'volatile solves visibility, not atomicity. For count++ I use AtomicInteger or a lock depending on the logic.'
Deep dive — Java Core
Architecture flow: parallel API aggregator
Request: GET /customers/{id}/dashboard
                       │
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │  Controller (HTTP)   │
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │ Application Service  │  ← capture MDC (traceId)
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       │  supplyAsync(executor)
        ┌──────────────┼──────────────┐
        ▼              ▼              ▼
  ┌───────────┐  ┌───────────┐  ┌───────────┐
  │ User API  │  │ Order API │  │ Score API │   ExecutorService
  │ 100ms     │  │ 150ms     │  │ 200ms     │   (10 threads, bounded queue 200,
  │ timeout 2s│  │ timeout 2s│  │ timeout 2s│    CallerRunsPolicy = backpressure)
  └─────┬─────┘  └─────┬─────┘  └─────┬─────┘
        └──────────────┼──────────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │  allOf().thenApply   │  ≈ max(100,150,200) = 200ms
            │  exceptionally()     │  fallback per future
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
                 200 OK (DTO)

Failure modes:
  • timeout → exceptionally() returns partial DTO
  • executor full → caller thread runs task (backpressure, not OOM)
  • downstream 5xx → circuit breaker opens, future fails fast
  • MDC lost → traceId missing in worker thread logs (must propagate)
War story — the 3 AM page

Service: customer-dashboard-api, ~800 RPS peak, 3 downstream calls per request.

Symptom: 02:47 — p99 latency jumped 200ms → 12s. Error rate normal. CPU 35%. No deploy in 24h.

Investigation: Hikari pool metric awaitingConnection spiked to 40+. But the dashboard endpoint doesn't touch DB directly. Heap dump showed 3,200 threads named pool-2-thread-* — someone had used Executors.newCachedThreadPool() for a "lightweight" SDK callback.

Root cause: Downstream score-api timed out (their incident, not ours). Default OkHttp client had no read timeout. Cached thread pool kept spawning threads to replace blocked ones — each thread acquired a Hikari connection via an unrelated @Async audit method. Pool exhausted → unrelated endpoints (which DO need DB) hung in queue.

Fix (immediate): Set OkHttp readTimeout(2s); replace cached pool with ThreadPoolExecutor(10, 50, queue=200, CallerRunsPolicy); add circuit breaker (Resilience4j) on score-api client.

Fix (structural): Per-dependency executor + bulkhead pattern. score-api failures can no longer starve order-api or user-api executors.

Lesson: Cached thread pool + no timeouts = "memory leak with extra steps." The bug was 6 months old; only manifested when a downstream broke.

Production-grade aggregator (CompletableFuture + bulkhead + MDC + timeout)
@Service
public class CustomerDashboardService {

    private final UserClient userClient;
    private final OrderClient orderClient;
    private final ScoreClient scoreClient;
    private final ExecutorService userExec;
    private final ExecutorService orderExec;
    private final ExecutorService scoreExec;

    public CustomerDashboardService(
            UserClient userClient,
            OrderClient orderClient,
            ScoreClient scoreClient,
            @Qualifier("userExecutor")  ExecutorService userExec,
            @Qualifier("orderExecutor") ExecutorService orderExec,
            @Qualifier("scoreExecutor") ExecutorService scoreExec) {
        this.userClient  = userClient;
        this.orderClient = orderClient;
        this.scoreClient = scoreClient;
        this.userExec    = userExec;
        this.orderExec   = orderExec;
        this.scoreExec   = scoreExec;
    }

    public CustomerDashboard load(CustomerId id) {
        Map<String, String> mdc = MDC.getCopyOfContextMap();

        CompletableFuture<User> uF = supply(userExec, mdc, () -> userClient.find(id))
                .orTimeout(800, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
                .exceptionally(ex -> { Log.warnf(ex, "user fallback"); return User.unknown(id); });

        CompletableFuture<List<Order>> oF = supply(orderExec, mdc, () -> orderClient.recent(id, 20))
                .orTimeout(1500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
                .exceptionally(ex -> { Log.warnf(ex, "orders fallback"); return List.of(); });

        CompletableFuture<Score> sF = supply(scoreExec, mdc, () -> scoreClient.fetch(id))
                .orTimeout(2000, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
                .exceptionally(ex -> { Log.warnf(ex, "score fallback"); return Score.unscored(); });

        return CompletableFuture.allOf(uF, oF, sF)
                .thenApply(v -> new CustomerDashboard(uF.join(), oF.join(), sF.join()))
                .join();
    }

    private static <T> CompletableFuture<T> supply(
            ExecutorService exec, Map<String, String> mdc, Supplier<T> task) {
        return CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> {
            if (mdc != null) MDC.setContextMap(mdc);
            try { return task.get(); }
            finally { MDC.clear(); }
        }, exec);
    }
}

// @Configuration — bounded, named, monitored
@Bean("userExecutor") ExecutorService userExecutor(MeterRegistry m) {
    return monitored("user", 8, 40, 200, m);
}
@Bean("orderExecutor") ExecutorService orderExecutor(MeterRegistry m) {
    return monitored("order", 12, 60, 300, m);
}
@Bean("scoreExecutor") ExecutorService scoreExecutor(MeterRegistry m) {
    return monitored("score", 4, 20, 100, m);
}
private ExecutorService monitored(String name, int core, int max, int qsize, MeterRegistry m) {
    ThreadPoolExecutor tp = new ThreadPoolExecutor(
            core, max, 60L, TimeUnit.SECONDS,
            new ArrayBlockingQueue<>(qsize),
            new ThreadFactoryBuilder().setNameFormat(name + "-%d").build(),
            new ThreadPoolExecutor.CallerRunsPolicy());
    return ExecutorServiceMetrics.monitor(m, tp, name);
}
Interview scenario — "Tell me about a concurrency bug you fixed"

Tell me about a concurrency bug you debugged in production.

A customer dashboard endpoint started showing p99 12s spikes. CPU was fine, so my first instinct wasn't "too much work" — it was "blocked threads." I checked Hikari metrics and saw awaitingConnection climbing. That was weird because the endpoint didn't touch the DB.

How did you find the root cause?

Heap dump. I saw ~3,200 threads from a cached pool that someone used for SDK callbacks. Downstream score-api was timing out, OkHttp had no read timeout, so threads blocked indefinitely. The cached pool spawned replacements without bound. Each callback also fired an @Async audit method that did grab a DB connection — and that's how an unrelated upstream dependency starved the DB pool.

Why didn't the JVM OOM?

It was approaching it — we saw unable to create new native thread in logs minutes before mitigation. We restarted before crash because the pod's liveness probe never failed: the HTTP server still accepted connections, just queued them. That's a separate lesson: liveness probes shouldn't just check "process alive."

What's the structural fix, not just the immediate one?

Three things. One, bulkhead pattern: per-dependency executor so score-api failures can't starve user-api or order-api capacity. Two, every external client gets connect + read timeouts and a Resilience4j circuit breaker — fail fast is non-negotiable. Three, we banned Executors.newCachedThreadPool() in our ArchUnit tests; only ThreadPoolExecutor with bounded queue + named ThreadFactory + rejection policy is allowed.

How do you size those executors?

I start from Little's Law: concurrency = arrival rate × latency. For score-api at 300 RPS with p99 200ms, I need ~60 in-flight. I size max-pool to about 2× that (120) for headroom but cap the queue smaller than that so we shed load before we blow heap. Then I instrument the pool with Micrometer and watch queue depth, active threads, and rejected count on Grafana — sizing is iterative, not a one-shot calc.

2. Spring Boot / Spring Framework

A strong engineer needs to understand that Spring works via proxy, bean lifecycle, auto configuration, transaction boundary and request flow.

#2
Key focus
IoC, DI and Bean Lifecycle
Concept

IoC (Inversion of Control) means an object does not create its own dependencies but delegates creation + wiring to the container. DI (Dependency Injection) is how the container "injects" dependencies in. A Bean is an object managed by Spring.

Why it exists: To reduce coupling: a class only declares "I need this", without caring who creates it. This makes it easy to swap implementations and easy to mock in tests.

How it works
  1. Spring scans @Component/@Service/@Configuration → creates bean definitions.
  2. The container instantiates beans in dependency order, injects dependencies (preferring constructor injection).
  3. Runs lifecycle callbacks: @PostConstruct after injection completes, @PreDestroy before destruction.
  4. Beans are singleton by default (1 instance/container) → must be thread-safe because they are shared.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Field injection: hard to test, hides circular dependencies, not final → switch to constructor injection.
  2. Circular dependency A↔B: the app does not start. Split responsibilities, use events, or @Lazy as a temporary workaround.
  3. Injecting a prototype into a singleton: you only get a single instance at the time the singleton is created → use ObjectProvider/Provider.
  4. State in a singleton bean: storing request data in a field of a singleton → races between requests. The bean must be stateless.
  5. Using a bean in the constructor before injection completes: move initialization logic to @PostConstruct.

It is not just about knowing @Autowired. A strong engineer needs to understand when an object is created, when it is injected, when a proxy is created, and why circular dependency or wrong scope causes bugs.

What to understand deeply
  • The bean lifecycle consists of specific stages

    Order: Instantiate → Populate properties (DI) → BeanNameAware/BeanFactoryAware → BeanPostProcessor.postProcessBeforeInitialization → @PostConstruct → afterPropertiesSet() → BeanPostProcessor.postProcessAfterInitialization (this is where the proxy is wrapped) → ready. Understanding this order helps debug "why does the bean init run before/after X".

    @Component
class MyBean {
    @PostConstruct
    void init() {
        // At this point all dependencies have been injected
        // But the PROXY has NOT been created yet - do not call this.transactionalMethod() here!
    }
}
  • Constructor injection — the standard code

    Final fields → immutable. Tests do not need Spring (just new OrderService(mockRepo, mockClient)). Circular dependencies are detected at startup rather than at runtime. Field injection hides dependencies, makes a bean hard to test, and violates immutability.

    // Recommended style
@Service
class OrderService {
    private final OrderRepository repo;
    private final PaymentClient client;
    // Spring 4.3+: drop @Autowired entirely if there is a single constructor
    OrderService(OrderRepository repo, PaymentClient client) {
        this.repo = repo; this.client = client;
    }
}
  • BeanPostProcessor — the source of Spring "magic"

    @Transactional, @Async, @Cacheable, @Validated are all implemented via AbstractAutoProxyCreator (a BeanPostProcessor). After the bean finishes init, it wraps the bean in a CGLIB/JDK proxy. All method calls from outside go through the proxy → interceptor chain → the real method.

  • Singleton ≠ Thread-safe

    Spring only guarantees there is one instance in the container. If the bean has mutable fields (Map, counter, state...) then N threads accessing it concurrently will race. Recommendation: keep beans stateless, or use concurrent collections for state.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Field injection — hidden dependency, hard to test

    @Autowired private FooService foo; → without bootstrapping the Spring context, foo = null. A class with 8 field injections looks compact but actually depends on 8 things — it violates Single Responsibility (a good reason to split it).

  • Singleton mutable state per request

    A field private String currentUserId; set inside handle() → thread 2 overwrites thread 1 before thread 1 is done using it. Store per-request state using method parameters, ThreadLocal (be careful with async), or a @RequestScope bean.

  • Circular dependency: A → B → A

    Spring Boot 2.6+ disables circular references by default, the app fails at startup. Hint: if A and B call each other, the design is wrong — usually extract the shared logic into a class C that both A and B inject. Temporary workaround: @Lazy or setter injection.

  • Self-invocation bypasses the proxy

    this.transactionalMethod() is a direct call, it does not go through the proxy → @Transactional/@Async/@Cacheable do not run. Fix: move the method to another bean, or inject the self bean (@Autowired private MyBean self;), or use AopContext.currentProxy() (requires exposeProxy = true).

    // Bad - @Transactional does not work
@Service
class Service {
    public void parent() {
        this.child(); // direct call, not through the proxy
    }
    @Transactional public void child() { ... }
}

// Good - split the class or inject self
@Service
class Service {
    @Autowired @Lazy private Service self;
    public void parent() { self.child(); } // through the proxy
    @Transactional public void child() { ... }
}
Example
@Service
class OrderService {
    private final PaymentClient paymentClient;
    private final OrderRepository orderRepository;

    OrderService(PaymentClient paymentClient, OrderRepository orderRepository) {
        this.paymentClient = paymentClient;
        this.orderRepository = orderRepository;
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Add: 'The bean lifecycle matters because annotations like @Transactional, @Async typically rely on a proxy created by a post processor.'
Key focus
@Transactional and Proxy
Concept

@Transactional wraps a method in a database transaction: either commit everything, or roll back everything. Spring does this by creating a proxy around your bean.

Why it exists: To ensure data consistency (atomicity), while separating business logic from the code that opens/closes the transaction.

How it works
  1. Spring creates a proxy wrapping the bean; every call from outside goes through the proxy first.
  2. The proxy opens the transaction → calls the real method → commits if OK, rolls back if there is a RuntimeException.
  3. Because it goes through the proxy: calling a @Transactional method from within the same bean (self-invocation) will NOT activate the transaction.
  4. By default it only rolls back on RuntimeException/Error, it does NOT roll back checked exceptions (unless you declare rollbackFor).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Self-invocation: calling a @Transactional method from another method in the same class → not through the proxy → NO transaction. Move it to another bean.
  2. private/final method: the proxy cannot wrap it → the annotation has no effect.
  3. Checked exception does not roll back: by default it only rolls back RuntimeException → declare rollbackFor = Exception.class.
  4. Calling HTTP/Kafka inside a transaction: holding a DB connection throughout the I/O call → exhausts the pool. Move I/O outside the TX.
  5. Transaction too broad: wrapping an entire long method → long locks, prone to deadlock. Narrow the boundary.
  6. Read-only query: add @Transactional(readOnly = true) to optimize and avoid dirty checking.

This is a question engineers very often face. You must understand transaction open/commit/rollback at the proxy boundary, not magic inside the method.

What to understand deeply
  • @Transactional works at the proxy boundary

    Spring wraps the bean in a CGLIB (class proxy) or JDK proxy (interface). Each method call from outside goes through the TransactionInterceptor: open connection, begin tx → invoke method → commit or rollback. There is no magic in the method's byte code — the magic is in the proxy.

    // Pseudo-code of the proxy
public Object invoke(Method m, Object[] args) {
    TransactionStatus tx = txManager.getTransaction(...);
    try {
        Object result = target.invoke(m, args);
        txManager.commit(tx);
        return result;
    } catch (RuntimeException e) {
        txManager.rollback(tx);
        throw e;
    }
}
  • Only external calls go through the proxy interceptor

    The controller calls service.x() → goes through the proxy. But inside the service, this.y() is a direct call on the target object, not through the proxy → no transaction. This is pitfall #1 of @Transactional.

  • Default rollback rule

    Spring rolls back: RuntimeException, Error. It does NOT roll back: checked Exception. Because a checked Exception (IOException, SQLException) is treated as an "expected business condition". To roll back on checked → @Transactional(rollbackFor = Exception.class).

  • Propagation: REQUIRED, REQUIRES_NEW, NESTED

    REQUIRED (default): join the outer transaction if one exists, or create a new one. REQUIRES_NEW: pause the outer one, open a new connection, commit/rollback independently — used for audit logs that should not roll back along with it. NESTED: use a savepoint within the outer tx.

    @Transactional public void parent() {
    save(...);
    auditLog(); // want the audit even if parent rolls back
}

@Transactional(propagation = Propagation.REQUIRES_NEW)
public void auditLog() { ... } // commits independently
  • Place the transaction boundary at the application service / use case

    Do not put @Transactional on the repository (too narrow) or the controller (too broad, covering serialization too). Put it on the use case method — representing a single business "unit of work".

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Self-invocation pitfall

    this.updateSomething() bypasses the proxy → @Transactional does not run. The code runs "without errors" but the data update is not within a transaction → when an exception occurs midway, there is no rollback. Symptom: data corruption with no clear cause.

  • Publishing to Kafka inside @Transactional — inconsistent

    Order Tx: save order → kafkaTemplate.send → commit. If the commit fails after the send → the consumer receives an "OrderCreated" event but the DB has no order. Fix: use the Outbox pattern (store the event in an outbox table within the same transaction, an async publisher sends it).

    // Bad
@Transactional
public void place(Order o) {
    repo.save(o);
    kafka.send("order-created", event); // commit has not run yet
    // If the commit fails, the event has already gone out
}

// Good - Outbox
@Transactional
public void place(Order o) {
    repo.save(o);
    outboxRepo.save(new OutboxEvent("OrderCreated", payload));
}
// The publisher polls the outbox, sends to Kafka, marks sent
  • Long transaction containing an external API call

    @Transactional wrapping a 3-second REST call → holds a DB connection for 3 seconds → pool exhausted under high load. Split it: external call outside the transaction, the transaction only contains local DB work.

  • Not understanding propagation REQUIRED vs REQUIRES_NEW

    REQUIRES_NEW opens a new connection — if the pool is small, it can deadlock against itself (the outer holds 1 connection, the inner waits for a second connection). Test thoroughly before using REQUIRES_NEW in production code.

Example
@Service
class OrderApplicationService {
    private final OrderRepository orderRepository;

    @Transactional
    public void placeOrder(PlaceOrderCommand command) {
        Order order = Order.create(command);
        orderRepository.save(order);
        // publish event? be careful: if publish before commit, consumer may see inconsistent state
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Strong answer: '@Transactional should wrap a business unit-of-work. I avoid calling external APIs inside a long transaction and use an outbox if I need to publish events after commit.'
Key focus
Spring Boot AutoConfiguration
Concept

AutoConfiguration is the Spring Boot mechanism that automatically configures beans based on what is on the classpath, following the "convention over configuration" principle.

Why it exists: To avoid writing hundreds of lines of manual config: whatever library it sees on the classpath, it guesses a reasonable configuration and applies it, while still letting you override.

How it works
  1. @SpringBootApplication enables @EnableAutoConfiguration.
  2. Boot reads the auto-config list (the ...AutoConfiguration.imports file).
  3. Each config has @Conditional (e.g. @ConditionalOnClass, @ConditionalOnMissingBean) → only applied when the condition holds.
  4. Beans you define yourself win → you can override the defaults.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. NoUniqueBeanDefinitionException: two auto-configs create the same type → @Primary/@Qualifier.
  2. Need to override a default: define your own ObjectMapper/DataSource → Boot's @ConditionalOnMissingBean backs off automatically.
  3. Not understanding why a bean exists: run with --debug → read the Condition Evaluation Report.
  4. Slow startup / requires a DB you do not need: @SpringBootApplication(exclude = ...) removes redundant auto-config.
  5. Mismatched starter versions: use a BOM / parent to manage versions consistently.

A strong engineer should be able to explain why just adding a dependency gives the app default beans/config.

What to understand deeply
  • AutoConfiguration: conditional bean creation

    Spring Boot scans the file META-INF/spring/org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.AutoConfiguration.imports (3.x) or spring.factories (2.x), loads the @AutoConfiguration classes, each with conditions to enable/disable it. Add the dependency spring-boot-starter-data-jpa → JPA classes are on the classpath → DataSourceAutoConfiguration activates.

  • Common conditions

    @ConditionalOnClass: the bean is created if the class is on the classpath. @ConditionalOnMissingBean: only created if the user has not declared one. @ConditionalOnProperty: enable/disable by property. @ConditionalOnBean: requires another bean to exist first.

    @AutoConfiguration
@ConditionalOnClass(DataSource.class)
class DataSourceAutoConfiguration {
    @Bean
    @ConditionalOnMissingBean   // if the user declares a DataSource, this bean is skipped
    @ConditionalOnProperty("spring.datasource.url")
    DataSource dataSource(DataSourceProperties p) { ... }
}
  • Override the default bean

    Declare your own @Bean with the same type/name → the default's @ConditionalOnMissingBean is skipped. The type must match exactly. If you declare a mismatched type, both beans coexist → ambiguous, the app fails at startup.

    @Bean
ObjectMapper objectMapper() {  // override Spring Boot's default ObjectMapper
    return new ObjectMapper()
        .registerModule(new JavaTimeModule())
        .disable(SerializationFeature.WRITE_DATES_AS_TIMESTAMPS);
}
  • Debug with the condition report

    Run the app with --debug or enable the actuator endpoint /actuator/conditions. The output lists: which beans had a positive match (created), and a negative match (skipped because a condition failed). When a bean is strangely missing in production, this is the first tool to use.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Not knowing where a bean comes from

    Maven transitively pulls in 10 starters, each starter creates many beans. When you need to customize, you do not know which bean is active. Use /actuator/beans or IntelliJ's "Endpoints" view to inspect.

  • Overriding with the wrong type → ambiguous bean

    You intend to override ObjectMapper but declare a bean named "myMapper" of type ObjectMapper. Spring has 2 beans of the same type → the controller does not know which one to use when deserializing, fails at startup or uses the wrong one.

  • Overusing starters

    Adding spring-boot-starter-actuator just because you want /health → it pulls in metrics, tracing, audit, etc. Bigger image, larger attack surface. Pick dependencies deliberately.

  • Not reading the condition report when debugging

    A needed bean is "nowhere to be found" → you guess randomly and change code at random. The condition report tells you exactly why the bean is not created (missing class, missing property, missing bean dep).

Example
@Configuration
class MyConfig {
    @Bean
    @ConditionalOnMissingBean
    MyClient myClient() {
        return new MyClient();
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Spring Boot reduces boilerplate with conditional auto configuration. On a production issue, I check the condition report to see which beans were or were not created.'
Key focus
Spring MVC Request Flow
Concept

Spring MVC processes an HTTP request using the front-controller model: a central servlet (DispatcherServlet) receives every request and dispatches it to the right controller.

Why it exists: To clearly separate receiving the request, mapping it to handling code, and producing the response — each part its own component, easy to extend (interceptor, converter, exception handler).

How it works
  1. Request → DispatcherServlet.
  2. HandlerMapping finds the controller/method matching the URL + HTTP method.
  3. HandlerAdapter calls the method; argument resolvers bind @RequestBody/@RequestParam; validation runs.
  4. The returned object → HttpMessageConverter (Jackson) serializes to JSON. Exceptions go through @ControllerAdvice.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Messy error contract: each controller has different try/catch → centralize on @RestControllerAdvice + @ExceptionHandler.
  2. Validation: @Valid throws MethodArgumentNotValidException → map to 400 with a list of field errors.
  3. Wrong JSON serialization (LocalDateTime, BigDecimal, null): configure the ObjectMapper (JavaTimeModule, NON_NULL, do not write timestamps).
  4. Choosing the wrong cross-cutting layer: auth/logging/rate-limit should be at the Filter/Interceptor, not stuffed into the controller.
  5. Slow request hogging servlet threads: use async/streaming or set sensible timeouts to avoid exhausting the thread pool.

Understanding the request going through filter, security, dispatcher servlet, interceptor, controller, exception handler helps debug production bugs quickly.

What to understand deeply
  • Servlet container → Filter chain

    The HTTP request reaches Tomcat/Undertow first, goes through the filter chain: CharacterEncodingFilter, CorsFilter, request logging filter, etc. Filters run outside the Spring MVC context, and can short-circuit (a CORS preflight does not reach the controller).

  • Spring Security is its own filter chain

    The SecurityFilterChain wraps many filters: SecurityContextPersistenceFilter, UsernamePasswordAuthenticationFilter, BearerTokenAuthenticationFilter, AuthorizationFilter. 401/403 are usually returned from here, before the controller is invoked.

    // Debug order on a 401:
// 1. Does the request have an Authorization header?
// 2. Is the token format correct (JWT decodable)?
// 3. Does JwtDecoder validate the signature OK?
// 4. Does the SecurityContextHolder have an Authentication?
// 5. Does @PreAuthorize match the role?
  • DispatcherServlet routing

    After the filters, the DispatcherServlet: (1) HandlerMapping matches the URL → HandlerMethod; (2) HandlerInterceptor.preHandle(); (3) HandlerAdapter invokes the controller method with argument resolvers; (4) postHandle; (5) the View resolver / message converter renders the response.

  • Validation with @Valid

    @Valid on a @RequestBody parameter → Spring triggers the javax.validation.Validator before the method runs. On failure → MethodArgumentNotValidException → by default returns 400 with a BindingResult. Customize via @ControllerAdvice.

  • Exception handling chain

    Exception thrown → HandlerExceptionResolver chain. If there is an @ExceptionHandler in @ControllerAdvice → match → handle. If no match, by default DefaultHandlerExceptionResolver returns 500. The whitelabel error page is just the final fallback.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Filter vs Interceptor — not distinguishing them

    A Filter runs outside Spring (web layer), sees the raw request/response, and can block before reaching Spring. An Interceptor runs inside Spring, has access to the HandlerMethod (controller method object) so it knows which endpoint will run. Logging the request body needs a Filter (reading the stream); inspecting the method annotation needs an Interceptor.

  • Business exception → 500

    Throwing RuntimeException("Email already exists") in the service → Spring returns 500. The client cannot tell whether this is a validation or a system error. Create a meaningful exception hierarchy (ConflictException → 409, NotFoundException → 404) and map it in @ControllerAdvice.

    @RestControllerAdvice
class GlobalExceptionHandler {
    @ExceptionHandler(BusinessException.class)
    ResponseEntity<ApiError> handle(BusinessException ex) {
        return ResponseEntity.status(ex.status())
            .body(new ApiError(ex.code(), ex.getMessage(), MDC.get("traceId")));
    }
}
  • Not logging a correlation id

    A request goes through 5 microservices, each service logs with its own requestId → you cannot trace it. A Filter generates or forwards X-Request-Id, sets it into the MDC, the log appender includes the MDC. Forward the header on downstream calls.

  • Non-standard error response

    Endpoint A returns {error: "..."}, endpoint B returns {message: "..."}, endpoint C returns a plain string. The client has to parse each kind. Define one shared ApiError schema: {code, message, details, traceId, timestamp}.

Example
@RestControllerAdvice
class ApiExceptionHandler {
    @ExceptionHandler(BusinessException.class)
    ResponseEntity<ApiError> handle(BusinessException ex) {
        return ResponseEntity.status(HttpStatus.CONFLICT)
            .body(new ApiError("BUSINESS_ERROR", ex.getMessage()));
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Walk through the request flow to show you can debug real problems: auth failures, validation failures, controller not hit, wrong exception mapping.
Deep dive — Spring
Request flow + @Transactional proxy boundary
HTTP request
   │
   ▼
┌────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ Servlet container (Tomcat)                 │
│  ├─ CharacterEncodingFilter                │
│  ├─ CorsFilter                             │
│  └─ Spring Security FilterChain            │
│      ├─ SecurityContextPersistenceFilter   │
│      ├─ BearerTokenAuthenticationFilter    │  ← 401 here
│      └─ AuthorizationFilter                │  ← 403 here
└─────────────────┬──────────────────────────┘
                  ▼
┌────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ DispatcherServlet                          │
│  ├─ HandlerMapping (URL → HandlerMethod)   │
│  ├─ HandlerInterceptor.preHandle()         │
│  ├─ HandlerAdapter (arg resolver, @Valid)  │  ← 400 here
│  ▼                                         │
│  Controller method (proxy)                 │
└─────────────────┬──────────────────────────┘
                  ▼
        ┌──────── proxy boundary ────────┐
        │  TransactionInterceptor        │  ← BEGIN TX
        │   getConnection() from Hikari  │
        │   ▼                            │
        │  UseCase.execute()             │
        │   ├─ domainRepo.save()         │  flush at end of TX
        │   ├─ outboxRepo.save()         │  same TX → atomic
        │   └─ NO external HTTP/Kafka    │  ← keep TX short
        │   ▼                            │
        │  return → COMMIT  or ROLLBACK  │
        │            (only RuntimeEx)    │
        └────────────────────────────────┘
                  │
                  ▼
        OutboxPublisher (separate TX)
         poll → send Kafka → mark SENT

Pitfall: this.method() inside same class
   ─ direct call, bypasses proxy → no TX, no @Async, no @Cacheable
War story — "the orders that never paid"

Service: order-service publishes OrderCreated to Kafka so payment-service can charge the card.

Symptom: Customer support: "I was charged but my order isn't visible." Reverse case too: orders shown as CREATED that were never charged. Both ~0.1% of traffic — rare enough to be invisible on dashboards, common enough to be a daily ticket.

Investigation: Read the code:

@Transactional
public void place(PlaceCommand cmd) {
    Order o = Order.create(cmd);
    orderRepo.save(o);
    kafkaTemplate.send("order-created", o.toEvent());
    // commit happens AFTER this method returns
}

Two failure modes happened concurrently: (a) Kafka send succeeded but DB commit failed (FK constraint, timeout) → payment charged for a phantom order; (b) Kafka broker briefly rejected → exception → DB rolled back, but a few sends had landed → same as (a).

Root cause: dual-write problem. There is no XA between Postgres and Kafka. Send-before-commit and commit-before-send are both wrong in different ways.

Fix: Outbox pattern. Same transaction writes to orders and outbox_event. A separate publisher polls outbox (every 500ms), sends to Kafka, marks SENT. Consumer dedupes by event_id.

Lesson: "Looks atomic, isn't atomic." Any time you write to two systems in one method, ask: what if the second one fails after the first succeeds, or vice versa? If the answer is "data inconsistency," you need outbox, saga, or 2PC (don't use 2PC).

Production-grade use case with outbox + transaction boundary
// Application layer — owns the transaction boundary
@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class PlaceOrderUseCase {

    private final OrderRepository orderRepo;
    private final OutboxEventRepository outboxRepo;
    private final IdempotencyService idempotency;
    private final Clock clock;
    private final ObjectMapper json;

    // Note: NO @Transactional here — we want fine-grained control below
    public PlaceOrderResult execute(PlaceOrderCommand cmd, String idempotencyKey) {
        // 1) Idempotency check OUTSIDE the business TX (uses its own short TX).
        var cached = idempotency.findCompleted(idempotencyKey);
        if (cached.isPresent()) return cached.get();

        // 2) The business TX is short: only local DB writes. No external calls.
        var result = transactionalPlace(cmd, idempotencyKey);

        // 3) Outbox publisher (separate process / scheduled bean) ships the event.
        return result;
    }

    @Transactional
    protected PlaceOrderResult transactionalPlace(PlaceOrderCommand cmd, String key) {
        Order order = Order.create(cmd, clock.instant());
        orderRepo.save(order);

        OrderCreated event = OrderCreated.of(order);
        outboxRepo.save(OutboxEvent.builder()
                .id(UUID.randomUUID())
                .aggregateType("Order")
                .aggregateId(order.id().value())
                .eventType("OrderCreated")
                .payload(writeJson(event))
                .status(OutboxStatus.NEW)
                .createdAt(clock.instant())
                .build());

        var result = new PlaceOrderResult(order.id(), order.total());
        idempotency.markCompleted(key, hash(cmd), result);
        return result;
    }

    private String writeJson(Object o) {
        try { return json.writeValueAsString(o); }
        catch (JsonProcessingException e) { throw new IllegalStateException(e); }
    }
}

// Infrastructure — outbox publisher
@Component
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class OutboxPublisher {

    private final OutboxEventRepository outboxRepo;
    private final KafkaTemplate<String, String> kafka;

    @Scheduled(fixedDelay = 500)
    @SchedulerLock(name = "outboxPublisher", lockAtMostFor = "30s")  // ShedLock = 1 publisher per cluster
    public void publishBatch() {
        List<OutboxEvent> batch = outboxRepo.findBatchForPublishing(100);
        for (OutboxEvent ev : batch) {
            try {
                ProducerRecord<String, String> rec = new ProducerRecord<>(
                        topicFor(ev.getAggregateType()),
                        ev.getAggregateId(),                       // key → ordering per aggregate
                        ev.getPayload());
                rec.headers().add("event-id", ev.getId().toString().getBytes());
                rec.headers().add("event-type", ev.getEventType().getBytes());
                kafka.send(rec).get(5, TimeUnit.SECONDS);          // sync per record, but batched outside
                outboxRepo.markSent(ev.getId(), Instant.now());
            } catch (Exception e) {
                outboxRepo.markFailed(ev.getId(), e.getMessage());
                Log.errorf(e, "outbox publish failed id=%s type=%s",
                        ev.getId(), ev.getEventType());
            }
        }
    }
}
Interview scenario — "@Transactional isn't working"

A junior says @Transactional on their method "isn't rolling back." Walk me through how you debug this.

I ask three questions before touching code. One: is the method called from outside the bean or from another method in the same class? If it's this.method(), that's self-invocation — bypasses the proxy, no transaction. Two: what exception is being thrown — checked or unchecked? Spring rolls back on RuntimeException and Error by default, not on checked exceptions unless rollbackFor is set. Three: is the method public? CGLIB-proxied @Transactional on package-private or protected methods silently does nothing on older Spring versions.

Suppose it's self-invocation. What do you do?

First choice: move the transactional method to a different bean. That makes the proxy boundary explicit and the design clearer. If splitting is artificial, the second option is to inject the bean into itself with @Lazy — slightly ugly but works. AopContext.currentProxy() is the last resort and only with @EnableAspectJAutoProxy(exposeProxy = true) — it works but couples your domain code to AOP plumbing.

Where should the transaction boundary live in a hexagonal architecture?

On the application service / use case method — that's the "unit of work" boundary. Not on controllers because they handle serialization too, which would extend the transaction unnecessarily. Not on repositories because that scopes too narrowly — a use case usually writes multiple aggregates. The rule I follow: a single use case = single TX, never spans an external HTTP or Kafka call.

Why never an external call inside the TX?

Two reasons. Latency: Hikari is holding a connection for the duration of the TX. A 3-second downstream call at 50 RPS means 150 connections held continuously — pool exhausted. Correctness: if you publish to Kafka inside the TX, you have a dual-write problem. Send succeeds → commit fails → consumers see an event for data that doesn't exist. The fix is Outbox.

How do you make sure the Outbox publisher itself doesn't double-publish?

Two layers. First, in the publisher: use SELECT ... FOR UPDATE SKIP LOCKED or ShedLock so only one instance picks up a row. Second, accept that at-least-once is the contract — the publisher can crash after send-success but before markSent. So consumers must dedupe on event_id. There is no exactly-once across systems; pretending there is causes bugs.

3. REST API Design

A strong engineer does not just create CRUD endpoints. A strong engineer designs APIs that are stable, idempotent, versionable, observable and easy to integrate.

#3
Key focus
Resource design and HTTP semantics
Concept

REST is a style of designing APIs around resources (nouns, e.g. /orders/123) and using HTTP methods (verbs) to operate on them. HTTP semantics = the standard meaning of each method and status code.

Why it exists: So the API is predictable, cacheable and leverages the HTTP infrastructure correctly — clients/proxies understand that GET is safe, DELETE is idempotent... without needing to read the documentation.

How it works
  1. The URL is a plural noun: /orders, /orders/{id}/items — not /getOrder.
  2. GET reads (safe, idempotent), POST creates, PUT replaces (idempotent), PATCH updates part, DELETE removes (idempotent).
  3. Status codes with correct meaning: 200/201/204, 400/401/403/404/409/422, 500.
  4. Errors are returned following a consistent contract (e.g. RFC 7807 problem+json) so the client handles them uniformly.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Verb in the URL (/createOrder): change it to POST /orders.
  2. Always returning 200 even on error: the client cannot distinguish success/failure. Use status codes with correct meaning.
  3. Confusing PUT and PATCH: PUT replaces the whole thing (missing fields become null), PATCH updates part.
  4. Errors without a contract: each endpoint returns a different format → standardize on problem+json (code, message, traceId).
  5. Returning the JPA entity directly: exposes internal fields + LazyInitializationException → return a DTO.

A strong engineer needs to explain why they choose POST/PUT/PATCH, which status code is correct, and the response contract must be stable.

What to understand deeply
  • GET = safe + idempotent

    It does not change state on the server side. It can be cached (HTTP cache, CDN), can be prefetched, can be retried any number of times without issue. Consequence: never use GET to delete/update, even if it "looks convenient."

  • POST = create / non-idempotent action

    POST /orders creates a new order — calling it twice = two different orders. Suitable when the server decides the id. Return 201 + a Location header pointing to the new resource.

    POST /orders -> 201 Created
Location: /orders/O-12345
{"id": "O-12345", ...}
  • PUT = replace the entire resource, idempotent

    PUT /users/123 with a full body → the server replaces user 123. Calling it multiple times with the same body → the same result (idempotent). Do NOT use PUT for partial updates — that is PATCH.

  • PATCH = partial update

    There are 2 standards: JSON Merge Patch (RFC 7396, simple) and JSON Patch (RFC 6902, with add/remove/replace operations). Spring supports it via jackson-datatype-jsr353. Whether it is idempotent depends on the semantics.

    PATCH /users/123
Content-Type: application/merge-patch+json
{"email": "new@x.com"}   // only this field changes
  • Precise status codes

    201 Created + Location for create. 204 No Content for a successful DELETE. 400 validation format, 422 validation logic. 409 Conflict: duplicate/version conflict/invalid state. 429 rate limit. 503 + Retry-After during maintenance.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • POST for everything — RPC over HTTP

    POST /getUser, POST /deleteOrder, POST /listProducts. You lose cacheability, cannot test via a browser link, and violate HTTP semantics. Correct resources + verbs help the gateway/CDN/log analytics understand the API.

  • 200 for every response, with the error in the payload

    200 OK {"success": false, "code": "ERR_005"}. Consequences: monitoring (5xx rate) does not detect the problem, the nginx log cannot distinguish it, the retry logic of the client gateway is wrong. The HTTP status code is a first-class signal, use it correctly.

  • Unstable error codes

    A refactor changes "INVALID_INPUT" to "VALIDATION_FAILED" → old mobile builds break. The error code is part of the contract, do not rename it randomly. Document it in the API spec, deprecate it before removing.

  • No idempotency for create payment/order

    Mobile times out after 30s, retries → creates 2 orders, charges twice. Especially dangerous with payments. Add an Idempotency-Key header and store the result on the server side (see the Idempotency subtopic).

Example
POST /orders
Idempotency-Key: 7d7f...

{
  "customerId": "C001",
  "items": [
    { "sku": "A", "quantity": 2 }
  ]
}

// Response
201 Created
Location: /orders/O123
Interview answer tip: Speak in terms of the contract: 'A good API does not just run, it must have clear semantics, be backward compatible and have predictable error responses.'
Key focus
Idempotency
Concept

Idempotency means calling the same request multiple times produces the same result as calling it exactly once — without creating additional side effects (e.g. not creating 2 orders).

Why it exists: The network is unreliable: the client retries on its own when it times out. Without idempotency → a single tap of "Pay" with a network error could charge the money twice.

How it works
  1. The client sends an Idempotency-Key (UUID) in the header for a data-creating request (POST).
  2. The server checks whether the key has already been processed (stored in DB/Redis).
  3. New key → process, store the key + result. Duplicate key → return the stored result, do NOT process again.
  4. The key should have a TTL; handle races with a unique constraint / insert-if-not-exists.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Double-clicking "Place order": creates 2 orders. Require an Idempotency-Key for each create operation.
  2. Client retries after a timeout (the server already succeeded): creates a duplicate record → return the old result by key.
  3. Two requests with the same key running concurrently: a unique constraint on the key + insert-if-not-exists so only one wins.
  4. Webhook redelivery (Stripe, Kafka): dedup by event id, do not process again.
  5. Choosing the wrong method: PUT/DELETE are inherently idempotent; only POST needs a key; GET does not.

Idempotency is a advanced topic because it relates to retry, network timeout, duplicate request, payment/order creation.

What to understand deeply
  • Why idempotency is needed

    The network is not reliable: a client timeout does not mean the server failed. The server may have: (1) not received the request, (2) received it and be processing, (3) finished processing but the response did not reach the client. If you retry without idempotency → duplicate.

  • Idempotency key — schema

    The client generates a UUID, sends it via the Idempotency-Key header. The server stores (key, request_hash, status, response, expires_at) in DB or Redis. PRIMARY KEY = idempotency_key. A 24h TTL is usually enough for the retry use case.

    CREATE TABLE idempotency_record (
    idempotency_key VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    request_hash VARCHAR(128) NOT NULL,
    status VARCHAR(20) NOT NULL,  -- IN_PROGRESS, COMPLETED
    response_status INT,
    response_body TEXT,
    created_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL,
    expires_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL
);
CREATE INDEX idx_idempotency_expires ON idempotency_record(expires_at);
  • Same key + same payload = return the cached response. Same key + different payload = 409

    Hash the payload (SHA-256), compare with the stored hash. Match → return the old response. No match → the key was reused for a different payload → reject (409 Conflict). Protects against client bugs.

  • Concurrent with the same key — must lock

    2 requests with the same key at the same time: INSERT with a unique constraint → 1 succeeds, 1 fails. The failing request waits for the result of the successful request (poll the status or return 409 ASAP). Or use SELECT FOR UPDATE on the record row.

  • Place the idempotency check at the use case boundary, not in the controller

    The controller only passes the header down. The check logic must be close to the business — in the same transaction as the business write — to avoid a race between "check key" and "create order".

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Checking for duplicates using a business id generated afterwards

    orderNumber = generated in the service before saving. 2 requests at the same time → checking the DB finds no order → both create different orderNumbers → 2 records with the same customer/timestamp/amount. You must check before processing using the idempotency key the client sent, not the id the server generated.

  • Not storing the request hash

    Client bug: reusing the key for 2 different requests (the key is created only once per session). The server does not check the payload → request 2 returns request 1's response → the client reports "ordered successfully" but it is actually a completely different order.

  • Not locking concurrent same-key requests

    2 requests with the same key arrive at the same time: both read that there is no record yet → both INSERT → 1 fails the unique constraint. If the failure does not complete the retry logic → the response is inconsistent. Solution: a UNIQUE constraint + handle DuplicateKeyException → re-fetch + return the existing response.

    try {
    idempotencyRepo.insert(record);
    var result = process(request);
    idempotencyRepo.markComplete(key, result);
    return result;
} catch (DuplicateKeyException e) {
    var existing = idempotencyRepo.findByKey(key);
    if (existing.status == IN_PROGRESS) return 409;  // or poll
    if (!matches(requestHash, existing.requestHash)) return 409;
    return existing.response;
}
  • Retrying an external call inside a transaction

    The idempotency record + business write + external call (charge payment) all in 1 tx. The tx holds the lock for a long time because retrying the external call 3 times × 5s = 15s. Pool exhausted. Move the external call out of the tx, or use an outbox + async processor.

Example
CREATE TABLE idempotency_record (
    idempotency_key VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    request_hash VARCHAR(128) NOT NULL,
    status VARCHAR(30) NOT NULL,
    response_body TEXT,
    created_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL
);
Interview answer tip: State the flow clearly: receive key → hash payload → insert unique record → process → save response → retry with the same key returns the old response.
Key focus
Pagination, filtering, versioning
Concept

Pagination splits large data into pages; filtering selects by condition; versioning manages API changes without breaking old clients.

Why it exists: Returning a million records at once will crash both the server and the client. And APIs change over time — you must change them without breaking running apps.

How it works
  1. Offset ?page=2&size=20: simple but slow and skews the data when data changes.
  2. Cursor/keyset ?after=<lastId>: stable and fast for large datasets.
  3. Filtering via query params (?status=PAID&from=...); validate against a whitelist of fields to avoid injection.
  4. Versioning via URL (/v1/) or header; keep backward compatibility, deprecate on a schedule.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Large offset (page=5000): the DB has to skip millions of rows → very slow. Use cursor/keyset pagination.
  2. Counting the total is expensive on a large table: drop the total, return hasNext or use an estimated count.
  3. Filtering by an arbitrary field: risk of SQL injection / full scan → whitelist fields + index by common filters.
  4. Changing/removing a field causing a breaking change: versioning, deprecate on a schedule, do not remove a field abruptly.
  5. Non-deterministic sort (tied values): add a tie-breaker (id) so rows do not jump between pages.
// Keyset pagination: stable, fast, no skew when data changes
SELECT * FROM orders
WHERE created_at < :lastCreatedAt
   OR (created_at = :lastCreatedAt AND id < :lastId)  -- tie-breaker
ORDER BY created_at DESC, id DESC
LIMIT 20;
// Return cursor = (lastCreatedAt, lastId) for the client to call the next page

A strong engineer must know that offset pagination can be slow and wrong when data changes continuously.

What to understand deeply
  • Offset pagination — slow with a large OFFSET

    LIMIT 20 OFFSET 1000000 — the DB has to read + discard 1 million rows before returning 20 rows. P95 latency increases with the page number. Suitable when the user only browses the first few pages (admin UI).

  • Cursor/Keyset pagination — O(log n) constant

    Use the value of the last row as the cursor. Query WHERE (created_at, id) < (?, ?) ORDER BY created_at DESC, id DESC LIMIT 20. Uses an index → fast regardless of the page number. Limitation: cannot jump to an arbitrary page, only next/prev.

    GET /transactions?limit=50&afterId=982123&afterCreatedAt=2026-05-22T10:00:00Z

SELECT *
FROM transaction
WHERE (created_at, id) < (:afterCreatedAt, :afterId)
ORDER BY created_at DESC, id DESC
LIMIT 50;
  • Filter/sort whitelist

    Map public fields ("status", "createdAt") → DB fields ("status", "created_at"). Reject fields not in the whitelist → 400. Avoid users exposing an internal column or causing a slow query (sorting by a non-indexed column).

  • Versioning: backward compatibility is the priority

    Additive changes (adding an optional field, adding an endpoint) do not need a version. Breaking changes (changing/removing a field, changing the type, changing the semantics) need a v2. Implementation approaches: URI version (/v1/orders) or Accept header (application/vnd.app.v2+json). Mark fields deprecated early, log a warning, document the timeline.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Mapping the filter directly into SQL

    GET /users?sort=password → ORDER BY password leaks the order of the hashes. Or /orders?filter[status][in]=... dynamic mapping → SQL injection / NPE. Always whitelist fields, reject unexpected input.

  • Pagination missing a stable sort
