    ORDER BY created_at alone → 2 rows with the same timestamp end up in random positions across queries → page 1 and page 2 repeat/lose records. Always add a tiebreaker (id): ORDER BY created_at DESC, id DESC.

  • Implicit breaking change in the response

    Changing the amount field from number → string (for big decimal), the client crashes. Changing createdAt from epoch → ISO 8601, the client fails to parse. Always be additive: add the new field alongside, deprecate the old field with a clear timeline.

  • Not documenting the deprecation

    A field deprecated for 6 months is still being used, and no one knows. Set the HTTP header Deprecation: true + Sunset: <date>, log usage to know which clients still use it, contact them to migrate before removing.

Example
GET /transactions?limit=50&afterId=982123

// Keyset pagination idea:
SELECT *
FROM transaction
WHERE id > :afterId
ORDER BY id ASC
LIMIT :limit;
Interview answer tip: Say: 'For large data, I prioritize cursor pagination, stable ordering, a max page size limit and a whitelist for filter/sort.'
Deep dive — REST API
Idempotency flow (retry-safe POST)
Client                  API                       DB
  │                       │                         │
  │ POST /orders          │                         │
  │ Idempotency-Key: K1   │                         │
  ├──────────────────────►│                         │
  │                       │ INSERT idempotency_rec  │
  │                       │  (K1, hash, IN_PROGRESS)├────►  unique PK
  │                       │◄────────────────────────┤      OK
  │                       │                         │
  │                       │ BEGIN TX                │
  │                       │   INSERT orders         ├────►
  │                       │   INSERT outbox_event   ├────►
  │                       │ COMMIT                  │
  │                       │                         │
  │                       │ UPDATE idempotency_rec  │
  │                       │  (K1, COMPLETED, body)  ├────►
  │ 201 Created           │                         │
  │◄──────────────────────┤                         │
  │                                                 │
  │ ─── network blip ───                            │
  │                                                 │
  │ POST /orders          │                         │
  │ Idempotency-Key: K1   │  (retry, same body)     │
  ├──────────────────────►│                         │
  │                       │ INSERT idempotency_rec  │
  │                       │  K1 → DuplicateKey      │
  │                       │ SELECT existing         ├────►
  │                       │  status=COMPLETED       │
  │                       │  hash matches ✓         │
  │ 201 Created (cached)  │                         │
  │◄──────────────────────┤                         │

Edge cases:
  • Same K1 + different body  → 409 Conflict (don't return cached!)
  • Same K1 + IN_PROGRESS     → 409 or poll (concurrent retry)
  • TTL expired (≥24h)        → treat as new key
War story — "the double-billed Black Friday"

Service: payment-api, accepts POST /payments from the mobile app.

Symptom: ~120 customers double-charged on a Friday sale. Total disputed amount ~$18k. The mobile team swore "we only call it once."

Investigation: Pulled access logs for one duplicate case. Two POSTs, 31 seconds apart, identical body, identical x-request-id from the mobile client. Server returned 201 both times. The mobile retry logic kicked in when the response from the first call didn't arrive within 30 seconds. The first call had succeeded; the response was lost in the carrier network.

Root cause: No idempotency layer at the API. "We deduplicate by checking if order_id already has a payment" — but the order_id wasn't on the request, only the cart_id. Two payments for the same cart became two orders.

Fix: Mandatory Idempotency-Key header (UUIDv4 per logical user action, persisted client-side until 2xx response received). Server stores (key, sha256(body), status, response) with 24h TTL. Same key + same body hash → return cached response. Same key + different body → 422 with explicit "idempotency conflict."

Bonus pitfall fixed later: The first version put the idempotency check in the controller, in a separate TX from the payment write. Race condition between "check key" and "create payment" still allowed dupes. Moved the INSERT into idempotency_record into the same TX as the payment write, relying on UNIQUE constraint to be the actual gate.

Lesson: Network is unreliable; the client will retry whether you want it or not. If POST creates resources that touch money, idempotency is not optional. And idempotency at the controller layer is theater — it must be at the persistence boundary.

Production idempotency interceptor (Spring + Postgres)
// Domain
public record IdempotencyRecord(
        String key, String requestHash, Status status,
        Integer responseStatus, String responseBody,
        Instant createdAt, Instant expiresAt) {
    public enum Status { IN_PROGRESS, COMPLETED, FAILED }
}

// Port
public interface IdempotencyStore {
    void insertInProgress(String key, String hash, Instant expiresAt);  // throws DuplicateKey
    Optional<IdempotencyRecord> findByKey(String key);
    void markCompleted(String key, int status, String body);
}

// Service — boundary aware
@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class IdempotentRequestExecutor {

    private final IdempotencyStore store;
    private final Clock clock;
    private static final Duration TTL = Duration.ofHours(24);

    public <T> ResponseEntity<T> run(
            String key, Object body, Class<T> responseType,
            ObjectMapper json, Supplier<ResponseEntity<T>> work) {

        if (key == null || key.isBlank())
            throw new BadRequestException("Idempotency-Key header required");

        String hash = sha256(canonicalJson(body, json));
        try {
            store.insertInProgress(key, hash, clock.instant().plus(TTL));
        } catch (DuplicateKeyException dup) {
            var existing = store.findByKey(key).orElseThrow();
            if (!existing.requestHash().equals(hash)) {
                throw new ConflictException(
                        "Idempotency key reused with a different request body");
            }
            if (existing.status() == IdempotencyRecord.Status.IN_PROGRESS) {
                // another request is still processing — caller should back off
                throw new ConflictException("Original request still in progress");
            }
            // COMPLETED → replay the stored response
            return rebuild(existing, responseType, json);
        }

        // First time we see this key — run the real work
        ResponseEntity<T> result = work.get();
        try {
            store.markCompleted(key, result.getStatusCode().value(),
                    json.writeValueAsString(result.getBody()));
        } catch (JsonProcessingException e) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Cannot serialize response", e);
        }
        return result;
    }

    private <T> ResponseEntity<T> rebuild(IdempotencyRecord rec, Class<T> type, ObjectMapper json) {
        try {
            T body = json.readValue(rec.responseBody(), type);
            return ResponseEntity.status(rec.responseStatus())
                    .header("Idempotency-Replay", "true")
                    .body(body);
        } catch (JsonProcessingException e) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Cannot rebuild cached response", e);
        }
    }

    private static String sha256(String s) {
        try {
            byte[] d = MessageDigest.getInstance("SHA-256").digest(s.getBytes(UTF_8));
            return HexFormat.of().formatHex(d);
        } catch (NoSuchAlgorithmException e) { throw new IllegalStateException(e); }
    }
    private static String canonicalJson(Object o, ObjectMapper json) {
        try {
            return json.writer().with(SerializationFeature.ORDER_MAP_ENTRIES_BY_KEYS)
                    .writeValueAsString(o);
        } catch (JsonProcessingException e) { throw new IllegalStateException(e); }
    }
}

// Controller usage
@PostMapping("/payments")
public ResponseEntity<PaymentResponse> create(
        @RequestHeader("Idempotency-Key") String key,
        @Valid @RequestBody CreatePaymentRequest body) {
    return idempotent.run(key, body, PaymentResponse.class, mapper,
            () -> paymentUseCase.create(body));
}
Interview scenario — "design a retry-safe payment API"

Design the contract for a POST /payments endpoint that the mobile app will hit. Walk me through your decisions.

I'd require an Idempotency-Key header — UUIDv4 generated on the client when the user taps "Pay." The client persists it locally and only deletes it after a terminal response (2xx, 4xx that isn't 408/425/429). On any timeout or 5xx, retry with the same key. Server-side, that key is the gate.

Where do you store the key — Redis or Postgres?

For payments, Postgres. Redis is faster but is a separate consistency boundary. I want the idempotency record and the business write in the same transaction — UNIQUE constraint on the key is what actually prevents the duplicate, and only Postgres gives me that with the business data. Redis would force me to do "check-then-act" across two systems, which is exactly the race we're trying to avoid. For high-volume, non-money use cases I'd revisit.

What status codes do you return on the retry?

If the key is found and complete and the body hash matches: return the original status code (201, 200, whatever it was) with an extra Idempotency-Replay: true header so the client knows. Body hash different: 422 with a clear error code like IDEMPOTENCY_CONFLICT — the client has a bug it needs to know about. Key found and still IN_PROGRESS: 409 Conflict and the client should back off and retry.

What's the TTL on the key?

24 hours covers virtually all legitimate retry windows including app-resume-after-overnight. The table needs a partial index on expires_at and a cleanup job. I'd also partition the table by created_at month and DROP old partitions — DELETE on a busy idempotency table is expensive and creates bloat.

If the API is behind a load balancer, can two replicas race on the same key at exactly the same time?

Yes, and that's exactly what UNIQUE constraint solves. Both inserts hit the DB; one succeeds, one gets DuplicateKeyException. The loser reads the existing row — if it's IN_PROGRESS, return 409 and let the client retry; if COMPLETED, return the cached response. The DB is the synchronization primitive, not the application. That's why I push the check down into the persistence layer instead of trying to coordinate at the app level.

4. Microservices / Distributed Systems

A strong engineer must understand that microservices are a trade-off. Splitting into services is not always good.

#4
Key focus
Service boundary
Concept

Microservices split the system into many small services, each owning the data and logic of one business area and deployed independently. The service boundary is that dividing line.

Why it exists: So teams can deploy/scale independently and failures are isolated. But splitting wrong → a "distributed monolith": many services still tightly coupled, carrying every drawback of distribution with none of the benefits.

How it works
  1. Split by business capability / bounded context, not by technical layer.
  2. Each service owns its own database — do NOT share tables; communicate via API/event.
  3. A correct boundary = changing one business concern only touches one service (high cohesion, low coupling).
  4. Sign of a wrong split: changing one feature requires deploying 5 services at once.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Distributed monolith: changing one feature requires deploying many services at once → wrong boundary, consolidate by capability.
  2. Shared database: two services read/write the same table → implicit coupling through the schema. Each service should own its own DB.
  3. Chatty calls: one request fans out into 20 sync calls → cumulative latency, cascading failures. Batch/aggregate or switch to events.
  4. Decomposing too early (nano-service): operational overhead > benefits. Start with a modular monolith, split when there is clear pain.
  5. Shared jar holding common entities: a model change ripples across the whole system → each context should keep its own model.
// Bad: service B reads service A's DB directly
orderRepo.findByCustomerId(id);   // Order Service's table!

// Good: call via API/contract, or listen to a published event
OrderSummary s = orderClient.getByCustomer(id);   // HTTP/gRPC
// or listen to the OrderCreated event and keep a separate read model

A strong engineer does not split services by table or controller, but by business capability/bounded context.

What to understand deeply
  • Split by business capability / bounded context, not technical layer

    Do not split into: user-service, controller-service, db-service. Instead: Customer (owns customer lifecycle), Order (order lifecycle), Payment (payment lifecycle), Inventory. Each service has its own model, language, and team. Apply DDD: bounded context = service boundary.

  • Database per service — the service owns its data

    Other services may not query the DB directly; only via API/event. This lets a service change its own schema, change DB engine, and scale independently. Trade-off: cross-service reporting is harder — typically use a data lake / read replica / event sourcing.

  • Operational maturity is a prerequisite

    Microservices require: CI/CD per service, observability (log/metric/trace), service mesh or circuit breaker, on-call rotation, contract testing. A team of < 10 people lacking DevOps practice → microservices cause more harm than benefit.

  • Conway's Law

    "Architecture mirrors organization." If teams have no clear boundaries, the services will overlap too. Successful microservices go hand in hand with an organization split by capability ownership (Customer team, Order team, ...).

Production issue / pitfall
  • Shared database — distributed monolith

    2 services write the same table. A schema change requires syncing both services to deploy at once, more painful than a monolith because you also have to touch 2 codebases. Migrate to database per service: split the tables, sync via event or API.

  • Chain call too long: A → B → C → D

    Each call 100ms → 400ms latency. Each call has a 0.1% error rate → 0.4% total error. A failure in D propagates back up to A. Solution: aggregate at the gateway/BFF, async messaging instead of a sync chain, cache intermediate results.

    // Bad: sync chain
A.handle() -> B.lookup() -> C.enrich() -> D.persist()

// Better: A publishes an event, downstream services handle it themselves
A.handle() -> publish OrderRequested
   B,C,D consume in parallel/saga
  • No contract testing

    Service B changes its response format → service A crashes at runtime. Use Pact or Spring Cloud Contract: the consumer defines expectations, the provider verifies them in CI. Breaking the contract = build fails before deploy.

  • No distributed tracing

    A request fails in 1 of 5 services, logs scattered, no idea which service is the root cause. Set up OpenTelemetry / Jaeger / Zipkin with the trace id forwarded across every call. You can then see the request flow + latency at each step.

Example
Bad boundary:
- user-service owns user table
- order-service directly reads user DB
- payment-service updates order DB

Better boundary:
- Customer service exposes customer capability
- Order service owns order lifecycle
- Payment service owns payment lifecycle
- Communication through API/events
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I don't pick microservices by default. I weigh team size, deployment independence, domain boundary, and operational cost.'
Key focus
Saga and eventual consistency
Concept

A saga ensures consistency for a business operation that spans multiple services without using a distributed transaction: a sequence of local steps, each with a "compensation step" to undo it.

Why it exists: You can't lock all 5 databases in a single transaction (2PC is slow and hard to scale). A saga accepts eventual consistency in exchange for scalability.

How it works
  1. Each service does a local transaction and then emits an event signaling the next step.
  2. If a step fails → run compensation for the steps already done (e.g. refund, release stock).
  3. Choreography: services listen to each other's events. Orchestration: a coordinator commands each step.
  4. Each step must be idempotent + have a timeout; you need observability to trace the flow.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Payment fails after stock was already deducted: run a release-stock compensation to undo it.
  2. Compensation also fails: bounded retries + alert + push to a manual-intervention queue.
  3. A step hangs (timeout): set a timeout per step, and compensate when it expires.
  4. Choreography gets tangled with many steps: switch to an orchestrator (state machine) to see the whole flow.
  5. A non-idempotent step: compensation runs twice and drives stock negative → each step must be idempotent + persist saga state.
// Orchestrator: each local step + its corresponding compensation
saga.step(reserveStock,   compensate: releaseStock);
saga.step(capturePayment, compensate: refundPayment);
saga.step(confirmOrder,   compensate: cancelOrder);
// If capturePayment fails -> automatically run releaseStock (reserved earlier)

A strong engineer must know how to handle a multi-service business flow without using a distributed 2PC transaction.

What to understand deeply
  • Saga = a chain of local transactions + compensation

    Instead of 1 ACID transaction spanning 5 services (not feasible), split into 5 local txs. Each tx commits independently and sends an event to trigger the next tx. If tx 4 fails → run compensating actions for tx 1, 2, 3 (rollback semantics, not a DB rollback).

  • Choreography — event-driven, no coordinator

    Order publishes "OrderCreated" → Payment listens, charges, publishes "PaymentCompleted" → Inventory listens, reserves, publishes "InventoryReserved" → Order listens, marks CONFIRMED. Loose coupling, easy to add a new service. Downside: the overall flow is scattered, hard to debug and observe.

  • Orchestration — central coordinator

    A Saga Orchestrator (Temporal, Cadence, Camunda, or self-built) calls each step and tracks state. The code is explicit: "step 1 → step 2 → step 3, and if step 3 fails run compensation for steps 2,1." Easy to retry, easy to observe; the trade-off is one more component that needs HA.

    // Orchestrator pseudo
public OrderResult placeSaga(Order o) {
    var payment = paymentService.charge(o);
    try {
        var reserved = inventoryService.reserve(o);
        try {
            return orderService.confirm(o);
        } catch (Exception e) {
            inventoryService.release(reserved);
            throw e;
        }
    } catch (Exception e) {
        paymentService.refund(payment);
        throw e;
    }
}
  • Compensation must be designed from the start

    Each step needs an inverse: chargePayment ↔ refundPayment, reserveStock ↔ releaseStock, confirmOrder ↔ cancelOrder. Some actions can't be reversed (sending an email): call them after the saga succeeds, or send a "correction email".

Production issue / pitfall
  • Not designing compensation from the start

    You implement the happy path and only then think about rollback → you discover some steps already committed cannot be undone (an email to the customer "thanks for your order"). Every step must have a paired compensating action designed in parallel. Idempotent so retries are safe.

  • Consumer processing duplicate events

    Kafka at-least-once → the same "ReservationRequested" is consumed twice → stock deducted twice. The consumer must dedup by a processed event_id (see Kafka idempotent consumer).

  • No timeout for the saga

    Step 3 doesn't respond → the saga is stuck forever. The order sits in PENDING, stock is reserved but can't be sold, payment holds the money. You need a per-step timeout + a global saga timeout + alerts.

  • No state machine

    When support asks "where is order X in the saga?", nobody knows. Persist saga state explicitly: saga_instance(id, current_step, status, data, started_at, updated_at). Build a dashboard querying this state.

Example
Order Saga:
1. Order service creates PENDING order
2. Payment service reserves/charges payment
3. Inventory service reserves stock
4. Order service confirms order

If inventory fails:
- release/refund payment
- mark order as CANCELLED
Interview answer tip: A Strong answer should include: state, retry, idempotency, compensation, timeout, observability.
Key focus
Outbox pattern
Concept

The outbox pattern solves the problem "saving to the DB and publishing the message must both succeed." Instead of calling Kafka directly, we write the event into an outbox table in the SAME transaction as the business data.

Why it exists: If you save to the DB and only then publish to Kafka, when the publish fails → the DB has data but the message is lost (the dual-write problem). The outbox turns the two operations into one transaction.

How it works
  1. In one DB transaction: save the entity + insert one row into the outbox_event table.
  2. Commit → both exist or neither does.
  3. A separate publisher (polling or CDC/Debezium) reads the outbox → sends to Kafka → marks it as sent.
  4. Sending is at-least-once → the consumer must be idempotent (use the event id to deduplicate).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Dual write: the DB save is ok but the Kafka publish fails → the event is lost, the system drifts. The outbox writes within the same transaction.
  2. Publisher sends duplicates: crashes after sending to Kafka but before marking it sent → at-least-once → the consumer must be idempotent.
  3. The outbox table grows huge: a job to clean up sent records / partition by day.
  4. Need ordering per aggregate: set the Kafka key = aggregate_id so events for the same aggregate go to the same partition.
  5. Polling lag: use CDC (Debezium) to read the WAL instead of polling for near-real-time.
@Transactional
void placeOrder(...) {
    orderRepo.save(order);                         // 1) write the business data
    outboxRepo.save(new OutboxEvent(               // 2) write the event - SAME TX
        order.id(), "OrderCreated", toJson(order)));
}   // commit: both exist or neither does
// A separate publisher reads the outbox -> Kafka(key=order.id) -> mark sent

The outbox solves the problem where the DB commit succeeds but the Kafka publish fails, or the publish succeeds but the DB rolls back.

What to understand deeply
  • Atomically write business data + event into the same DB

    In 1 transaction: INSERT into the business table + INSERT into the outbox_event table. Either both commit, or both roll back. No more "DB commit but Kafka fail" or vice versa — because Kafka has not been called yet.

    CREATE TABLE outbox_event (
    id UUID PRIMARY KEY,
    aggregate_type VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    aggregate_id VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    event_type VARCHAR(80) NOT NULL,
    payload JSONB NOT NULL,
    status VARCHAR(20) NOT NULL DEFAULT 'NEW',
    created_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL,
    sent_at TIMESTAMP
);
CREATE INDEX idx_outbox_status ON outbox_event(status, created_at);
  • Background publisher poll + publish

    A periodic job (every 1s) SELECT WHERE status = 'NEW' LIMIT 100 → publish to Kafka → UPDATE status = 'SENT'. Or use Debezium CDC to read the binlog/WAL — no polling needed, near-realtime, low overhead.

  • Why the consumer still needs to be idempotent

    The publisher may: publish to Kafka successfully but crash before the UPDATE status. The next tx sees status still 'NEW' → publishes again. This is at-least-once delivery. The consumer dedups by event id (or business key).

  • Ordering & Cleanup

    Ordering: use aggregate_id as the Kafka key → the same aggregate goes to the same partition → ordering preserved. Cleanup: the outbox grows fast, schedule a job to delete rows sent > N days ago. Or partition the table by created_at.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Publishing to Kafka inside the transaction

    Service.method() @Transactional: save() → kafkaTemplate.send() → throw exception before commit → DB rolls back but Kafka already received it. The consumer sees the event but the DB has no data → the "event arrived too early" bug. Fix: the outbox pattern.

  • No retry/backoff for the publisher

    Kafka is temporarily down → the publisher marks the event as FAILED → never retries → the event is lost. Retry with exponential backoff, and track retry_count to alert when it gets unusually high.

  • Not cleaning the outbox

    The outbox table grows to tens of millions of rows, the query WHERE status='NEW' gets slow despite the index (bloat). Schedule a job to DELETE rows sent > 7 days ago, or partition by month and drop old partitions.

  • Consumer not deduping → double side effects

    The outbox guarantees at-least-once, not exactly-once. The consumer processes a duplicate → stock deducted twice, charged twice. Persist processed_event(event_id PRIMARY KEY) before the side effect.

Example
BEGIN;
INSERT INTO orders(id, status) VALUES ('O1', 'CREATED');
INSERT INTO outbox_event(id, aggregate_id, type, payload, status)
VALUES ('E1', 'O1', 'OrderCreated', '{...}', 'NEW');
COMMIT;

// Publisher later sends E1 to Kafka
Interview answer tip: Say: 'The outbox makes the state change and event creation atomic within the DB. Delivery out to Kafka is eventually consistent and needs an idempotent consumer.'
Deep dive — Microservices
Orchestrated saga: place-order across 3 services
     ┌─────────────┐
     │   Client    │
     └──────┬──────┘
            ▼
   ┌──────────────────┐
   │ Saga Orchestrator│  state machine, persisted: (saga_id, step, status)
   │   (Temporal /    │
   │    Camunda /     │
   │    custom)       │
   └────────┬─────────┘
            │
   ┌────────┼─────────┬──────────────┬──────────────┐
   ▼        │         ▼              ▼              ▼
 STEP 1     │       STEP 2         STEP 3        STEP 4
 reserve    │      charge         confirm      send email
 inventory  │      payment         order        (after success)
 ─────►     │     ─────►         ─────►        ─────►
   │        │         │              │              │
   │ ok     │         │ ok           │ ok           │ ok → DONE
   │ fail   │         │ fail         │ fail         │
   ▼        │         ▼              ▼
 ── ──      │   ───────────    ─────────────
 nothing    │   COMP step 1    COMP step 2,1
 to undo    │   release stock  refund + release

Failure of step N triggers reverse compensation N-1, N-2, ..., 1
Each compensation must be idempotent (might be retried)
Each step must be idempotent (orchestrator may retry on timeout)

Observable state:
  SELECT step, status, last_error
  FROM saga_instance WHERE saga_id = ?
→ support team can answer "where is order X stuck?"
War story — "the inventory that vanished"

System: e-commerce, choreographed saga: Order → Payment → Inventory → Shipping, each service publishing/consuming Kafka events.

Symptom: Slow burn over weeks. Inventory count drifted negative on a few SKUs. Finance flagged it in monthly reconciliation — ~2,400 "reserved" units that no order claimed, mixed with a few oversells.

Investigation: No single root cause. Three bugs compounding: (1) Inventory service consumed PaymentSucceeded and called reserve() — but the consumer wasn't idempotent. Kafka rebalance during a deploy redelivered ~200 messages → 200 double-reservations. (2) When a downstream timeout occurred in the order saga, "release reservation" was published — but the consumer error path silently swallowed exceptions on FK-violation rows ("reservation already released"). (3) No saga timeout: when a service was down for hours, reservations sat in PENDING forever; release was published by a manual cleanup that wasn't logged.

Root cause: Choreography without observability. No saga state machine, no view of "which orders are mid-flight." Every service did its part correctly in isolation; emergent behavior was wrong.

Fix: Three changes. (a) Idempotent consumer: processed_event table with event_id PK, inserted in the same TX as the side effect. (b) Move from choreography to orchestration (Temporal) for the order flow — saga state is now queryable. (c) Per-step timeout + dead-letter to ops queue with a runbook for manual reconciliation.

Lesson: Choreography is seductive ("look, no coordinator!") but a saga without explicit state is a saga without observability. When you can't answer "where is this order right now?" in one query, you'll discover problems weeks later in a finance report instead of minutes later in an alert.

Orchestrated saga with explicit state + compensation
// Saga state, persisted in Postgres
@Entity @Table(name = "saga_instance")
public class SagaInstance {
    @Id private UUID id;
    @Enumerated(STRING) private SagaType type;     // PLACE_ORDER
    @Enumerated(STRING) private SagaStatus status; // RUNNING, COMPENSATING, COMPLETED, FAILED
    private String currentStep;
    @Column(columnDefinition = "jsonb") private String context;
    private Instant startedAt;
    private Instant updatedAt;
    @Column(columnDefinition = "text") private String lastError;
}

@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class PlaceOrderSaga {

    private final SagaInstanceRepository sagas;
    private final InventoryClient inventory;
    private final PaymentClient payment;
    private final OrderClient order;
    private final ObjectMapper json;

    public UUID start(PlaceOrderCommand cmd) {
        SagaInstance s = SagaInstance.create(SagaType.PLACE_ORDER, json.writeValueAsString(cmd));
        sagas.save(s);
        return s.getId();
    }

    @Transactional
    public void advance(UUID sagaId) {
        SagaInstance s = sagas.lockForUpdate(sagaId);          // SELECT ... FOR UPDATE
        if (s.getStatus() != SagaStatus.RUNNING) return;       // already done or compensating
        try {
            switch (s.getCurrentStep()) {
                case "RESERVE_INVENTORY" -> reserveInventory(s);
                case "CHARGE_PAYMENT"    -> chargePayment(s);
                case "CONFIRM_ORDER"     -> confirmOrder(s);
                default -> throw new IllegalStateException("Unknown step " + s.getCurrentStep());
            }
        } catch (RetryableException e) {
            s.recordError(e.getMessage());                     // stays RUNNING, retried by scheduler
            sagas.save(s);
        } catch (NonRetryableException e) {
            s.markCompensating(e.getMessage());
            sagas.save(s);
        }
    }

    @Scheduled(fixedDelay = 5_000)
    public void tick() {
        sagas.findRunningOlderThan(Instant.now().minusSeconds(2), 50)
             .forEach(s -> advance(s.getId()));
        sagas.findCompensatingOlderThan(Instant.now().minusSeconds(2), 50)
             .forEach(s -> compensate(s.getId()));
        sagas.findStuckOlderThan(Instant.now().minus(Duration.ofMinutes(30)))
             .forEach(this::alertOnCall);                      // saga timeout
    }

    @Transactional
    public void compensate(UUID sagaId) {
        SagaInstance s = sagas.lockForUpdate(sagaId);
        // Walk back through completed steps in reverse
        var done = s.completedSteps();
        for (int i = done.size() - 1; i >= 0; i--) {
            switch (done.get(i)) {
                case "CHARGE_PAYMENT"    -> payment.refund(s.getId());     // idempotent
                case "RESERVE_INVENTORY" -> inventory.release(s.getId());  // idempotent
            }
        }
        s.markFailed();
        sagas.save(s);
    }
}
Interview scenario — "design place-order across 3 services"

A user clicks "Place Order." It must reserve inventory, charge payment, and create the order. These are three services. How do you do it?

I'd reach for an orchestrated saga, not 2PC and not pure choreography. 2PC is off the table — it doesn't work across heterogeneous services and has known availability issues. Pure choreography is fine for two services but at three it becomes hard to answer "where is this order right now?" and that's a question support will ask weekly.

Why orchestration over choreography here?

Three reasons. Observability: with an orchestrator, saga state is one row I can query — "show me all sagas stuck in CHARGE_PAYMENT for over 5 minutes." With choreography, state is implicit in the event log and hard to reconstruct. Compensation: orchestrator code reads top-to-bottom, the reverse path is explicit. With choreography, every service has to know how to undo its own action and trust everyone else to do theirs. And testability: I can unit-test the orchestrator state machine without standing up Kafka.

What happens if the payment succeeds but the order service is down when you try to confirm?

The orchestrator retries with backoff for transient failures. If retries exhaust, it transitions the saga to COMPENSATING and walks back: refund the payment, release the inventory. Both compensations must be idempotent because the orchestrator may retry them too. I'd also put a global timeout per saga — say 10 minutes for place-order — after which we stop retrying, mark FAILED, and page on-call with the saga ID. A saga stuck for 30 minutes is almost always a system issue, not a transient one.

How do you handle the case where refund itself fails?

Honest answer: it can't fail "for real" — we keep retrying. If refund returns a permanent error (account closed, charge already disputed), it goes into a manual reconciliation queue with the saga context, and finance handles it. The saga is marked FAILED with the residue noted. The system doesn't pretend it can mechanically resolve every edge — it makes the unresolvable ones loud.

How do you avoid duplicate reservations if Kafka redelivers an event?

Every consumer dedupes by event_id. There's a processed_event table with event_id as primary key. The consumer's transaction is: INSERT processed_event → do the business write → commit. If the INSERT fails with duplicate key, the event was already processed and we skip the body. This works because both INSERTs are in the same Postgres TX — the side effect can't happen without the dedup record. Pure at-least-once delivery + idempotent persistence layer = exactly-once business semantics, which is the only kind that actually exists.

5. Kafka / Event-driven Architecture

A strong engineer Kafka engineer does not just know producer/consumer. You must master partition, ordering, offset, retry, DLQ, idempotency and schema evolution.

#5
Key focus
Partition, consumer group, ordering
Concept

Kafka is a distributed message log. A Topic is split into many partitions (sub-logs) to scale. A Consumer group is a group of consumers that share the reading of a topic's partitions.

Why it exists: A partition processes sequentially, so it limits throughput; multiple partitions allow parallelism. A consumer group enables scaling consumption and ensures each message is processed once within the group.

How it works
  1. The producer picks a partition by key (hash(key) % numPartitions) → the same key always lands in the same partition.
  2. Message order is only guaranteed WITHIN a partition, not across the whole topic.
  3. Each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group → parallelism level = number of partitions.
  4. More consumers than partitions → the surplus consumers sit idle.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Lost order processing sequence: events of the same order are needed in order → set key = orderId so they go to the same partition.
  2. Hot partition: one key takes most of the traffic → skew, one consumer overloaded. Choose a more evenly distributed key.
  3. Increasing partitions while running: key hash remaps → historical ordering lost. Plan a sufficiently large partition count from the start.
  4. Fewer consumers than partitions: one consumer holds many partitions → lag. Scale consumers (≤ number of partitions).
  5. Rebalance storm: consumers restart continuously → tune session.timeout, use static membership.
// Same key -> same partition -> keep ordering per order
producer.send(new ProducerRecord<>(
    "order-events",
    order.getId(),          // KEY: determines the partition (hash(key) % n)
    toJson(event)));
// Different orders still run in parallel across many partitions

An experienced engineer must know that Kafka scales via partitions, but ordering is only guaranteed within the same partition.

What to understand deeply
  • Topic = collection of partitions; partition = ordered log

    Each partition is an append-only log, with messages having increasing offsets (0, 1, 2, ...). Order is only guaranteed WITHIN a partition, not globally. A topic with 6 partitions = you can consume 6 streams in parallel.

  • Consumer group — partition distribution

    A topic with 6 partitions, a group with 3 consumers → each consumer "owns" 2 partitions. A different group (group B) reads the same topic independently of group A. A partition belongs to ONLY 1 consumer within the same group at a time.

    Topic: order-events (6 partitions)
Group "order-projection" (3 consumers):
  consumer-1 → P0, P1
  consumer-2 → P2, P3
  consumer-3 → P4, P5

Group "audit-log" (1 consumer):
  consumer-A → P0..P5 (reads independently of group order-projection)
  • Message key → partition selection

    Default partitioner: hash(key) % numPartitions. Same key → same partition → keeps order by key. Setting orderId as the key ensures OrderCreated, OrderPaid, OrderShipped of the same order go to the same partition and are processed sequentially.

  • Maximum throughput = number of partitions

    Adding a 7th consumer to a group with 6 partitions → consumer 7 is IDLE. To increase throughput you must increase partitions (irreversible online — you can only add, not reduce). Plan the partition count from the start.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Random key — lost ordering

    The producer sends send(topic, randomUUID, event) → events of the same order go to many partitions → the consumer receives OrderPaid before OrderCreated. A bug hard to debug because it only happens under load. The key must be a stable business id (orderId, userId, accountId).

  • Too few partitions

    A topic with 3 partitions, traffic grows → max parallelism = 3 consumers. Consumer lag grows. You can add partitions online but key hashing changes → ordering may break for old keys. Predict capacity ahead.

  • Too many partitions

    1000 partitions for a 100 msg/s topic → controller overhead, ZooKeeper/KRaft metadata, file handles, replication network. Each partition is 1 file. Rule of thumb: 10-100 partitions per topic is common.

  • Rebalance pauses all consumers in the group

    Consumer crash, restart, scale up/down → triggers a rebalance. During a rebalance (Stop-the-World rebalance, by default) all consumers in the group stop. A large update = downtime. Use cooperative rebalance (incremental) in newer Kafka clients to reduce impact.

Example
Order events key = orderId

OrderCreated(orderId=O1) -> partition 2
OrderPaid(orderId=O1)    -> partition 2
OrderShipped(orderId=O1) -> partition 2

=> order per orderId is kept within the same partition.
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Kafka does not guarantee global order. I guarantee order by business key using the message key, for example orderId.'
Key focus
Offset commit and delivery semantics
Concept

The offset is the position (sequence number) of the message the consumer has read up to in the partition. Committing the offset = recording "processed up to here". Delivery semantics are the message delivery guarantee level: at-most-once, at-least-once, exactly-once.

Why it exists: When a consumer restarts, it reads on from the committed offset. The commit timing (before or after processing) decides whether a message is lost or processed multiple times.

How it works
  1. Commit BEFORE processing → crash mid-way = lost message (at-most-once).
  2. Commit AFTER processing → crash after processing before commit = reprocessed (at-least-once) — the most common.
  3. Exactly-once needs Kafka transactions or an idempotent consumer + outbox.
  4. Disable auto-commit, commit manually after processing finishes to control semantics.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Commit before processing: crash mid-way → lost message (at-most-once). Commit after processing finishes.
  2. Auto-commit + async processing: offset committed while the message is not yet processed → lost. Disable auto-commit.
  3. Reprocess after crash (at-least-once): message reprocessed → the consumer must be idempotent.
  4. Consumer lag growing steadily: scale consumers, optimize processing, or process in batches.
  5. Replay from an old offset: reset --to-earliest/--to-datetime carefully to avoid double-processing.

An experienced engineer must understand that committing the offset before/after processing affects message loss or duplicate messages.

What to understand deeply
  • At-least-once: commit after processing

    poll → process → commit offset. If it crashes after process before commit → the message is polled again and processed again. Duplicate processing can occur → the consumer must be idempotent. This is the most common pattern.

    // Spring Kafka manual ack
@KafkaListener(topics = "orders", containerFactory = "manualAckFactory")
public void onMessage(ConsumerRecord<String,String> rec, Acknowledgment ack) {
    processIdempotent(rec); // safe to run multiple times
    ack.acknowledge();       // commit only after success
}
  • At-most-once: commit before processing

    poll → commit → process. If it crashes between commit and process → the message is skipped (not polled again). Suitable when losing a message is acceptable (metrics, logs). Very rarely used for business events.

  • Exactly-once: Kafka transactions

    The producer uses transactional.id + the consumer uses isolation.level=read_committed. The producer writes to Kafka + commits the consumer offset within the same transaction. Only exactly-once within Kafka. An external DB/API write still needs idempotency.

  • Manual ack vs auto-commit

    Default enable.auto.commit=true commits periodically every 5s, not tied to business processing. A strong engineer in production always sets enable.auto.commit=false + manual ack after processing succeeds.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Auto-commit firing too early — lost message

    Default auto-commit interval 5s. Processing a message takes long, not finished but auto-commit already ran → restart pod → the message is considered done, not polled again → lost. Disable auto-commit, use manual ack.

  • Infinite retry → stuck partition

    A poison message (wrong format) causes an exception → the consumer does not ack → polls the same message again → loops forever. The whole partition is stuck, lag grows infinitely. You must have max retry + DLQ.

  • Not distinguishing retryable vs non-retryable

    A 500 from an external API → retry may work (transient). A 400 validation → retry is useless, the message is malformed. The consumer code classifies exceptions → transient = retry, permanent = DLQ immediately.

    try {
    processor.handle(event);
} catch (TransientException e) {
    throw e; // retry
} catch (ValidationException | NonRetryableException e) {
    dlq.send(event, e);  // no retry
    ack.acknowledge();
}
  • No DLQ

    Poison messages pile up in the main topic, the consumer crashes continuously, lag = forever. DLQ = a separate topic that receives rejected messages. Has alerts + a replay tool after fixing the bug.

Example
@KafkaListener(topics = "order-created")
public void consume(OrderCreated event, Acknowledgment ack) {
    orderProjectionService.apply(event); // idempotent
    ack.acknowledge(); // commit after successful processing
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I usually commit after successful processing, accept duplicates and handle them with an idempotent consumer.'
Key focus
Retry, DLQ, idempotent consumer
Concept

When a consumer processes a message and fails: Retry is trying again; DLQ (Dead Letter Queue) is a topic holding messages that can never be processed; an idempotent consumer is a consumer for which processing a message multiple times is still safe.

Why it exists: A transient error (DB congestion) should be retried; a permanent error (corrupted message) should not be retried infinitely, which stalls the partition → push it to the DLQ. Because Kafka is at-least-once, the consumer is forced to be idempotent.

How it works
  1. Transient error → retry with backoff (usually via a separate retry topic, not blocking the main partition).
  2. Exceeded the retry count → push the message to the DLQ with error metadata for later investigation/replay.
  3. The consumer stores the processed message id (DB/Redis) → a duplicate message is skipped.
  4. Have a mechanism to replay from the DLQ after the bug is fixed.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Poison message stalls the partition: infinite retry blocks subsequent messages → limit retries then push to DLQ.
  2. Synchronous retry blocks the partition: use a separate retry topic with delay (non-blocking retry).
  3. Nobody handles the DLQ: alert when the DLQ grows + a replay tool after fixing the bug.
  4. Retry on a non-idempotent side effect: sending mail/charging money repeatedly → make the consumer idempotent by message id.
  5. Fixed backoff causes a thundering herd: exponential backoff + jitter.

In production, Kafka failures are normal. An experienced engineer must design retry that does not stall the whole partition.

What to understand deeply
  • In-memory retry — short transient errors

    Retry 2-3 times with exponential backoff (100ms, 500ms, 2s). Suitable for transient: connection reset, lock timeout. Note: in-memory retry blocks the partition during the retry period → the added delay should not be too large.

  • Retry topic — longer delay without blocking

    Create topics orders-retry-1m, orders-retry-5m, orders-retry-30m. On failure, publish the message to a retry topic with a delay header → the retry topic's consumer waits the required time then re-publishes back to the main topic. The main partition is not blocked.

    // Spring Kafka non-blocking retry
@RetryableTopic(
    attempts = "5",
    backoff = @Backoff(delay = 60000, multiplier = 5),
    dltStrategy = DltStrategy.FAIL_ON_ERROR,
    autoCreateTopics = "true"
)
@KafkaListener(topics = "orders")
public void handle(OrderEvent e) { ... }
  • DLQ — message kept for inspect/replay

    After N retries still failing → publish to the DLQ topic with metadata: original topic, partition, offset, error stack trace. Have monitoring (DLQ consumer lag, count). Have a UI/CLI to view the payload and replay after fixing the bug.

  • Idempotent consumer = dedup at the DB layer

    A processed_event table with event_id as PK. Before the side effect: INSERT processed_event. Duplicate → exception → skip safely. Or use a version: only apply if the version is newer than the applied version.

    @Transactional
public void apply(OrderEvent e) {
    try {
        processedEventRepo.insert(e.getEventId());
    } catch (DuplicateKeyException dup) {
        return;  // already processed
    }
    orderProjectionRepo.update(e);
}
Production issues / pitfalls
  • In-memory retry too long blocks the partition

    Retry 10 times × 10s backoff = 100s per message. Meanwhile other messages in the same partition must wait. Lag grows dramatically. Limit in-memory retry to < 5s total, longer than that use a retry topic.

  • DLQ not monitored — silent failure

    A message goes to the DLQ, nobody knows → the bug persists for weeks → a backlog that cannot be replayed. Set an alert: DLQ message count > 0 within N minutes → page on-call. A dashboard showing DLQ size per topic.

  • Replaying the DLQ non-idempotently → double side-effect

    A "ChargePayment" message went to the DLQ due to a transient error. After the fix, replay → charge 1 extra time. The consumer must be idempotent (dedup event_id) — replay is safe.

  • Logs missing information to debug

    A "process failed" log has no: event_id, payload (sanitize sensitive), correlation_id, retry count, exception stack. When production fails, you cannot reproduce. Structured JSON logs with every necessary field.

Example
CREATE TABLE processed_event (
    event_id VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    processed_at TIMESTAMP NOT NULL
);

// Before processing:
// INSERT event_id
// If duplicate key => skip safely
Interview answer tip: State it fully: retry policy, max attempts, backoff, DLQ, alert, replay, idempotency.
Deep dive — Kafka
Partition layout + non-blocking retry + DLQ
Topic: orders (6 partitions, replication.factor=3)
key = orderId → hash → partition

  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │ P0: O1, O7, ...    O1 events stay ordered here  │
  │ P1: O2, ...                                     │
  │ P2: O3, O9, ...                                 │
  │ P3: O4, ...                                     │
  │ P4: O5, ...                                     │
  │ P5: O6, ...                                     │
  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
   Group "order-projection" (3 consumers):
     c1 ← P0, P1     c2 ← P2, P3     c3 ← P4, P5

Consumer flow (per record):
   poll  ─►  process  ─►  manual ack (commit offset)
            │
            ├─ TransientException (5xx, timeout, lock_timeout)
            │     │
            │     ▼
            │  send to orders-retry-1m  (with attempt header)
            │  ack original  (don't block partition!)
            │
            └─ NonRetryable (validation, schema mismatch)
                  │
                  ▼
              send to orders-dlq  (with stack + payload)
              ack original
              ALERT on-call (dlq.size > 0)

Retry topics ladder:
  orders-retry-1m  → consumer waits 1 min, republish to orders
  orders-retry-5m  → consumer waits 5 min,  ditto
  orders-retry-30m → after 5 attempts → DLQ

Why ladder, not in-memory retry?
  ─ in-memory retry blocks the partition
  ─ ladder isolates slow retries from healthy traffic
War story — "the poison message that ate Friday"

Topic: customer-events, 12 partitions, consumed by 6 services for various projections.

Symptom: Friday 14:00, customer service dashboard shows stale data. Email projection 8 hours behind. CRM sync 12 hours behind. Nothing alerted.

Investigation: All six consumer groups had massive lag on the exact same partition (P7). Other partitions were healthy. We pulled the message at the head of P7's lag: a producer had emitted a malformed event with customer_id = null after a schema migration. Every consumer threw NPE → no ack → poll the same message → loop forever. Six teams' projections stuck on the same broken record.

Root cause: Consumers had no max-retry, no DLQ, no validation at the consumer boundary. The producer's schema migration test had passed because the staging dataset didn't include the edge case. And the lag alert was on the consumer group level — but lag was only on one partition, averaging out to "fine."

Fix: Three layers. (a) Spring Kafka @RetryableTopic with non-blocking retry → after 3 attempts a record goes to customer-events-dlq. (b) Schema validation at the consumer (Avro + Schema Registry compatibility checks) — bad records get DLQ'd at the edge, not deep inside business code. (c) Alert on per-partition lag, not just per-group. One stuck partition is invisible in a group-average metric.

Lesson: "At-least-once" means you also have to design for "if-broken-don't-block-forever." A poison message is a daily occurrence in any system that exists for more than a year. The choice isn't "will we get one" but "what happens when we do." And: average metrics hide tail problems — alert on max, not avg, for anything that can be partitioned.

Idempotent consumer with non-blocking retry + DLQ + tracing
// 1. Schema for dedup
CREATE TABLE processed_event (
    event_id    UUID PRIMARY KEY,
    aggregate_id VARCHAR(100),
    processed_at TIMESTAMPTZ NOT NULL DEFAULT now()
);
CREATE INDEX idx_processed_event_processed_at ON processed_event(processed_at);

// 2. Spring Kafka listener
@Component
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class OrderEventListener {

    private final OrderProjectionService projection;
    private final ProcessedEventRepository processed;
    private final ObservationRegistry observation;

    @RetryableTopic(
        attempts = "4",
        backoff = @Backoff(delay = 60_000, multiplier = 5),    // 1m, 5m, 25m
        autoCreateTopics = "true",
        dltStrategy = DltStrategy.FAIL_ON_ERROR,
        include = { TransientException.class, ResourceAccessException.class }
        // NOT included → goes straight to DLT: ValidationException, schema mismatches
    )
    @KafkaListener(
        topics = "orders",
        groupId = "order-projection",
        containerFactory = "manualAckFactory")
    @Transactional
    public void onMessage(
            @Header("event-id") UUID eventId,
            @Header(value = "traceparent", required = false) String traceparent,
            @Payload OrderEvent event,
            Acknowledgment ack) {

        Observation.createNotStarted("kafka.consume.order", observation)
            .contextualName("orders/" + event.type())
            .lowCardinalityKeyValue("event.type", event.type().name())
            .observe(() -> {
                try {
                    processed.insert(new ProcessedEvent(eventId, event.aggregateId(), Instant.now()));
                } catch (DuplicateKeyException dup) {
                    Log.debugf("Skipping already-processed event %s", eventId);
                    ack.acknowledge();
                    return;
                }
                projection.apply(event);     // idempotent business write
                ack.acknowledge();
            });
    }

    @DltHandler
    public void onDlt(
            OrderEvent event,
            @Header(KafkaHeaders.ORIGINAL_TOPIC) String origTopic,
            @Header(KafkaHeaders.EXCEPTION_MESSAGE) String error,
            @Header("event-id") UUID eventId,
            Acknowledgment ack) {
        Log.errorf("DLT received eventId=%s origTopic=%s error=%s payload=%s",
                eventId, origTopic, error, event);
        dltAlerts.notify(eventId, origTopic, error);
        ack.acknowledge();
    }
}

// 3. Sane producer config (idempotent + ordered + durable)
spring.kafka.producer.acks: all
spring.kafka.producer.enable-idempotence: true
spring.kafka.producer.max-in-flight-requests-per-connection: 5
spring.kafka.producer.retries: 2147483647
spring.kafka.producer.compression-type: zstd

// 4. Consumer config
spring.kafka.consumer.enable-auto-commit: false
spring.kafka.consumer.max-poll-records: 50
spring.kafka.consumer.max-poll-interval-ms: 300000      // 5 min headroom
spring.kafka.consumer.partition-assignment-strategy:
  org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.CooperativeStickyAssignor
Interview scenario — "your Kafka consumer is lagging, what now?"

You're on call. PagerDuty fires: "order-projection lag > 100k." Walk me through the next 10 minutes.

First minute: check the dashboard for which partition is lagging — is it spread across all partitions or concentrated on one or two? Spread = consumer is uniformly slow (CPU, downstream, GC). Concentrated = poison message or hot key. The remediation is completely different.

Say it's concentrated on partition 7.

Almost certainly a poison message or a hot key. I'd grab the record at the lag head — Kafka UI lets me peek at offset = current-committed + 1. If the payload is malformed, that's a bug at the consumer's validation boundary; I'd push it to DLQ manually (or restart the consumer with a config to skip parse errors) and file a ticket on the producer. If it's a hot key (same orderId getting hundreds of events), the consumer is doing per-record work that's piling up — I'd check whether per-key locking or external API calls are the bottleneck.

Say the lag is spread evenly. What do you check?

Three things in parallel. Consumer CPU and GC — sometimes a heap pressure issue makes everything 10x slower. Downstream dependencies — if the consumer writes to Postgres and Postgres CPU is at 100%, scaling consumers will make it worse. And lag rate — is it growing, flat, or shrinking? If shrinking, we're recovering, don't make changes. If growing, we need to either scale or shed.

Can you "just add more consumers" to drain faster?

Only up to the number of partitions. If the topic has 12 partitions, the 13th consumer in the group is idle. And adding consumers triggers a rebalance — with default stop-the-world rebalance, all consumers pause briefly, which can make lag spike before it improves. Cooperative rebalancing helps, but it's not free. Better to size partitions ahead of expected throughput. I keep a runbook line that says: "Don't scale during incident unless you've already eliminated downstream bottlenecks."

After the incident, what's the post-mortem action item?

Two. One, alert on per-partition lag, not group-average — concentrated lag was invisible until customers complained. Two, audit retry strategy: if we'd had non-blocking retry with DLQ, the poison message would have moved out of the main topic in under 5 minutes and we'd have alerted on DLQ size rising, not on lag. The structural fix is to make sure the next incident isn't the same incident.

6. SQL / Transaction / Database Performance

A strong engineer must understand that the DB is the most common bottleneck: index, transaction, lock, pool, isolation, query plan.

#6
Key focus
ACID and isolation level
Concept

ACID is the four guarantees of a transaction: Atomicity (all or nothing), Consistency (preserve constraints), Isolation (transactions do not step on each other), Durability (once committed, not lost). The isolation level tunes the degree of isolation.

Why it exists: Many transactions running in parallel can read/overwrite each other's data, causing bugs (dirty read, lost update). The isolation level allows a trade-off between correctness and performance.

How it works
  1. READ UNCOMMITTED: sees uncommitted data (dirty read) — almost never used.
  2. READ COMMITTED: only sees committed data (Postgres default) — still has non-repeatable read.
  3. REPEATABLE READ: re-reading within the same TX yields the same result (MySQL InnoDB default).
  4. SERIALIZABLE: behaves as if running sequentially — most correct but slowest. Prevents lost update via optimistic (version) or pessimistic lock.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Lost update: two users edit the balance at the same time → optimistic lock (version) or SELECT ... FOR UPDATE.
  2. Read stock then deduct (check-then-act): oversell under concurrency → atomic UPDATE ... WHERE stock >= qty.
  3. Long transaction holding a lock: blocks other transactions → shrink the TX, do not call external I/O inside the TX.
  4. Report hit by phantom read: needs SERIALIZABLE or a range lock for the sensitive computation.
  5. Different default isolation across environments: Postgres READ COMMITTED vs MySQL REPEATABLE READ → behavior diverges if you are unaware.

A strong engineer does not just read the ACID definition, but knows how isolation affects concurrency bugs like duplicate booking, overselling, lost update.

What to understand deeply
  • Atomicity: all-or-nothing

    BEGIN ... COMMIT/ROLLBACK. Either all statements in the tx succeed, or all are undone. The DB implements this via undo log/WAL. Application code does not have to worry about cleanup on rollback.

  • Consistency: invariants are not violated

    Constraints (NOT NULL, CHECK, FK, UNIQUE) + business rules in the app. After commit, the DB is in a valid state. Note: consistency here is constraint-level, not the distributed consistency of CAP.

  • Isolation levels — trade-off correctness vs concurrency

    Read Uncommitted: allows dirty read. Read Committed (Postgres default): no dirty read, has non-repeatable read. Repeatable Read (MySQL InnoDB default): no non-repeatable, has phantom read (MySQL fixes it with gap lock). Serializable: safest, lowest throughput (Postgres uses SSI — Serializable Snapshot Isolation).

    -- Postgres: check the current isolation
SHOW transaction_isolation;  -- read committed

-- Raise isolation for one tx
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL REPEATABLE READ;
  • Durability: commit = persistent across a crash

    The DB writes WAL (Write-Ahead Log) before updating the data file. Crash → recovery uses WAL replay. fsync ensures the WAL has been flushed to disk. Disabling fsync (for performance) = losing ACID's D.

  • Lost update — still happens at Read Committed

    Tx A reads balance = 100, Tx B reads balance = 100. A: SET balance = 80 (withdraw 20). B: SET balance = 70 (withdraw 30). Result: 70 (A's 20 is lost). Read Committed does not prevent it. You need an explicit lock (SELECT FOR UPDATE) or optimistic versioning.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Thinking @Transactional fixes a race condition

    @Transactional only guarantees ACID for one transaction (atomic, isolated per the default level). It does NOT prevent 2 concurrent transactions from reading the same row and overwriting each other. You need an explicit lock or optimistic version.

  • Not knowing the default isolation of the DB in use

    Postgres = Read Committed; MySQL InnoDB = Repeatable Read; Oracle = Read Committed. Code moving from MySQL to Postgres → phantom read appears. Read the DB engine docs, understand the default before designing logic.

  • Updating inventory/balance without a lock

    Overselling: 2 requests both buy the last item, both read stock = 1, both update stock = 0 → sells 2 while only 1 exists. Fix with an atomic update WHERE clause, optimistic version, or pessimistic SELECT FOR UPDATE.

    -- Atomic conditional update (safest, simplest)
UPDATE product_stock
SET quantity = quantity - 1
WHERE id = ? AND quantity > 0;
-- rowsAffected = 0 → out of stock
  • Long transaction holding a lock

    @Transactional wrapping 5s of logic (with an HTTP call) → row locked for 5s → other transactions wait. Lock cascading can cause a timeout chain. Keep the tx short, external calls outside the tx.

Example
Overselling problem:
1. Tx A reads stock = 1
2. Tx B reads stock = 1
3. Tx A sells 1, stock = 0
4. Tx B sells 1, stock = 0

=> Sold 2 while only 1 item existed.

Fix:
- optimistic locking with version
- pessimistic lock SELECT FOR UPDATE
- atomic update WHERE stock > 0
Interview answer tip: Give a real case: 'For inventory or balance, I do not just rely on read-then-write. I use an optimistic lock or an atomic conditional update.'
Key focus
Index and query plan
Concept

An index is an auxiliary data structure (usually a B-tree) that helps the DB find rows quickly without scanning the whole table. A query plan is the plan the DB chooses to execute a query (which index, what kind of join).

Why it exists: A full table scan over millions of rows is very slow. An index is like a book's table of contents — you jump straight to the page you need, without reading the whole book. Read the query plan to know why a query is slow.

Analogy: Index = the index at the back of a book; without it you have to flip through every page.

How it works
  1. The optimizer estimates the cost of each execution path and picks the cheapest.
  2. EXPLAIN ANALYZE shows the plan: Index Scan (good) vs Seq Scan (a warning if the table is large).
  3. A composite index (a,b,c) can be used for conditions on the left prefix (a, or a+b).
  4. An index speeds up reads but slows down writes and costs storage → only index according to the actual query pattern.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Query slow due to seq scan: add an index on the columns in WHERE/JOIN/ORDER BY.
  2. Composite index with wrong column order: index (b,a) does not serve WHERE a → order columns by query pattern + selectivity.
  3. Function on a column disables the index (WHERE lower(email)=): use a functional index or store a pre-normalized form.
  4. Over-indexing: too many indexes slow writes + cost disk → drop unused indexes (see pg_stat_user_indexes).
  5. LIKE '%x%' does not use an index: use full-text / trigram (pg_trgm).
  6. Wrong plan due to stale statistics: run ANALYZE so the optimizer estimates correctly.
EXPLAIN ANALYZE
SELECT * FROM orders WHERE customer_id = 42 AND status = 'PAID';
-- Seq Scan (bad, large table)  ->  need an index:
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_cust_status ON orders(customer_id, status);
-- After the index: Index Scan, rows filtered early, many times faster

A strong engineer must know that an index is not always faster just by adding it. An index helps reads but makes writes slower and costs storage.

What to understand deeply
  • B-tree index: usable for =, <, >, BETWEEN, ORDER BY

    The B-tree structure allows O(log n) lookup and sequential range scan. Does NOT support LIKE '%kw', NOT, OR (unless the optimizer merges indexes). Hash index, GiST, GIN are for other use cases.

  • Composite index — column order matters

    Index (a, b, c): query WHERE a=? works; WHERE a=? AND b=? works; WHERE b=? does NOT work (a is the leading column). Rule: equality columns first, range/sort columns after.

    -- Query
SELECT * FROM orders
WHERE customer_id = ? AND status = ?
ORDER BY created_at DESC LIMIT 20;

-- Suitable index
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_cust_status_created
ON orders(customer_id, status, created_at DESC);
  • Function on a column → bypasses the index

    WHERE LOWER(email) = ? does not use the index on email — the DB must compute LOWER for every row. Fix: a functional index CREATE INDEX ON users(LOWER(email)), or store a separate lowercase column.

  • LIKE leading wildcard — full scan

    LIKE 'abc%' can use a B-tree. LIKE '%abc' or LIKE '%abc%' cannot. Use full-text search (Postgres tsvector, Elasticsearch) or a trigram index (pg_trgm) for substring search.

  • EXPLAIN ANALYZE — read the actual plan

    EXPLAIN only estimates; EXPLAIN ANALYZE actually runs and measures actual rows + actual time. Read the important keywords: Seq Scan (full scan), Index Scan, Index Only Scan, Bitmap Heap Scan, Nested Loop, Hash Join, Sort.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Indexing each column instead of a composite

    3 single indexes on (customer_id), (status), (created_at) — the DB must merge indexes or can use only 1 index → still slow. A composite index matching the query pattern is always faster.

  • SELECT *

    Reading many unneeded columns → increases IO, cannot leverage an index-only scan. Index-only scan: if the query only needs columns present in the index → the DB does not need to read the heap table → much faster.

  • Large OFFSET

    LIMIT 20 OFFSET 1000000 — the DB must fetch 1,000,020 rows then discard 1,000,000. Latency grows linearly with OFFSET. Use keyset pagination instead of offset (see the Pagination subtopic).

  • Statistics outdated → wrong plan

    Postgres autovacuum is usually enough; but a large batch load (importing 1M rows) → statistics not yet updated → the optimizer picks a wrong plan. Run ANALYZE table_name after a large import.

  • Too many indexes — slow insert/update

    Every index must update when a row changes (insert, update, delete). A table with 10 indexes → writes 10 times slower. Check index usage: pg_stat_user_indexes drop unused indexes. Reindex periodically for bloated indexes.

Example
-- Query
SELECT *
FROM orders
WHERE customer_id = ?
  AND status = ?
ORDER BY created_at DESC
LIMIT 20;

-- Possible composite index
CREATE INDEX idx_orders_customer_status_created
ON orders(customer_id, status, created_at DESC);
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I do not guess at indexes. I look at the query pattern, create a suitable index, verify with explain analyze and measure again.'
Key focus
Locking, deadlock, connection pool
Concept

A lock is a mechanism so that two transactions do not modify the same data at the same time. A deadlock is two transactions waiting on each other's lock forever. A connection pool is a set of reused DB connections.

Why it exists: Locks ensure consistency but cause waiting. Opening a DB connection is expensive → the pool keeps a few connections ready for reuse and limits the number of concurrent connections to avoid bringing down the DB.

How it works
  1. A TX locks a row on UPDATE / SELECT FOR UPDATE; another TX touching that row must wait.
  2. Deadlock: TX1 holds A waiting for B, TX2 holds B waiting for A → the DB detects it and kills one side. Avoid it by locking in the same order.
  3. The app borrows a connection from the pool, returns it when done; pool exhausted → the request waits then times out.
  4. Pool exhausted is usually due to a transaction holding a connection too long (e.g. calling an external API inside the TX).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Deadlock due to different lock order: always lock rows in the same order (e.g. ascending id).
  2. Pool exhausted: a transaction holds a connection while calling an external API → separate I/O from the TX, return the connection early.
  3. SELECT FOR UPDATE too broad: locks many rows → contention. Lock the minimum.
  4. idle-in-transaction (Postgres): forgetting to commit/rollback leaves a connection hanging → set an idle TX timeout.
  5. Wrong pool size: too large brings down the DB, too small causes waiting → tune to the number of cores/connection limit of the DB.

A strong engineer must be able to debug production issues: request hanging because of a lock, pool exhausted, deadlock, slow query.

What to understand deeply
  • Optimistic lock — version field

    Every update carries WHERE version = ?. If the version has changed (a concurrent update committed), rowsAffected = 0 → throw OptimisticLockException → the application retries. No DB lock, scales well when conflicts are rare.

    @Entity
class Account {
    @Id Long id;
    @Version Long version;
    BigDecimal balance;
}
// Hibernate auto: UPDATE account SET balance=?, version=version+1
//                 WHERE id=? AND version=?
  • Pessimistic lock — SELECT FOR UPDATE

    Locks the row until commit. Another tx touching this row must wait. Suitable for critical sections like balance, inventory, sequence number. Hold the lock as briefly as possible — do not call an external API or do other work inside the tx.

    // JPA
entityManager.find(Account.class, id, LockModeType.PESSIMISTIC_WRITE);

// Or native:
SELECT * FROM account WHERE id = ? FOR UPDATE;
  • Deadlock — different lock order

    Tx A: lock orders → lock payments. Tx B: lock payments → lock orders. → Both wait on each other. The DB detects the wait cycle, aborts 1 tx (Postgres returns error 40P01). Rule: lock in a consistent order, hold locks briefly, retry the deadlock victim.

  • Connection pool — sizing is both art and science

    Too small → the app stalls (threads waiting for a connection). Too large → the DB overloads (each connection costs ~10MB RAM on the DB, plus context switching). HikariCP rule of thumb: connections = ((core_count * 2) + effective_spindle_count) on the DB side. On the app side, add the number of replicas.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • External API inside @Transactional

    The connection is acquired at the start of the tx, held until commit. Calling HTTP for 3s inside the tx → the connection is idle for 3s but still occupies a pool slot. 10 RPS × 3s = 30 connections held. The pool exhausts quickly. Pattern: external call OUTSIDE the tx, or use outbox + async.

  • Pool size too large

    App pool 200, with 5 pods → 1000 connections to the DB. Postgres default max_connections = 100. Connection refused, or DB CPU thrashing from context switching. PgBouncer (transaction pooler) helps keep the pool outside the DB.

  • Pool size too small

    5 connections × 50 RPS × 200ms query → threads wait for a connection > 10s. High latency, even though DB CPU is idle. Monitor metric: awaitingConnection > 0 = pool too small.

  • Not setting a query/transaction timeout

    A slow query of 5 minutes holds a connection for 5 minutes, blocking a deploy. Set statement_timeout at the DB level (Postgres) or @Transactional(timeout = 30) at the app level. Failing fast is better than hanging.

  • Locking multiple tables in an inconsistent order

    Method A: lock(orders, payments). Method B: lock(payments, orders). Concurrent → deadlock. Document the rule: "always lock in alphabetical order" or "always parent before child".

Example
// Optimistic locking
@Entity
class ProductStock {
    @Id
    private Long id;

    @Version
    private Long version;

    private int quantity;
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'When the pool is exhausted, I check slow queries, transaction duration, external calls inside transactions, pool metrics and DB active sessions.'
Deep dive — Database
Deadlock anatomy + diagnosis path
Time   TX A                          TX B
─────  ────────────────────────────  ────────────────────────────
t1     BEGIN
       UPDATE orders SET ...
       WHERE id = 1        (lock row orders.1)
t2                                   BEGIN
                                     UPDATE payments SET ...
                                     WHERE id = 9   (lock payments.9)
t3     UPDATE payments SET ...
       WHERE id = 9       (waits for B)
t4                                   UPDATE orders SET ...
                                     WHERE id = 1   (waits for A)
                                     ↓
                                     Wait-for graph cycle:
                                       A waits B, B waits A
t5     Postgres detector aborts B:
       ERROR: deadlock detected
       SQLSTATE 40P01

What to do:
  • App layer: catch SQLState 40P01 → exponential backoff retry
  • Design: lock resources in a consistent order across all TXs
    e.g. "always lock the lower id first"
  • Diagnose: pg_locks + pg_stat_activity to see who holds what

Diagnostic queries:
  -- who's waiting on whom right now
  SELECT blocked.pid, blocked.query AS blocked_query,
         blocking.pid AS blocking_pid, blocking.query AS blocking_query
  FROM pg_stat_activity blocked
  JOIN pg_stat_activity blocking
       ON blocking.pid = ANY(pg_blocking_pids(blocked.pid))
  WHERE blocked.wait_event_type = 'Lock';

  -- long-running transactions (often the root cause)
  SELECT pid, now() - xact_start AS tx_age, state, query
  FROM pg_stat_activity
  WHERE xact_start IS NOT NULL
  ORDER BY xact_start ASC;
War story — "the inventory oversell on a flash sale"

Service: inventory-api, MySQL backend, ~100 SKUs in a Friday 8pm flash sale.

Symptom: 14 units of a 10-unit SKU sold. Customer escalations the next morning. Finance pulled the receipts; the units never existed.

Investigation: The reservation code looked like this:

@Transactional  // isolation = READ_COMMITTED (default)
public boolean reserve(Long skuId, int qty) {
    Stock stock = stockRepo.findById(skuId).orElseThrow();
    if (stock.getQuantity() < qty) return false;
    stock.setQuantity(stock.getQuantity() - qty);
    stockRepo.save(stock);
    return true;
}

Classic lost-update. At the sale's peak, 40 concurrent reserve calls for SKU=42 (stock=10) all read quantity=10, all decided "okay, I can reserve 1," all wrote stock=9. The last writer wins; 39 reservations of nonexistent inventory committed.

Root cause: READ_COMMITTED doesn't prevent lost updates on the read-modify-write pattern. The team's mental model was "@Transactional protects me" — it doesn't; it only gives atomicity and isolation per the level, neither of which is "serializable."

Fix: Atomic conditional UPDATE — push the check into the WHERE clause:

@Modifying
@Query("update Stock s set s.quantity = s.quantity - :qty " +
       "where s.id = :id and s.quantity >= :qty")
int reserve(@Param("id") Long id, @Param("qty") int qty);
// returns rows-affected; 0 = couldn't reserve, retry or 409

This is one DB roundtrip, one row lock, no application-side compare-and-set. Throughput went up too because the transaction is shorter.

Lesson: "Default isolation level + read-then-write" is the most common concurrency bug in line-of-business code. Three fixes are valid: atomic conditional UPDATE (simplest, best for counters), optimistic locking with @Version (good for richer entities), or pessimistic SELECT FOR UPDATE (when you need to read-decide-write multiple fields). Pick one consciously; default isolation is not a fix.

Inventory reservation — three idiomatic approaches
// === A) Atomic conditional UPDATE (preferred for counters) ===
public interface StockRepository extends JpaRepository<Stock, Long> {
    @Modifying
    @Query("""
        update Stock s
           set s.quantity = s.quantity - :qty,
               s.updatedAt = :now
         where s.id = :id and s.quantity >= :qty
    """)
    int reserve(@Param("id") Long id, @Param("qty") int qty, @Param("now") Instant now);
}

@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class InventoryService {
    private final StockRepository stockRepo;
    private final Clock clock;

    @Transactional
    public ReservationResult reserve(SkuId sku, int qty) {
        int rows = stockRepo.reserve(sku.value(), qty, clock.instant());
        if (rows == 0) throw new OutOfStockException(sku);
        return ReservationResult.ok();
    }
}

// === B) Optimistic locking (when you need to read-modify-write multiple fields) ===
@Entity
public class Stock {
    @Id private Long id;
    @Version private Long version;
    private int quantity;
    private int reservedQuantity;
}

@Service @RequiredArgsConstructor
public class InventoryServiceOptimistic {
    private final StockRepository repo;

    @Retryable(retryFor = ObjectOptimisticLockingFailureException.class,
               maxAttempts = 5, backoff = @Backoff(delay = 50, multiplier = 2))
    @Transactional
    public ReservationResult reserve(SkuId sku, int qty) {
        Stock s = repo.findById(sku.value()).orElseThrow();
        if (s.getQuantity() < qty) throw new OutOfStockException(sku);
        s.setQuantity(s.getQuantity() - qty);
        s.setReservedQuantity(s.getReservedQuantity() + qty);
        // dirty checking emits UPDATE ... WHERE id=? AND version=?
        // concurrent commit → OptimisticLockException → @Retryable
        return ReservationResult.ok();
    }
}

// === C) Pessimistic (when conflict is high and retry cost outweighs lock cost) ===
public interface StockRepoPessimistic extends JpaRepository<Stock, Long> {
    @Lock(LockModeType.PESSIMISTIC_WRITE)
    @QueryHints(@QueryHint(name = "jakarta.persistence.lock.timeout", value = "3000"))
    Optional<Stock> findForUpdate(Long id);
}

@Service @RequiredArgsConstructor
public class InventoryServicePessimistic {
    private final StockRepoPessimistic repo;

    @Transactional(timeout = 5)   // belt-and-suspenders
    public ReservationResult reserve(SkuId sku, int qty) {
        Stock s = repo.findForUpdate(sku.value()).orElseThrow();
        if (s.getQuantity() < qty) throw new OutOfStockException(sku);
        s.setQuantity(s.getQuantity() - qty);
        return ReservationResult.ok();
    }
}

// Tuning rule of thumb:
//   conflict rate < 5%  → optimistic
//   conflict rate > 30% → pessimistic (retries dominate cost otherwise)
//   counter-only        → atomic UPDATE, no lock needed
Interview scenario — "Hikari pool exhausted in production"

App is slow. Hikari metric activeConnections == maxPoolSize and pendingThreads is climbing. How do you debug?
