Pool exhaustion has three usual suspects: (1) transactions holding connections too long, (2) too many concurrent requests for the pool size, (3) external calls inside transactions. I'd check them in that order because the first two are way more common than people admit.

Walk me through the first one — how do you find long-held connections?

Postgres: SELECT now() - xact_start AS age, pid, state, query FROM pg_stat_activity WHERE xact_start IS NOT NULL ORDER BY xact_start ASC. Anything over a second is suspect; anything over 10s is wrong. Then I check whether those queries are slow (need an index, missing statistics) or whether the app code is holding the TX open while doing non-DB work — which is bug #3 in my list.

Suppose you find a TX that's open for 4 seconds doing a fast query. What's the smoking gun?

It's almost always an external HTTP call inside @Transactional. The connection is acquired at TX start and not released until commit. So a 3-second downstream API call ties up a Hikari slot for 3 seconds doing nothing. At 50 RPS that's 150 connections held just for the network round trip. The fix is structural: pull the external call out of the TX boundary, or replace it with an Outbox event. I'd grep for "@Transactional" + "restTemplate\|webClient\|httpClient" in the same method.

What's the right Hikari pool size?

Smaller than people think. There's an old Hikari paper that quotes ((cores × 2) + spindles) for the DB side. For a typical Postgres on 8 cores, that's around 20-30 connections per DB. If you're running 5 app pods, that's 4-6 connections per pod, not 50. Bigger pool ≠ faster — it means more context switches at the DB, more lock contention, and at some point the DB itself becomes the bottleneck. I'd start at maxPoolSize = 10 per pod and adjust based on the awaitingConnection metric.

When would you reach for PgBouncer?

When app-side scaling gets too aggressive — say 20+ pods × 10 connections each = 200 connections, and Postgres max_connections is 100. PgBouncer in transaction mode pools connections across pods so Postgres sees ~20 connections while the app sees the same pool size locally. Caveat: transaction mode breaks features that need session state — prepared statements (use prepareThreshold=0 or text protocol), SET LOCAL, advisory locks. Document the constraints clearly or pick session-mode (less savings).

7. JPA / Hibernate

A strong engineer must understand Persistence Context, dirty checking, lazy loading, fetch strategy and transaction boundary to avoid performance bugs.

#7
Key focus
Persistence Context and Dirty Checking
Concept

The Persistence Context is the "memory area" of a transaction holding the entities being managed (managed). Dirty checking is JPA automatically detecting that an entity has changed and emitting an UPDATE on flush.

Why it exists: So you do not have to call save() manually, and so that each DB row has only one representing object within a transaction (identity map), avoiding inconsistent data.

How it works
  1. Load/save an entity → it becomes managed, residing in the persistence context.
  2. You change a field of the managed entity (setName...) — no need to call save.
  3. On flush/commit, JPA compares the original snapshot with the current state → emits an UPDATE for the changed field (dirty checking).
  4. End of transaction → the entity becomes detached; modifying it now no longer auto-persists.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. LazyInitializationException: accessing a lazy relationship outside the transaction (in the controller) → fetch within the TX or return a DTO.
  2. Unexpected UPDATE due to dirty checking: accidentally changing a managed entity field in a loop → emits redundant UPDATEs. Use readOnly when only reading.
  3. Merge overwriting newer data: merging an old detached entity overwrites a newer update → update specific fields, check version.
  4. First-level cache keeping stale data: not seeing an update from another TX within the same TX → refresh() when needed.
  5. Native query not seeing unflushed changes: adjust the flush mode or flush manually before the query.

A strong engineer needs to know when SQL is fired, why an update happens even without calling save, and why a detached entity does not auto-update.

What to understand deeply
  • Persistence Context = first-level cache within a tx

    Within the same tx, calling find(id) twice returns the SAME INSTANCE (==). Hibernate keeps a map (id → entity). Consequence: modifying an entity reflects immediately within the tx without requerying.

  • Entity states: transient, managed, detached, removed

    Transient: new Order() — no id, not in the PC. Managed: loaded from DB or persist() — tracked by Hibernate. Detached: tx ended — no longer tracked. Removed: remove() called — will DELETE on flush.

    // Managed → modify → flush automatically emits UPDATE
@Transactional
public void updateEmail(Long id, String email) {
    User u = em.find(User.class, id); // MANAGED
    u.setEmail(email);                 // no save() needed
    // commit → Hibernate compares current state vs snapshot
    //         → emit UPDATE user SET email=? WHERE id=?
}
  • Dirty checking — compare snapshot vs current

    When an entity is loaded, Hibernate clones a snapshot. At flush time, it compares each field — any differing field → creates an UPDATE. Performance: the snapshot consumes memory for entities with many fields; entities with a custom @Type may compare incorrectly.

  • Flush ≠ Commit

    Flush: synchronize PC → DB (sends SQL, no COMMIT yet). Commit: flush then COMMIT the tx. Flush may be triggered before a query so the query sees fresh data (auto-flush). You can manually call em.flush() to force it.

Production pitfalls
  • Confuse managed vs detached

    Load an entity in service A (with @Transactional) → return it to the controller. Service B receives the entity, modifies it, calls repo.save(). Hibernate detects the entity is detached → it needs a MERGE (generates UPDATE) or creates a new one (INSERT) depending on the id. The behavior is hard to predict if you do not understand it.

  • Calling save() carelessly

    Code everywhere: user = repo.findById(id); user.setX(...); repo.save(user);. save() here is unnecessary — dirty checking handles it. save() is only needed for a transient entity (newly created, not yet persisted). Careless save() adds code noise and obscures understanding.

  • Modifying an entity outside a transaction → no update

    The controller loads an entity (the repository's tx), goes outside the tx, and modifies it. In another service it has its own tx — the entity is detached, the modification does not persist. Symptom: "I called setEmail but the DB did not update" → the tx scope did not span it.

  • Not flushing in a large batch → OOM

    Inserting 100k entities in one tx without flush/clear → the PC keeps all 100k objects in memory → OOM. Pattern: flush() + clear() every 50-100 rows in a batch insert. Or use JdbcTemplate batch update to bypass Hibernate.

    for (int i = 0; i < items.size(); i++) {
    em.persist(items.get(i));
    if (i % 50 == 0) {
        em.flush();
        em.clear(); // free memory
    }
}
Example
@Transactional
public void changeEmail(Long userId, String newEmail) {
    User user = userRepository.findById(userId).orElseThrow();
    user.changeEmail(newEmail);
    // No save needed if user is managed.
    // Hibernate dirty checking will update on flush/commit.
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Within a transaction, a loaded entity is managed. Hibernate tracks changes and updates on flush/commit. save is not always needed.'
Key focus
Lazy loading and N+1
Concept

Lazy loading: a relationship (e.g. order.items) is only loaded from the DB when you access it, not loaded immediately. N+1 is a bug where you load N entities and then inadvertently run N additional child queries to load the relationship of each one.

Why it exists: Lazy avoids loading unnecessary data. But if you iterate over a list and access the lazy relationship of each element → 1 root query + N child queries = N+1, extremely slow.

How it works
  1. Query fetches N orders (1 query).
  2. The loop calls order.getItems() for each order → one query each time → N queries.
  3. Total = N+1 queries (the number one culprit for slow APIs).
  4. Fix: JOIN FETCH, @EntityGraph, or projection/DTO query to fetch everything in 1–2 queries.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. List API slow due to N+1: load N orders then call getItems() on each → JOIN FETCH/@EntityGraph.
  2. JOIN FETCH + pagination: HHH000104 warning (in-memory pagination) → split into 2 queries or use @BatchSize.
  3. EAGER everywhere: cartesian product, gigantic query → default to LAZY, fetch per use case.
  4. Many child relationships: @BatchSize(size=n) groups N child queries into ceil(N/n) IN queries.
  5. Read-heavy screen: use a DTO projection (select only the needed fields) instead of loading the whole entity graph.

N+1 is a very common question because it affects performance in production.

What to understand deeply
  • Lazy loading — proxy + on-demand query

    When loading an Order, Hibernate does NOT load items. order.getItems() returns a proxy. The first access (size(), iterate) → SELECTs items. Saves IO if unused. But causes N+1 if accessed in a loop.

  • N+1 problem

    1 query fetches N orders, then accessing getItems() in a loop → N more queries. Total = N+1. With 1000 orders → 1001 queries → latency skyrockets. Detect it by enabling spring.jpa.show-sql=true in a test.

    // Dangerous N+1
List<Order> orders = orderRepo.findRecent(); // 1 query
for (Order o : orders) {
    System.out.println(o.getItems().size()); // N queries, 1 per order
}
  • Fix N+1: JOIN FETCH, EntityGraph, batch size, DTO projection

    JOIN FETCH: 1 join query fetches everything, simple but cartesian with multiple collections. @EntityGraph: declarative, easy to reuse. hibernate.default_batch_fetch_size: groups N+1 into ceil(N/batch). DTO projection: bypasses the entity, queries straight into a DTO.

    // JOIN FETCH
@Query("select distinct o from Order o left join fetch o.items where o.createdAt >= :from")
List<Order> findRecent(LocalDateTime from);

// EntityGraph
@EntityGraph(attributePaths = {"items", "customer"})
List<Order> findByStatus(OrderStatus status);

// DTO projection - no lazy loading issue
@Query("select new com.x.OrderSummary(o.id, o.total, c.name) from Order o join o.customer c")
List<OrderSummary> listSummaries();
  • EAGER fetching by default = anti-pattern

    EAGER loads every association on each query → large, slow queries, loading everything even when not needed. Hibernate recommends defaulting to LAZY and choosing the fetch per use case. @OneToMany is LAZY by default; @ManyToOne is EAGER by default — change it to LAZY explicitly.

Production pitfalls
  • EAGER everywhere

    Order EAGER customer + items + address + history → each Order query drags 4 JOINs. Worse than small N+1 because it is always heavy even when unused. Default to LAZY, choose the fetch per use case.

  • Serializing entities directly to the API → LazyInitializationException

    The controller returns an entity. Spring Jackson serializes → accesses getItems() → the entity is already detached (the repo tx ended) → exception. Another anti-pattern: using @JsonIgnore as a workaround → the API contract gets decided by the entity structure, not by the requirements.

    Fix: return a DTO, not an entity. Map it in the service layer while the entity is still managed.

  • Not looking at the SQL log

    The code returns correct results but is slow → you do not know how many queries ran. Enable spring.jpa.show-sql=true or logging.level.org.hibernate.SQL=DEBUG. Better: use a Datasource proxy (P6Spy) to log query + parameters + actual time.

  • JOIN FETCH collection + pagination = wrong

    JOIN FETCH 1 collection + LIMIT 10 → the DB does a cartesian join then LIMITs on the joined rows → you might get 3 orders with 7 items (not 10 orders). Hibernate warns HHH000104: firstResult/maxResults specified with collection fetch; applying in memory — loads ALL of them then paginates in-memory (OOM risk).

    // Fix: 2-step query
// Step 1: paginate IDs
List<Long> ids = em.createQuery(
    "select o.id from Order o order by o.createdAt desc", Long.class)
    .setMaxResults(10).getResultList();

// Step 2: fetch items by ID
List<Order> orders = em.createQuery(
    "select o from Order o left join fetch o.items where o.id in :ids", Order.class)
    .setParameter("ids", ids).getResultList();
Example
// N+1 risk
List<Order> orders = orderRepository.findRecentOrders();
for (Order order : orders) {
    System.out.println(order.getItems().size()); // query per order
}

// Better
@Query("""
    select distinct o
    from Order o
    left join fetch o.items
    where o.createdAt >= :from
""")
List<Order> findRecentOrdersWithItems(LocalDateTime from);
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I do not expose entities directly. I use DTOs/projections and control the fetch per use case.'
Key focus
Mapping and Cascade
Concept

Mapping is how you map relationships between tables into relationships between entities (@OneToMany, @ManyToOne...). Cascade is propagating an operation (persist, remove) from a parent entity to a child entity.

Why it exists: To operate on a whole cluster of objects as a single unit (saving an Order auto-saves its OrderItems). But cascade in the wrong place can mistakenly delete data en masse.

How it works
  1. @ManyToOne is the side that "owns" the foreign key; @OneToMany(mappedBy) is the inverse side.
  2. CascadeType.PERSIST/MERGE propagates saves; CascadeType.REMOVE + orphanRemoval propagates deletes.
  3. orphanRemoval=true: removing a child from the parent collection → the child is DELETEd.
  4. Be careful with CascadeType.ALL/REMOVE on shared relationships → deleting the parent also deletes data that something else is referencing.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. CascadeType.REMOVE deleting by mistake: deleting the parent also deletes a child that something else references → only cascade within the truly owning aggregate.
  2. orphanRemoval when reassigning a collection: resetting the list causes children to be DELETEd unintentionally → use explicit add/remove operations.
  3. Bidirectional not synced both ways: setting only one side → FK null/inconsistent state. Write a helper addItem() that sets both sides.
  4. @ManyToMany with extra columns (quantity, addedAt): cannot be represented → split into an intermediate entity.
  5. EAGER + chained cascade: one operation drags loading/writing of a whole cluster unintentionally → control fetch + cascade.

A strong engineer must know cascade is dangerous if you do not understand ownership. Especially delete cascade can delete data unintentionally.

What to understand deeply
  • Owning side vs Inverse side

    The side with the foreign key column = the owning side. In @OneToMany / @ManyToOne, the @ManyToOne side is owning. mappedBy on @OneToMany points back to the field on the owning side. Only updates on the owning side cause a SQL UPDATE — two-way sync matters for correct code.

    @Entity class Order {
    @OneToMany(mappedBy = "order") // inverse
    List<OrderItem> items = new ArrayList<>();
}

@Entity class OrderItem {
    @ManyToOne   // owning - has the FK order_id
    Order order;
}
  • Cascade ≠ DB ON DELETE CASCADE

    JPA cascade: em.remove(parent) → Hibernate calls remove() on the children (generates DELETE child then DELETE parent). DB cascade: the server auto-DELETEs children when the parent is deleted. JPA cascade executes at the app layer; you still need an FK + DB cascade if deleting directly in the DB.

  • orphanRemoval — child gone from the collection ⇒ DELETE

    order.items.remove(item) → on flush, Hibernate emits DELETE item. DIFFERENT from cascade REMOVE (which only runs when removing the parent). Suitable when the child is part of the aggregate and does not exist independently.

  • ManyToMany — only use it when the link table has no attributes

    User_Role has columns (user_id, role_id, granted_at, granted_by) → do NOT use @ManyToMany. Create a UserRole entity with @ManyToOne back to User and Role. Reason: @ManyToMany does not allow adding fields to the link table.

Production pitfalls
  • CascadeType.ALL on the wrong aggregate

    Order.customer @ManyToOne cascade ALL → deleting an Order → deleting the Customer (along with that customer's other Orders). Cascade ALL is only for children belonging to the parent's lifecycle: OrderItem within Order, Address within User. Peer relationships do NOT use cascade.

  • ManyToMany for a complex domain

    UserRole has grantedAt → @ManyToMany cannot store it. You have to bypass it with an entity listener / native SQL. Or the data is wrong. Refactoring to a JoinEntity from the start is much easier.

  • Not syncing both sides of a bidirectional relationship

    Code: order.getItems().add(item) but forgetting item.setOrder(order). The item is inserted with order_id = null. Hibernate does not auto-sync — you must code a helper method order.addItem(item) that sets both sides.

    // In Order
public void addItem(OrderItem item) {
    items.add(item);
    item.setOrder(this);
}
public void removeItem(OrderItem item) {
    items.remove(item);
    item.setOrder(null);
}
  • orphanRemoval with collection reset

    order.setItems(newItems) or order.setItems(new ArrayList<>()) → Hibernate detects all old items as "orphans" → DELETEs them all. Bad if you meant to replace them another way. Modify the collection in-place instead of resetting the reference.

Example
@OneToMany(mappedBy = "order", cascade = CascadeType.ALL, orphanRemoval = true)
private List<OrderItem> items = new ArrayList<>();

public void removeItem(OrderItem item) {
    items.remove(item);
    item.detachFromOrder();
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I only use cascade when the child truly depends on the aggregate root's lifecycle, for example OrderItem belonging to Order.'
Deep dive — JPA / Hibernate
Entity lifecycle + N+1 trap
Entity states (within a Persistence Context)

    new Order()
        │ persist()
        ▼
   ┌─────────────┐   load via find() / JPQL
   │   MANAGED   │◄────────────────────────  detached entity + merge()
   └──────┬──────┘
          │  field mutation → tracked by dirty checking
          │  flush() → UPDATE statement
          │
          │  em.close() / TX ends
          ▼
   ┌─────────────┐
   │  DETACHED   │   mutations no longer tracked!
   └─────────────┘

          remove() while MANAGED
          ▼
   ┌─────────────┐  DELETE issued on flush
   │   REMOVED   │
   └─────────────┘


N+1 anatomy:
  List<Order> orders = repo.findAll();      ── 1 query
  for (Order o : orders) {
      o.getItems().size();                  ── N queries (lazy proxy hit)
  }                                            (one per order)
  Total: 1 + N

Fix matrix:
                       ┌─ small N, simple object ─► JOIN FETCH
  Need full entity? ───┤
                       └─ pagination required    ─► 2-step query
                                                    (IDs first, fetch second)

                       ┌─ list/feed page         ─► DTO projection
  Read-only output?  ──┤
                       └─ analytics              ─► native SQL / view

                       ┌─ unpredictable access   ─► default_batch_fetch_size
  Mixed read pattern? ─┤
                       └─ predictable            ─► @EntityGraph per use case
War story — "the dashboard that took 47 seconds"

Service: admin-dashboard, list view showing 50 orders with their line items and customer.

Symptom: Page took 47 seconds in production for the largest tenant. Localhost was <1 second. The team blamed "network" for two sprints.

Investigation: Enabled spring.jpa.show-sql=true for one trace. Output: 1,503 queries for one page load. The endpoint was a clean Spring Data findAll(Pageable) on Order; the JSON serializer touched order.getItems() and order.getCustomer() for each Order, each triggering a lazy load. 50 orders × (1 items query + 1 customer query) + a couple of nested associations = 1,500+ queries. Each query was fast (1ms), but 1,500 round trips over a transatlantic connection × 30ms RTT = the missing 45 seconds.

Root cause: Returning entities directly to the JSON serializer. Lazy loading triggered after the transaction closed in some paths — those would have crashed with LazyInitializationException. To avoid the crash, someone had wrapped the controller in @Transactional, which "fixed" the exception but turned every page load into a query storm.

Fix: DTO projection. Replaced the entity return with a constructor expression query that JOINs orders, items, and customers and returns a flat OrderListItemDto. One query. Page load dropped from 47s to 180ms. The "@Transactional on controller" anti-pattern got removed in the same PR.

Lesson: "It works on localhost" means "the latency cost was hidden by 0.1ms loopback." For any read API, default to DTO projection. Entities are for writes; DTOs are for reads. The moment a controller returns an entity directly, you've given up control of what queries fire.

Read API done right: DTO projection + 2-step pagination
// DTO — designed for the response shape, decoupled from entity
public record OrderListItemDto(
        UUID orderId,
        String customerName,
        BigDecimal total,
        OrderStatus status,
        Instant createdAt,
        int itemCount) {}

// 1) Pagination-safe query: don't JOIN FETCH a collection with LIMIT
// Step 1: page the parent IDs
public interface OrderRepository extends JpaRepository<Order, UUID> {

    @Query("""
        select o.id from Order o
         where o.tenantId = :tenant
           and o.createdAt >= :from
         order by o.createdAt desc, o.id desc
    """)
    Page<UUID> pageIds(@Param("tenant") UUID tenantId,
                       @Param("from") Instant from,
                       Pageable pageable);

    // Step 2: hydrate with fetches in one shot
    @Query("""
        select distinct o from Order o
        left join fetch o.items i
        left join fetch o.customer c
        where o.id in :ids
        order by o.createdAt desc, o.id desc
    """)
    List<Order> findWithItemsByIdIn(@Param("ids") List<UUID> ids);
}

// 2) For list endpoints, prefer flat DTO projection (no lazy traps)
public interface OrderQueryRepository {
    Page<OrderListItemDto> listForTenant(UUID tenantId, Instant from, Pageable p);
}

@Repository
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class OrderQueryRepositoryImpl implements OrderQueryRepository {
    private final JdbcTemplate jdbc;
    private final NamedParameterJdbcTemplate named;

    @Override
    public Page<OrderListItemDto> listForTenant(UUID tenantId, Instant from, Pageable p) {
        Integer total = named.queryForObject("""
            select count(*) from orders
             where tenant_id = :t and created_at >= :f
        """, Map.of("t", tenantId, "f", Timestamp.from(from)), Integer.class);

        List<OrderListItemDto> rows = named.query("""
            select o.id, c.name as customer_name, o.total, o.status, o.created_at,
                   (select count(*) from order_item oi where oi.order_id = o.id) as item_count
              from orders o
              join customer c on c.id = o.customer_id
             where o.tenant_id = :t and o.created_at >= :f
             order by o.created_at desc, o.id desc
             limit :limit offset :offset
        """, Map.of("t", tenantId, "f", Timestamp.from(from),
                    "limit", p.getPageSize(), "offset", p.getOffset()),
        (rs, n) -> new OrderListItemDto(
                rs.getObject("id", UUID.class),
                rs.getString("customer_name"),
                rs.getBigDecimal("total"),
                OrderStatus.valueOf(rs.getString("status")),
                rs.getTimestamp("created_at").toInstant(),
                rs.getInt("item_count")));

        return new PageImpl<>(rows, p, total == null ? 0 : total);
    }
}

// 3) For write paths, work with managed entities inside one TX
@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class CancelOrderUseCase {
    private final OrderRepository repo;
    private final DomainEventPublisher events;

    @Transactional
    public void execute(UUID orderId, String reason, UserId actor) {
        Order o = repo.findById(orderId).orElseThrow(OrderNotFound::new);
        o.cancel(reason, actor);                  // domain method, enforces invariants
        // No save() needed — dirty checking handles UPDATE on commit
        events.publish(new OrderCancelled(o.id(), reason, Instant.now()));
    }
}
Interview scenario — "diagnose N+1 in production"

A teammate reports that GET /orders got slow after a recent change. What's your debugging approach?

First I'd quantify it: what's the change in p50/p95/p99? If only p95+ changed, it's probably a data-size issue (some users have a lot of orders); if all percentiles regressed equally, it's likely an introduced query pattern. Then I'd turn on SQL logging in staging for that endpoint — not show-sql, which spams stdout, but the Hibernate statistics MBean or P6Spy to see the exact count of queries per request. If I see "1 + N" pattern, I have my answer.

Suppose it's N+1. What's the simplest fix?

Depends on what changed. If someone added a new association access in a serializer or a Jackson mixin, the fix is usually @EntityGraph on the repository method — declarative and reusable. If the endpoint shouldn't be touching the association at all, the right fix is to return a DTO that doesn't include it. join fetch in JPQL works but I avoid it when pagination is involved — collection fetch with LIMIT is silently broken; Hibernate logs HHH000104 and falls back to in-memory pagination, which is even worse than N+1 for large result sets.

Why does join fetch break with pagination?

A LEFT JOIN to a collection multiplies rows. LIMIT 10 in SQL truncates the joined result, not distinct parents. You end up with 3 orders' worth of rows instead of 10 orders. Hibernate detects this and warns, then loads all matching parents and paginates in JVM heap. On a large table, that's an OOM waiting to happen. The clean fix is the two-step query: page the IDs first, then fetch the full graph for that ID set.

When do you reach for default_batch_fetch_size?

For unpredictable access patterns where I don't want to define a graph per use case. With batch_fetch_size = 50, lazy loading a collection from 100 parents fires 2 queries instead of 100. It's a safety net more than a design choice — I prefer explicit @EntityGraph for hot paths because it documents intent. Batch fetch is the "won't make things catastrophically bad" fallback.

When is JPA the wrong tool entirely?

Bulk operations and complex reporting. Inserting 100,000 rows with managed entities means 100,000 snapshots in the Persistence Context — you'll OOM unless you flush+clear in batches, and even then it's slower than a JDBC batch by 5-10×. For analytics, the moment you need window functions, CTEs, or grouping sets, write SQL. JPA's strength is the write path for transactional aggregates — outside that, drop down to JDBC or jOOQ. Knowing when not to use the tool is part of being advanced with it.

8. Docker / Kubernetes / Cloud-native

Engineers must understand how the app runs in production: container, health check, resource limit, scaling, logs and troubleshooting.

#8
Key focus
Production Dockerfile
Concept

Docker packages the app + every dependency into a single image that runs the same everywhere. The Dockerfile is the recipe to build the image. A multi-stage build separates the build step and the run step into distinct stages.

Why it exists: So that "works on my machine" = "works everywhere". Multi-stage keeps the final image small, free of the build toolchain → lightweight, fast startup, fewer security vulnerabilities.

How it works
  1. Build stage: use an image with JDK + Maven to compile the jar.
  2. Runtime stage: use a small JRE/jlink image, COPY only the needed artifact from the build stage.
  3. Run as a non-root user; split the dependency layer to take advantage of the cache.
  4. The final image contains only runtime + app → many times smaller than the build image.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Huge image (>1GB): multi-stage + slim JRE/jlink + .dockerignore.
  2. Running as root: security risk → create and use a non-root user.
  3. Broken layer cache: COPY . . before the build invalidates the dependency cache → copy pom.xml and download dependencies first.
  4. Baking secrets into the image: the secret leaks through a layer → use env/secret manager.
  5. The latest tag: non-reproducible deploys, hard to roll back → pin version/digest.

An experienced engineer does not just build an image that runs. They must optimize size, security, startup, config and reproducibility.

What to understand deeply
  • Multi-stage build — small and clean image

    Stage 1: jdk + maven image builds the jar. Stage 2: lightweight jre image + copy the jar. Final image ~200MB instead of ~1GB. No source, build cache, or test artifacts included. Reduces attack surface and push/pull time.

  • Non-root user

    By default a container runs as root → compromising the container = compromising root. Create user 1001 or use a distroless base image. Comply with the Pod Security Standards in Kubernetes (restricted PSA).

    FROM eclipse-temurin:21-jre
RUN useradd -r -u 1001 -g root appuser
USER 1001
COPY --chown=1001:0 app.jar /app/app.jar
ENTRYPOINT ["java", "-jar", "/app/app.jar"]
  • Externalized config — 12-factor app

    Immutable image, deploy 1 image for every env (dev/staging/prod). Config via ENV var, K8s ConfigMap/Secret. DO NOT bake credentials into the image (an image pushed to a registry = a leak).

  • Clear image tags

    Use the git SHA (app:a1b2c3d) or semver (app:1.2.3). latest = you don't know which version is deployed → no deterministic rollback. The CI build must push SHA + environment (app:1.2.3-prod-a1b2c3d).

  • Container-aware JVM

    Java 10+ automatically detects the cgroup limit. Use -XX:MaxRAMPercentage=75 instead of a fixed -Xmx512m → it auto-scales with the container memory limit. The heap is usually 60-80% of the container limit, leaving a buffer for native memory and metaspace.

    ENTRYPOINT ["java", \
  "-XX:MaxRAMPercentage=75.0", \
  "-XX:+UseG1GC", \
  "-XX:+ExitOnOutOfMemoryError", \
  "-jar", "/app/app.jar"]
Production issue / pitfall
  • Unreasonably large image

    COPY . . in the Dockerfile → pulls in the whole .git, target/, node_modules, test resources. A 2GB image. Fix: use .dockerignore (like .gitignore) to exclude unneeded files. Or multi-stage: the build stage has the full source, the runtime stage only copies the jar.

  • Hardcoded config in the image

    application.yml containing the prod DB password → each env builds its own image. Not reusable. Worse: the image pushed to a public registry → credential leak. Externalize: ENV var, K8s Secret mounted as a file/env.

  • latest tag in production

    A K8s rolling update re-pulls the image because of the "latest" tag → it may pull an unintentionally newer version (due to a CI rebuild). "kubectl rollout undo" has no knowledge of the old version. Always pin the image by SHA digest or an immutable tag.

  • JVM not respecting container memory

    Java < 10 is not aware of cgroups. -Xmx hardcoded to 2GB but the container limit is 1GB → the kernel OOMKills the container. Or Java 8 uses a default heap = 25% of physical → it may exceed the limit. Upgrade to a newer Java and use MaxRAMPercentage.

Example
FROM eclipse-temurin:21-jdk AS build
WORKDIR /app
COPY . .
RUN ./mvnw -DskipTests package

FROM eclipse-temurin:21-jre
WORKDIR /app
COPY --from=build /app/target/app.jar app.jar
EXPOSE 8080
ENTRYPOINT ["java", "-XX:MaxRAMPercentage=75", "-jar", "app.jar"]
Interview answer tip: Say: 'A production Dockerfile needs to be repeatable, small, secure and have externalized config.'
Key focus
Kubernetes probes and resources
Concept

In Kubernetes, a probe is how K8s checks a pod's health: liveness (is it still alive), readiness (is it ready to receive traffic), startup (has it finished booting). Resource request/limit declare the CPU/RAM the pod needs and its maximum level.

Why it exists: So K8s can auto-restart a hung pod, stop sending traffic to a pod that isn't ready, and schedule pods properly. Misconfiguration → pods get killed unfairly or receive traffic before they are ready.

How it works
  1. Liveness fail → K8s restarts the pod (used for deadlock/hang).
  2. Readiness fail → K8s removes the pod from the load balancer but does NOT restart it (used when busy/booting).
  3. The startup probe protects a slow-starting app from being killed early by liveness.
  4. request = the guaranteed level for the scheduler to place the pod; limit = the ceiling, exceeding the RAM limit → OOMKilled.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Liveness calling the DB: a slow DB makes K8s kill the pod unfairly → cascading restart. Liveness should only check the process; readiness is where you check the dependency.
  2. Missing readiness: traffic reaching a pod that hasn't warmed up → 500s during deploy/scale.
  3. A slow-starting app killed early by liveness: use startupProbe to protect the boot phase.
  4. No memory limit set: the pod eats all the node's RAM → noisy neighbor; set too low → OOMKilled.
  5. CPU limit too low: throttling → latency increases. Measure for real then set a reasonable request/limit.
livenessProbe:        # alive? -> restart if it fails. Do NOT check the DB.
  httpGet: { path: /health/live, port: 8080 }
  initialDelaySeconds: 10
readinessProbe:       # ready to receive traffic? -> check the dependency
  httpGet: { path: /health/ready, port: 8080 }
startupProbe:         # protect a slow-booting app from liveness
  httpGet: { path: /health/live, port: 8080 }
  failureThreshold: 30
  periodSeconds: 5

An experienced engineer must know that wrong readiness/liveness can cause an outage even when the code has no bug.

What to understand deeply
  • Readiness — gate traffic

    Fail → the pod is removed from the Service endpoints (receives no requests). Pass again → re-added. Used to: warm the cache, wait for the DB to be ready, wait for Kafka to connect. Does not restart the pod. Spring Boot Actuator: /actuator/health/readiness.

  • Liveness — restart behavior

    Fail (after N retries) → kubelet kills and restarts the container. Use only for a "deadlock" or an unrecoverable state. The liveness check must be simple: thread alive, not hung. Do NOT check the DB in liveness.

    readinessProbe:
  httpGet: { path: /actuator/health/readiness, port: 8080 }
  initialDelaySeconds: 10
  periodSeconds: 5
  failureThreshold: 3
livenessProbe:
  httpGet: { path: /actuator/health/liveness, port: 8080 }
  initialDelaySeconds: 60
  periodSeconds: 20
  failureThreshold: 6
  • Startup probe — slow-starting app

    Java Spring Boot has a 30-60s startup. If liveness initialDelay is 60s → a pod that hasn't started within 60s will be caught in a kill loop. The startup probe disables liveness/readiness until it passes. Only then do liveness/readiness become active.

  • Resource request vs limit

    Request: the scheduler uses it to pick a node with enough resources. The pod is "reserved" this amount. Limit: the ceiling. Exceeding the memory limit → OOMKilled (exit 137). Exceeding the CPU limit → throttled (slow, not killed). Set request = limit for memory to avoid overcommit.

  • OOMKilled vs CPU throttling

    OOMKilled is a hard kill, the container exits 137, K8s restarts the pod. CPU throttling is soft, the app still runs but slowly → P99 latency rises silently. Monitor container_cpu_cfs_throttled_seconds_total.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Liveness check depending on the DB — cascading outage

    The liveness endpoint checks the DB connection. The DB is slow for 5s → liveness fails on all pods → K8s kills every replica → they all restart at once → the DB gets even more overloaded. Liveness MUST be simple: the process being alive is a pass. The DB check belongs to readiness.

  • Readiness passing too early

    The app just started, JIT not warm, classloader not cached → traffic comes in → 5xx or very high latency. Set initialDelaySeconds large enough, or have readiness check include warm-up logic (preload cache, JIT-compile the critical path).

  • Not setting request/limit

    The pod takes up all the node's CPU → "noisy neighbor" slowing down other pods. When the node is full with no headroom → K8s evicts a random pod. Always set request/limit, especially memory.

  • CrashLoopBackOff without looking at events

    You only look at the app log, but the pod hasn't even started. The reason is usually in a kubelet event: image pull failure, missing secret, probe failure, OOM. Debug command: kubectl describe pod <name> to see the Events section at the bottom.

Example
readinessProbe:
  httpGet:
    path: /actuator/health/readiness
    port: 8080
  initialDelaySeconds: 20
  periodSeconds: 10

livenessProbe:
  httpGet:
    path: /actuator/health/liveness
    port: 8080
  initialDelaySeconds: 60
  periodSeconds: 20
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Liveness should only check that the app process is alive. Readiness is where you check the ability to receive traffic and the critical dependencies.'
Key focus
Production troubleshooting
Concept

Production troubleshooting on K8s is the process of diagnosing when a pod isn't running correctly: reading status, logs, events and metrics to find the root cause (CrashLoopBackOff, OOMKilled, ImagePullBackOff...).

Why it exists: When a service goes down at 2 in the morning, knowing the right commands to type and the right signals to read helps you recover fast instead of guessing.

How it works
  1. kubectl get pods → see the status (CrashLoopBackOff, Pending, OOMKilled...).
  2. kubectl describe pod → read the Events (why it was killed, schedule failure, image pull failure).
  3. kubectl logs --previous → see the log of the crashed container.
  4. Cross-reference metrics (CPU/RAM/restart count) + readiness to narrow it down: code, resource, or dependency.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. CrashLoopBackOff: kubectl logs --previous + describe Events to learn the exit reason.
  2. OOMKilled: raise the memory limit / fix the leak / adjust -Xmx to the container (use MaxRAMPercentage).
  3. ImagePullBackOff: wrong tag or missing registry auth → check the image name + imagePullSecret.
  4. Pod Pending: insufficient resources / nodeSelector / unbound PVC → describe to see the scheduling reason.
  5. Readiness failing forever: a dependency (DB/Kafka) isn't ready → check what the readiness endpoint calls.
kubectl get pods                      # status: CrashLoopBackOff/Pending/OOMKilled
kubectl describe pod <name>            # Events: why it was killed / not scheduled
kubectl logs <name> --previous         # log of the crashed container
kubectl top pod <name>                 # actual CPU/RAM (vs the limit)

An experienced engineer must know that when a service dies/slows down you check in order, not guess.

What to understand deeply
  • Step 1: Pod status + events

    kubectl get pods to see the status (Pending, Running, CrashLoopBackOff). kubectl describe pod <name> to see the Events at the bottom — this is the kubelet/scheduler log: image pull failure, schedule failure, probe failure, OOMKilled. Events are the first source of truth.

    kubectl get pods -n myns
kubectl describe pod myapp-7f8c9-xyz
kubectl logs myapp-7f8c9-xyz --previous   # log of the container that died before the restart
kubectl get events --sort-by=.lastTimestamp -n myns
  • Step 2: Resource (actual CPU/mem)

    kubectl top pod to see current CPU/memory usage. Compare with the limit to know if it's near OOM or throttled. A high restart count = the pod is looping. exit code 137 = OOMKilled, 143 = SIGTERM (graceful), 1 = app exception.

  • Step 3: Downstream dependency

    Is the app failing because of the DB? Is Kafka lag rising? Is an external API timing out? Check the connection pool metric: hikaricp.connections.usage > 80% = the pool is full. Kafka consumer lag > 10k = not consuming fast enough. Trace 1 failing request to see which step fails.

  • Step 4: Metrics + tracing

    Grafana dashboard: latency p95/p99, error rate, throughput, saturation (CPU, mem, pool). A latency spike must have a cause in one of the 4 areas. Tracing (Jaeger/Tempo) to see the request flow and the bottleneck step.

  • Step 5: Trace correlation id end-to-end

    Take 1 failing request with an x-request-id, search the ELK/Loki logs of ALL services. You can see where the request went, where it failed, and why. No correlation id → you cannot debug a distributed system.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Only looking at the app log, ignoring kubelet events

    The app log is empty/has no error but the pod restarts continuously. The real reason: K8s OOMKill (info in the event) or the node being evicted. Always check kubectl describe pod + kubectl get events before digging into the app log.

  • Not realizing the container was OOMKilled

    The app log stops midway, no stack trace. Status Last State: Terminated, Reason: OOMKilled, Exit Code: 137. Symptom: memory usage climbing toward the limit then the pod restarts. Fix: raise the limit, or find the memory leak (heap dump).

  • Scaling pods when the bottleneck is the DB

    Latency rises → scale pods x2 → DB connections x2 → DB CPU 100% → everything gets slower. You must identify the bottleneck (Grafana saturation panel) before scaling. Scaling up doesn't mean a rescue; sometimes it makes things worse.

  • No correlation id, unstructured logs

    A log like "Error processing request" with no context. When support needs to trace 1 user → it can't be found. Set the MDC from a filter, log JSON, forward x-request-id to downstream calls. A necessary standard for every production service.

Example
kubectl get pods
kubectl describe pod <pod>
kubectl logs <pod> --previous
kubectl top pod
kubectl get events --sort-by=.lastTimestamp
Interview answer tip: Answer following the flow: symptom → metrics/logs/events → isolate dependency → mitigate → root cause → prevent recurrence.
Deep dive — Docker / Kubernetes
Pod lifecycle + probe interplay
kubelet:                       container:
                               │
1. pullImage                   │  ── image pulled, signature verified
2. createContainer             │
3. start                       ▼
                               JVM boot ──────────────► startupProbe begins
                                                          ├─ ✓ pass → handoff to readiness
                                                          └─ ✗ fail × N → kill, restart
                               │
                               │  Spring context loading (5-30s)
                               │
4. startupProbe ok             │  ── readinessProbe & livenessProbe activate
                               │
5. readinessProbe loop ──────► HTTP GET /actuator/health/readiness
                                  ├─ checks: DB, Kafka, cache
                                  ├─ ✓ pod added to Service endpoints
                                  └─ ✗ pod removed from endpoints  (NO restart)
                               │
6. livenessProbe loop ───────► HTTP GET /actuator/health/liveness
                                  ├─ "is the JVM responsive?"
                                  ├─ ✓ nothing
                                  └─ ✗ × failureThreshold → kill + restart

Termination (rolling update or pod delete):
   ─ K8s sends SIGTERM
   ─ kubelet removes pod from Service endpoints (some delay!)
   ─ preStop hook runs (sleep 10s → bleed in-flight traffic)
   ─ container has terminationGracePeriodSeconds to drain
   ─ if alive at end → SIGKILL

Failure modes mapped to fixes:
   CrashLoopBackOff       → check `describe pod` events first
   OOMKilled (exit 137)   → raise memory limit OR fix leak
   probe failures         → liveness too strict, or startup too aggressive
   503 mid-deploy         → no preStop, no readiness gate
War story — "the cascading restart"

Service: A 20-replica order-api, Spring Boot, Postgres backend.

Symptom: 03:14 Sunday, every replica restarted within 2 minutes. Three minutes of full outage. Restarts continued in waves for 40 minutes.

Investigation: Postgres had a brief CPU spike — autovacuum on a large table while the app was idle. That made simple queries slow for ~30 seconds. The app's liveness probe called /actuator/health, which included a DB connectivity check. Slow DB → probe timed out → kubelet killed pod → pod restarted → reconnected to DB → repeat. All 20 replicas hit the same condition within the same probe window. Cold-start storm hit Postgres with 20× new connections at once, prolonging the slow window, prolonging the restarts.

Root cause: Liveness probe checked DB. Liveness is supposed to be "is the process alive" — DB problems are a readiness concern (don't send me traffic) not a liveness concern (kill me). The shared /actuator/health endpoint conflated the two.

Fix: Split the probes. Liveness: /actuator/health/liveness — Spring Boot's built-in liveness group, just "is the application context running." Readiness: /actuator/health/readiness — includes DB, Kafka, downstream APIs. Also added terminationGracePeriodSeconds: 30 and a preStop sleep of 10s so removal from Service endpoints could propagate before SIGTERM. And: PgBouncer in front of Postgres to absorb cold-start connection storms.

Lesson: Liveness probes that check dependencies turn local issues into cluster-wide self-DDOS. The probe types exist for a reason; conflating them creates failure modes that don't exist in either type individually. A good liveness probe is boring — it almost never fails. A good readiness probe is honest — it fails immediately when serving traffic would harm users, and recovers when the world is okay again.

Production K8s manifest + Spring Boot health groups
# Deployment manifest — production hardened
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata: { name: order-api }
spec:
  replicas: 3
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate: { maxUnavailable: 0, maxSurge: 1 }   # no-downtime updates
  template:
    spec:
      terminationGracePeriodSeconds: 30
      securityContext:
        runAsNonRoot: true
        runAsUser: 1001
        fsGroup: 1001
        seccompProfile: { type: RuntimeDefault }
      containers:
        - name: app
          image: registry.example.com/order-api:1.4.2-a1b2c3d   # SHA-pinned
          imagePullPolicy: IfNotPresent
          ports: [{ containerPort: 8080, name: http }]
          env:
            - { name: JAVA_TOOL_OPTIONS, value: "-XX:MaxRAMPercentage=75 -XX:+ExitOnOutOfMemoryError -XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError -XX:HeapDumpPath=/var/log/heapdump" }
            - { name: SPRING_PROFILES_ACTIVE, value: "prod" }
            - name: DB_PASSWORD
              valueFrom: { secretKeyRef: { name: order-api-secrets, key: db-password } }
          resources:
            requests: { cpu: "500m", memory: "768Mi" }
            limits:   { cpu: "2",    memory: "768Mi" }     # memory: request == limit (no overcommit)
          startupProbe:
            httpGet: { path: /actuator/health/liveness, port: http }
            periodSeconds: 5
            failureThreshold: 24                  # gives 120s for cold start
          livenessProbe:
            httpGet: { path: /actuator/health/liveness, port: http }
            periodSeconds: 10
            timeoutSeconds: 3
            failureThreshold: 6                   # 1 minute before kill
          readinessProbe:
            httpGet: { path: /actuator/health/readiness, port: http }
            periodSeconds: 5
            timeoutSeconds: 3
            failureThreshold: 3
          lifecycle:
            preStop:
              exec: { command: ["/bin/sh", "-c", "sleep 10"] }   # bleed traffic
          volumeMounts:
            - { name: heapdump, mountPath: /var/log/heapdump }
            - { name: tmp, mountPath: /tmp }
          securityContext:
            allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
            readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
            capabilities: { drop: ["ALL"] }
      volumes:
        - { name: heapdump, emptyDir: {} }
        - { name: tmp, emptyDir: {} }
---
# PodDisruptionBudget — voluntary disruptions stay safe
apiVersion: policy/v1
kind: PodDisruptionBudget
metadata: { name: order-api }
spec:
  minAvailable: 2
  selector: { matchLabels: { app: order-api } }
---
# Spring Boot application.yml
management:
  endpoint.health:
    probes.enabled: true
    show-details: never
    group:
      liveness:
        include: [ livenessState ]               # just "Spring context alive"
      readiness:
        include: [ readinessState, db, kafka ]   # gate traffic on these
  endpoints.web.exposure.include: health,info,prometheus

# Graceful shutdown
server.shutdown: graceful
spring.lifecycle.timeout-per-shutdown-phase: 25s
Interview scenario — "pod is CrashLoopBackOff in production"

A pod is in CrashLoopBackOff. Walk me through your first 5 minutes.

First command, always: kubectl describe pod <name>. I read the Events section at the bottom before I look at any application log. Events tell me whether the pod even got scheduled, whether the image pulled, whether the probes fired, and the container's last exit code. 90% of the time the answer is already there.

What exit codes do you care about?

137 means OOMKilled — kernel killed the container for exceeding memory limit. That's a memory problem, not a code problem; either the limit is too low or there's a leak. 143 means SIGTERM accepted cleanly. 1 or 2 mean app error — that's when I switch to kubectl logs --previous to see the stack trace from the dying container. If I see no exit code and just "Back-off restarting failed container," it's usually a probe issue — pod is starting but failing readiness immediately.

You see OOMKilled. What do you do?

Two questions: is it a leak or a misconfiguration? Misconfig is easier — JVM doesn't respect cgroup limits on old Java, or -Xmx is hardcoded higher than the limit. Quick check: log Runtime.getRuntime().maxMemory() at startup against cat /sys/fs/cgroup/memory.max. If they don't agree, upgrade Java or use -XX:MaxRAMPercentage. If they agree and OOM still happens, it's a leak. I'd take a heap dump on next OOM (-XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError -XX:HeapDumpPath to an emptyDir volume), then analyze in Eclipse MAT.

Pod is healthy but customers report timeouts. What probes do you set?

Three probes, each with a different job. Startup probe — gives the JVM time to boot without liveness killing it. failureThreshold × periodSeconds ≥ worst-case cold start. Liveness — pure "is the JVM responsive" check, no DB, no downstream. Never. Readiness — includes the dependencies that, if broken, mean traffic to this pod will fail. Spring Boot 2.3+ has dedicated /health/liveness and /health/readiness endpoints that separate these correctly out of the box.

Why "never check DB in liveness"?

Because a brief DB issue becomes a cluster-wide restart storm. Every pod's liveness probe fails simultaneously, kubelet kills them all, pods restart and reconnect at the same moment, hit the DB with a connection storm that prolongs the original issue. I've seen this exact failure mode produce a 40-minute outage from a 30-second DB hiccup. Liveness should fail only when the JVM itself is broken — deadlock, OOM degenerate state — not when an external dependency is misbehaving.

9. Cloud Basics for Java

A Java engineer doesn't need to know every cloud service, but must understand how an app is deployed, secured, scaled and observed on the cloud.

#9
Key focus
Compute choice: VM, Container, Kubernetes, Lambda
Concept

On the cloud there are many places to run code: VM (virtual machine), Container (Docker on ECS/Cloud Run), Kubernetes (orchestrating containers at large scale), Serverless/Lambda (runs per request, no server management).

Why it exists: Each choice trades off control, operational cost and elasticity. Choose wrong → you pay too much or carry unnecessary operations.

How it works
  1. VM: maximum control but you handle OS/patch/scale yourself — fits legacy or specialized workloads.
  2. Container (managed): consistent packaging, easy to scale, less operations than a VM.
  3. Kubernetes: powerful for many microservices but complex to operate.
  4. Lambda: pay per invocation, auto-scales to 0, but has cold start and time limits — fits event/spiky workloads.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Spiky/event-driven workload: Lambda auto-scales, pays per use; cold start is latency-sensitive → provisioned concurrency.
  2. Long-running/stateful tasks: not a fit for Lambda (~15 minute limit) → container/VM.
  3. Many microservices needing orchestration: Kubernetes; if few services → managed container (Cloud Run/ECS Fargate) saves operations.
  4. Legacy needing OS control: VM.
  5. Cost optimization: idle services should scale-to-zero (Lambda/Cloud Run); stable traffic → reserved/committed-use is cheaper than on-demand.
// Quick decision framework
spiky + short (< 15m)        -> Lambda (scale-to-zero, pay per request)
HTTP service, low ops       -> Cloud Run / ECS Fargate (managed container)
many services, high control -> Kubernetes
stateful / legacy / GPU     -> VM

A strong engineer must choose compute based on workload, not based on trend.

What to understand deeply
  • VM / EC2 — full control, full responsibility

    You install the OS, patch security, monitor disk, handle HA. Fits legacy apps that are hard to containerize, apps that need kernel tweaks, or very isolated environments. High operational cost. Should not be used for new services if there is another option.

  • ECS / EKS / Kubernetes — container orchestration

    Fits long-running services that need auto-scale, service discovery, network policy. Network and resource model are built in. EKS = managed Kubernetes (control plane handled by AWS), ECS = AWS proprietary, simpler but with vendor lock-in.

  • Lambda — event-driven, automatic scaling

    Pay per invocation + execution time. No server management. Cold start 100ms-2s (heavier with Spring), max timeout 15 minutes, max memory 10GB. Fits: image resize on S3 upload, webhook handler, short scheduled jobs.

    Cold start reference:
- Node.js plain: ~100-200ms
- Java + Spring Boot: 2-5s (Lambda SnapStart helps reduce)
- Python: ~200-500ms

For latency-sensitive Java workloads, consider:
- SnapStart (Lambda) or native image (GraalVM)
- Or use container + Fargate/ECS
  • K8s Job / AWS Batch — long-running workloads

    Jobs that run many minutes to hours, need large memory/CPU, batch processing. K8s Job defines resource, restart policy, parallelism. AWS Batch manages queue + compute environment that scales with demand.

Production pitfall
  • Lambda for a job that exceeds the limit

    PDF generation 20 minutes → Lambda times out after 15 minutes → job fails midway + may be retried → multiple partial results. Or uploading a 500MB file → Lambda memory is not enough. Pick K8s Job, Fargate, or Step Functions with split work.

  • Kubernetes for a small app

    1 service at 10 RPS → Kubernetes is overkill, large operational overhead (cluster, monitoring, upgrade). For a small app pick App Engine, Cloud Run, Elastic Beanstalk, Heroku-style. K8s when you have many services / need fine-grained control.

  • Not accounting for cold start in the SLA

    A Lambda function with little traffic → a new invocation hits a 3s cold start. P99 latency spikes. Not a fit for customer-facing endpoints. Workaround: provisioned concurrency, or SnapStart (Java), or use a container service.

  • Not accounting for event retry/idempotency

    S3 event → SQS → Lambda. SQS at-least-once → Lambda may receive a duplicate event. If Lambda sends an email → the email is sent twice. Idempotency key at Lambda or DynamoDB conditional write.

Example
PDF generation example:
- Few small files on upload event: Lambda may be good.
- Thousands of PDFs, long runtime, high memory: Kubernetes Job may be safer.
- Continuous stream tasks: Kafka consumer on Kubernetes with KEDA.
Interview answer tip: State the trade-offs: runtime duration, cost, ops effort, scaling pattern, timeout, memory, retry behavior.
Key focus
S3/Object Storage, presigned URL, security
Concept

Object Storage (S3) stores files (images, documents, backups) as an object in a bucket, accessed via API rather than a filesystem. A presigned URL is a signed URL that allows direct upload/download within a limited time window without exposing credentials.

Why it exists: Large files should not go through the app server (it wastes bandwidth/RAM). The bucket should be private; a presigned URL lets the client interact directly with S3 in a safe way, with a time limit.

How it works
  1. Keep the bucket private; block public access.
  2. The app signs a presigned URL (short TTL) using its credential/IAM role.
  3. The client uses that URL to upload/download straight to S3, not through the app server.
  4. TTL expires → URL is invalidated; grant minimal permission via IAM role, do not hardcode keys.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Uploading a large file through the app server: wastes RAM/bandwidth, prone to timeout → presigned PUT, client uploads straight to S3.
  2. Bucket left public by mistake: data leak → enable Block Public Access, keep it private.
  3. Hardcoded access key: easy to leak → use IAM role + STS, no static keys.
  4. Presigned URL TTL too long: shared and abused → short TTL + content-type/size constraints.
  5. Letting users download a private file: time-limited presigned GET, do not proxy the file through the app server.
// App signs a presigned URL (short TTL) - client uploads directly, not through the server
PutObjectRequest req = PutObjectRequest.builder()
    .bucket("private-bucket").key("invoices/" + id + ".pdf")
    .contentType("application/pdf").build();
PresignedPutObjectRequest url = presigner.presignPutObject(b -> b
    .signatureDuration(Duration.ofMinutes(5))   // expires quickly
    .putObjectRequest(req));

A strong engineer needs to understand private bucket + presigned URL, IAM role and encryption at a practical level.

What to understand deeply
  • Object storage for large, unstructured files

    S3/GCS/Azure Blob fits: image, video, PDF, report, log archive, backup. Not a fit: data that needs querying (DB), realtime (Kafka). Cheap cost ($0.023/GB/month standard, cheaper with glacier).

  • Private bucket is the default

    Block Public Access at account and bucket level. Access via IAM policy or presigned URL. Some buckets are intentionally public (website CDN assets) and must be approved separately + explicit bucket policy. Private by default reduces misconfiguration risk.

  • Presigned URL — temporary access

    Backend generates a URL with signature (key, method, expiry) using the AWS SDK. The client uses the URL to upload/download directly without credentials. TTL is usually 5-30 minutes. When it expires, the URL is useless. DO NOT share the URL in a public channel — anyone who has the URL within the TTL can access it.

    // Spring + AWS SDK v2
PresignedGetObjectRequest req = s3Presigner.presignGetObject(b -> b
    .signatureDuration(Duration.ofMinutes(15))
    .getObjectRequest(g -> g.bucket("reports").key("user/123/report.pdf"))
);
return req.url().toString();
  • IAM role — do not hardcode access keys

    EC2 instance profile, EKS IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts), Lambda execution role → the SDK fetches temporary credentials automatically, rotates automatically. Hardcoding AWS_ACCESS_KEY in code/config = dangerous leak (commit to a public git repo → attacker mining within minutes).

  • Encryption at rest ≠ access control

    SSE-S3 / SSE-KMS encrypt data on disk → protects against physical attack or disk leak. But if IAM allows GET → S3 decrypts itself and returns plaintext. Two different concepts, you need to combine both.

Production pitfall
  • Bucket public because it's "convenient for testing"

    A dev sets the bucket public to test upload quickly → forgets to revert → user data, internal reports get crawler-indexed. Countless major incidents (Verizon, AccuDoc, Pentagon) have happened because of public S3. Block Public Access at account level, requires explicit override.

  • Presigned URL TTL too long

    TTL 7 days → URL shared in a group chat → still accessible 6 days later. Set the minimum TTL (5-30 minutes) sufficient for the use case. Need long-term access → the user authenticates directly with the app, and the app generates a short-lived URL each time.

  • Logging the URL containing the signature

    Access log (nginx/CloudFront) logs the full URL including the query string with the signature → log analytics tools can leak it. Mask the signature in logs, or use PUT/POST with a signed header instead of a signed query.

  • Hardcoded access key

    application.yml: aws.accessKey: AKIA.... Commit to a public git repo → bots scan repos, GitHub secret scanning may catch it but not 100%. Migrate to IAM role (EC2/EKS/Lambda) or AWS SSO. If you accidentally commit, rotate the key NOW and investigate.

Example
Flow:
1. Service uploads result.zip to private bucket.
2. Service generates presigned URL valid 30 minutes.
3. Client downloads using URL.
4. URL expires, object remains private.
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I use a private bucket, IAM role, short-lived presigned URL, do not log the secret URL and have a lifecycle policy if needed.'
Key focus
Observability on cloud
Concept

Observability is the ability to understand what a system is doing internally through the data it emits: logs (events), metrics (measurements over time), traces (the path of one request through many services).

Why it exists: A distributed system can't be debugged by "reading the code" — it must be measurable. Golden signals (latency, traffic, errors, saturation) tell you whether the system is healthy.

How it works
  1. Metrics: aggregated measurements (p99 latency, error rate, CPU) → used for alerts + dashboards.
  2. Logs: details of each event, with a correlation/trace id to connect services.
  3. Traces: follow one request across services to find the bottleneck.
  4. Track the 4 golden signals; alert on the symptoms users see, don't spam alerts.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Can't trace a request across services: missing correlation/trace id → inject traceId into MDC + propagate via header.
  2. Log spam / PII leak: structured log (JSON), mask PII, sample log volume.
  3. Alert noise: alerting on every spike → alert by SLO/golden signals + sustain for a period before firing.
  4. Cardinality explosion: attaching a dynamic label (userId) to a metric → the number of series explodes. Limit labels.
  5. Only looking at the average: the average hides the tail → track p95/p99 latency.
// Attach traceId to MDC so every log of a request shares the same id
@Component
class TraceIdFilter extends OncePerRequestFilter {
  protected void doFilterInternal(HttpServletRequest r, ... chain) {
    String traceId = firstNonNull(r.getHeader("X-Trace-Id"), UUID.randomUUID());
    MDC.put("traceId", traceId);
    try { chain.doFilter(r, resp); } finally { MDC.clear(); }  // avoid leak
  }
}

A strong engineer doesn't just deploy, but must know how to measure and debug production.

What to understand deeply
  • Logs — what happened, detail

    Structured JSON with fields: timestamp, level, message, traceId, spanId, userId (if any), errorCode. Aggregator: Loki, ELK, CloudWatch Logs, Datadog. Sample log error level + 1 line. Don't log passwords, don't log credit cards.

    {
  "timestamp": "2026-05-22T10:00:01.234Z",
  "level": "ERROR",
  "service": "order-service",
  "traceId": "abc-123",
  "userId": "U-001",
  "message": "Failed to charge payment",
  "errorCode": "PAYMENT_GATEWAY_TIMEOUT",
  "exception": "..."
}
  • Metrics — golden signals: Latency, Traffic, Errors, Saturation

    Latency: P50, P95, P99 response time. Traffic: req/sec. Errors: 4xx/5xx rate. Saturation: CPU, memory, DB pool utilization, Kafka lag. Counter (increment), Gauge (current value), Histogram (distribution). Prometheus + Grafana are common.

  • Tracing — request path across many services

    Distributed trace with a trace id forwarded through every service. Each service emits a span (operation + duration + tags). Jaeger/Tempo/Zipkin/AWS X-Ray. Find the bottleneck quickly: trace 1 slow request to see which step takes the most time.

  • Alert — symptom-based, not action-based

    Good: "p95 latency > 500ms for 5 minutes" (user experience). Bad: "exception count > 10" (countless exceptions don't impact the user). SLO-driven: SLO p99 < 1s → alert when the error budget burn rate is high. Page on-call when truly critical.

Production pitfall
  • Logs without a correlation id

    log.info("Processing order") has no context. With 100 concurrent requests, logs are scattered, and you don't know which line belongs to which request. Set up a filter to generate/forward x-request-id, set MDC, and have the log appender include MDC fields in every line.

  • No connection pool metrics

    HikariCP exposes metrics (active, idle, awaiting). No monitoring → the pool gets exhausted silently, the app slows to death, no alert. Scrape Micrometer → Prometheus, dashboard hikaricp.connections.active and awaiting.

  • Alert fatigue — alerting on every exception

    Paging on-call every single exception → on-call mutes it → during a real incident no notification is seen. An alert must be: actionable, with clear severity, batching similar errors. Use SLO-based alerts: only page when the error budget burns fast.

  • Not monitoring Kafka consumer lag

    Consumer down for 30 minutes, lag backlog of 100k messages → restart has to catch up for a long time, and may not keep up with realtime business. Alert on lag > threshold (depends on topic), dashboard lag per consumer group.

Example
Golden signals:
- Latency: p95/p99 response time
- Traffic: requests per second
- Errors: 4xx/5xx rate
- Saturation: CPU, memory, DB pool, Kafka lag
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I prioritize golden signals, correlation id, structured logs, tracing and dashboards organized by business flow.'
Deep dive — Cloud
Presigned URL flow (secure file upload + download)
UPLOAD path:
   Browser/App           Backend (auth'd)            S3 (private)
       │                       │                        │
       │  POST /uploads/sign   │                        │
       │  {filename, size, mime}                        │
       ├──────────────────────►│                        │
       │                       │ validate user, quota   │
       │                       │ generate key e.g.      │
       │                       │  tenants/T1/u/U7/      │
       │                       │  2026/05/uuid.pdf      │
       │                       │ s3.presignPutObject(   │
       │                       │   bucket, key,         │
       │                       │   contentType,         │
       │                       │   maxBytes,            │
       │                       │   expires=10m)         │
       │  { uploadUrl, key }   │                        │
       │◄──────────────────────┤                        │
       │                                                │
       │  PUT uploadUrl  (binary)                       │
       ├───────────────────────────────────────────────►│
       │                                  201 (stored)  │
       │◄───────────────────────────────────────────────┤
       │                                                │
       │  POST /uploads/confirm { key }                 │
       ├──────────────────────►│                        │
       │                       │ HEAD key → check size, │
       │                       │ size  & contentType    │
       │                       │ persist file record    │
       │                       │ (tenant_id, key, ...)  │
       │  { fileId }           │                        │
       │◄──────────────────────┤                        │

DOWNLOAD path:
   Browser/App           Backend (auth'd)            S3 (private)
       │  GET /files/F1/url    │                        │
       ├──────────────────────►│                        │
       │                       │ authorize: user can    │
       │                       │ access F1?             │
       │                       │ presignGet (TTL=5m,    │
       │                       │   ResponseContentDisposition=attachment)
       │  { url } (TTL 5min)   │                        │
       │◄──────────────────────┤                        │
       │  GET url              │                        │
       ├───────────────────────────────────────────────►│
       │       200 binary stream                        │
       │◄───────────────────────────────────────────────┤

Guardrails:
   • Bucket: Block Public Access ON, default SSE-KMS encryption
   • Backend: IAM role (IRSA on EKS), no static keys
   • Key naming embeds tenant → prevents cross-tenant guessing
   • Always validate after upload (size, content type, malware scan)
   • Never log full URL (signature leaks)
   • CloudFront in front of GET URLs with short TTL = even less surface
War story — "the public bucket no one remembered"

Service: internal report generator. Reports written to S3, internal users got HTML emails with direct S3 links.

Symptom: Friday afternoon, a customer support engineer noticed search engine results returning internal reports under site:s3.amazonaws.com. Some reports contained customer PII.

Investigation: The bucket had been created in 2019 for a "quick demo," set public for one afternoon to share a screenshot externally, and never reverted. Three years of CRON output sat in it. AWS Config didn't flag because the org's policy excluded that account for "legacy reasons." Search engines had been crawling links shared in marketing decks.

Root cause: One-time decision + no review = permanent leak. Block Public Access was off at the bucket level, no aws:SecureTransport condition, no SSE-KMS, and links were direct S3 URLs (not presigned, not gated by app auth).

Fix (immediate): Block Public Access ON at account + bucket level. Rotated/deleted exposed objects. Filed disclosures per policy.

Fix (structural): All buckets enforce: Block Public Access at account level via SCP (can't be turned off without org-admin), default encryption with SSE-KMS, bucket policy denying non-TLS requests, and CloudTrail data events with alerting on PutBucketPolicy changes. Application-side: report links now go through GET /reports/{id}/download on the API, which authorizes the user then returns a 5-minute presigned URL. The S3 path is never exposed to clients.

Lesson: "Temporarily public" is permanent. Defense in depth means: org-level deny (SCP), bucket-level deny (BPA), object-level deny (policy), encryption, and access only via app-authenticated indirection. Any single failure of the above shouldn't leak data.

Production presigned URL service (AWS SDK v2, Spring Boot)
// Domain
public record FileUpload(
        UUID id, TenantId tenantId, UserId ownerId,
        String filename, String contentType, long sizeBytes,
        String storageKey, Instant createdAt) {}

@Service
@RequiredArgsConstructor
public class FileStorageService {

    private final S3Presigner presigner;
    private final S3Client s3;
    private final FileUploadRepository repo;
    private final Clock clock;

    private static final Duration UPLOAD_TTL  = Duration.ofMinutes(10);
    private static final Duration DOWNLOAD_TTL = Duration.ofMinutes(5);
    private static final long MAX_UPLOAD_BYTES = 50L * 1024 * 1024;     // 50 MiB
    private static final Set<String> ALLOWED_TYPES = Set.of(
            "application/pdf", "image/png", "image/jpeg");

    @Value("${app.s3.bucket}")
    private String bucket;

    public PresignedUpload signUpload(TenantId tenant, UserId user, NewUploadRequest req) {
        if (req.sizeBytes() > MAX_UPLOAD_BYTES)
            throw new BadRequestException("File too large");
        if (!ALLOWED_TYPES.contains(req.contentType()))
            throw new BadRequestException("Unsupported content type");

        // Tenant-scoped key prevents cross-tenant guessing
        String key = "tenants/%s/users/%s/%s/%s.%s".formatted(
                tenant.value(), user.value(),
                LocalDate.now(clock).format(DateTimeFormatter.ofPattern("yyyy/MM")),
                UUID.randomUUID(),
                extensionFor(req.contentType()));

        PutObjectRequest put = PutObjectRequest.builder()
                .bucket(bucket)
                .key(key)
                .contentType(req.contentType())
                .contentLength(req.sizeBytes())
                .serverSideEncryption(ServerSideEncryption.AWS_KMS)
                .ssekmsKeyId(System.getenv("S3_KMS_KEY_ID"))
                .metadata(Map.of(
                        "tenant-id", tenant.value().toString(),
                        "owner-id",  user.value().toString(),
                        "trace-id",  Optional.ofNullable(MDC.get("traceId")).orElse("")))
                .build();

        PresignedPutObjectRequest signed = presigner.presignPutObject(b -> b
                .signatureDuration(UPLOAD_TTL)
                .putObjectRequest(put));

        return new PresignedUpload(signed.url().toString(), key, signed.expiration());
    }

    public FileUpload confirmUpload(TenantId tenant, UserId user, String key) {
        HeadObjectResponse head;
        try {
            head = s3.headObject(b -> b.bucket(bucket).key(key));
        } catch (NoSuchKeyException e) {
            throw new BadRequestException("Object not present");
        }
        if (head.contentLength() > MAX_UPLOAD_BYTES)
            throw new BadRequestException("File exceeds size limit");
        if (!ALLOWED_TYPES.contains(head.contentType()))
            throw new BadRequestException("Unsupported content type");
        // Tenant pinning: refuse to register a key not belonging to the caller's tenant
        if (!key.startsWith("tenants/" + tenant.value() + "/"))
            throw new ForbiddenException("Key does not belong to your tenant");

        var record = FileUpload.builder()
                .id(UUID.randomUUID())
                .tenantId(tenant)
                .ownerId(user)
                .filename(head.metadata().getOrDefault("original-name", "untitled"))
                .contentType(head.contentType())
                .sizeBytes(head.contentLength())
                .storageKey(key)
                .createdAt(clock.instant())
                .build();
        return repo.save(record);
    }

    public PresignedDownload signDownload(TenantId tenant, UserId user, UUID fileId) {
        FileUpload f = repo.findById(fileId).orElseThrow(NotFoundException::new);
        if (!f.tenantId().equals(tenant)) throw new ForbiddenException();
        // Could add ACL check (shared-with) here

        GetObjectRequest get = GetObjectRequest.builder()
                .bucket(bucket)
                .key(f.storageKey())
                .responseContentDisposition("attachment; filename=\"" + f.filename() + "\"")
                .build();

        PresignedGetObjectRequest signed = presigner.presignGetObject(b -> b
                .signatureDuration(DOWNLOAD_TTL)
                .getObjectRequest(get));
        // Note: never log signed.url().toString() — query string contains the signature
        return new PresignedDownload(signed.url().toString(), signed.expiration());
    }
}

# application.yml — no static credentials, IAM role only
spring.cloud.aws.credentials.use-default-aws-credentials-chain: true
spring.cloud.aws.region.static: eu-west-1
app.s3.bucket: ${S3_BUCKET}                    # injected per environment
Interview scenario — "design a secure file upload feature"

Users need to upload up to 50MB files. Files should not be accessible to other users. Design the upload and download flow.

Two-step flow for upload, one-step for download. Upload: client calls POST /uploads/sign, server validates size/type/quota and returns a presigned PUT URL with a 10-minute TTL plus the storage key. Client PUTs directly to S3. Then client calls POST /uploads/confirm with the key; server HEADs the object to verify what was actually uploaded (size, content type) and persists a database record linking that key to the user. Download: GET /files/{id}/url, server authorizes, returns a 5-minute presigned GET URL.

Why two steps for upload? Why not just have the client tell you when it's done?

The HEAD on confirm is the verification — clients can lie about size and content type when they request the signature, but S3 records the actual bytes uploaded. Without that step, a malicious client could request a signature for 1MB and upload 5GB, then bill goes up and the database thinks they're under quota. Defense in depth: client constraint + URL constraint + post-upload verification.

Why presigned URLs instead of streaming through your API?

Cost and reliability. A 50MB upload through your app burns 50MB of egress, app server CPU for buffering, and a held HTTP connection. With presigned URLs, your app stays small and fast — it only sees a few-KB JSON request. S3 handles the multi-gigabit transfer with infinite parallelism. For large files or many concurrent uploads, streaming through your app is a scalability anti-pattern.

How do you prevent someone from accessing another user's file?

The bucket is private — Block Public Access on, no anonymous reads. Every download request goes through the API, which checks "does this user have access to file F1?" before generating a presigned URL. The presigned URL is for a specific S3 key, valid for 5 minutes, and only the API can generate one. Additionally, the storage key embeds the tenant ID — tenants/T1/users/U7/... — so even if our authorization logic had a bug, accidentally returning a key from another tenant would be caught by a path prefix check at confirm-time.

Anything you'd add for compliance / audit?

CloudTrail data events on the bucket, so every S3 GET and PUT is logged with the principal. Object-level encryption with SSE-KMS using a customer-managed key — that lets us revoke access by disabling the key without re-encrypting objects. Object Lock (Governance mode) for legal-hold use cases. And a lifecycle rule transitioning old objects to S3 IA or Glacier after N days for cost. None of this is "extra" — for any compliance scope (GDPR, SOC2, PCI), all of those become non-negotiable.

10. System Design / Clean Architecture / CI-CD

Strong engineers differ from mid-levels in the ability to design systems that are maintainable, testable, deployable and observable.

#10
Key focus
Clean Architecture / Hexagonal
Concept

Clean/Hexagonal Architecture organizes code into concentric layers: domain (pure business) at the innermost, infrastructure (DB, web, message) on the outside. Port is an interface defined by the domain; Adapter is the implementation in infrastructure.

Why it exists: So that the business does not depend on the framework/DB. Switching MySQL to Mongo, REST to gRPC only requires swapping the adapter, the domain does not change. The domain can be tested without a DB.

How it works
  1. Dependencies always point inward: infrastructure depends on the domain, not the reverse (Dependency Inversion).
  2. The domain defines ports (e.g. the OrderRepository interface).
  3. An adapter on the outside implements the port (JpaOrderRepository).
  4. Use cases orchestrate the domain through ports; framework/DB are just details plugged in at the edge.
