Common real-world scenarios
  1. Domain imports JPA/Spring annotations: business gets coupled to the framework → split out a pure domain, JPA entities live in the adapter.
  2. Business logic in controller/repository: pull it back into the use case/domain so it is testable and reusable.
  3. Changing DB/broker forces domain edits: define a port; changing only swaps the adapter.
  4. Applying hexagonal to a small CRUD: over-engineering → only use it for a core domain with heavy business logic.
  5. Mapping entity↔domain is tiring: accept the cost in the core, simplify in supporting/generic areas.

Engineers use architecture to protect business logic from the framework, database, message broker and external API.

What to understand deeply
  • Domain layer — pure business

    Entity, value object, domain service. No dependency on Spring, JPA, REST. If you must use JPA annotations, some teams split out a separate JPA entity (DB model) and map it to the domain entity. Testing the domain needs no Spring context, no DB.

    // domain/Order.java - pure Java
public final class Order {
    private final OrderId id;
    private final CustomerId customer;
    private List<OrderItem> items;
    private OrderStatus status;
    private Money total;

    public void confirm() {
        if (items.isEmpty()) throw new DomainException("Empty order");
        this.status = OrderStatus.CONFIRMED;
    }
}
  • Application layer — use case + transaction boundary

    Orchestrate domain objects + ports. The transaction lives here, not in the repository. Inject ports (interfaces), not implementations. One use case = one method (PlaceOrderUseCase.execute()).

  • Infrastructure — adapters implement ports

    JpaOrderRepository implements OrderRepository (port). KafkaOrderEventPublisher implements OrderEventPublisher. StripePaymentClient implements PaymentClient. Changing vendor = swapping the adapter, the domain does not change.

  • API/UI layer — input adapter

    Controller, gRPC service, CLI, message consumer are input adapters. Map DTO request → command → call use case → map result → DTO response. No business logic.

  • Dependency rule — Dependency Inversion Principle

    Inner layers do NOT know outer layers. Domain → does not know Application/Infrastructure. Application → knows Domain (through ports), does NOT know Infrastructure. Infrastructure → implements ports from Application/Domain. Spring DI wires it back together at startup.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Domain calls JpaRepository directly

    Order.confirm() injects OrderRepository (JPA) → the domain becomes coupled to persistence. Hard to test (needs a DB), hard to change DB. Fix: the domain receives already-loaded data, or use a port-style repository interface. JPA exists only in infrastructure.

  • Controller contains business logic

    A 100-line controller: validate, query DB, compute, transform, log audit. When you need to expose it through gRPC or CLI → copy-paste or a painful refactor. Logic must live in the use case, the controller only maps I/O and calls the use case.

  • God application service

    OrderService holds: createOrder, cancelOrder, refundOrder, exportOrders, importOrders, calcStats, ... 30 methods, 2000 lines. Split into separate use case classes: PlaceOrderUseCase, CancelOrderUseCase, ... single responsibility.

  • Layers in name only, messy dependencies

    There are 4 directories (domain, application, infrastructure, api) but the domain imports from infrastructure, the controller calls the repository directly → the layers are meaningless. Use ArchUnit tests to enforce the dependency rule in CI.

    // ArchUnit test
@ArchTest
static final ArchRule domain_should_not_depend_on_infrastructure =
    noClasses().that().resideInAPackage("..domain..")
        .should().dependOnClassesThat().resideInAPackage("..infrastructure..");
Example
order/
  domain/
    Order.java
    OrderItem.java
    OrderRepository.java   // port
  application/
    PlaceOrderUseCase.java
  infrastructure/
    JpaOrderRepository.java // adapter
    KafkaOrderEventPublisher.java
  api/
    OrderController.java
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I separate the use case from the adapter so the business logic is easy to test and not locked into the framework/database.'
Key focus
Design Patterns
Concept

A Design Pattern is a template solution for recurring design problems (Strategy, Factory, Builder, Adapter, Observer...). They are a shared vocabulary that helps express design intent.

Why it exists: So we do not reinvent the wheel and so the code is easier for others to read. But using a pattern that does not match a real pain = over-engineering, needless added complexity.

How it works
  1. Strategy: encapsulate a swappable algorithm → drop if/else by type.
  2. Factory/Builder: separate the creation of complex objects from where they are used.
  3. Adapter: wrap an external API to fit the domain interface (ACL).
  4. Choose a pattern starting from the problem, do not apply a pattern then stuff the problem into it.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Long if/else by type: Strategy + registry Map<Type, Handler>.
  2. Creating objects with many/optional parameters: Builder; creating by condition/type → Factory.
  3. Integrating a constantly changing vendor: Adapter/ACL isolates the external SDK from the domain.
  4. Need to notify many places when an event occurs: Observer/domain event instead of calling each place directly.
  5. Over-engineering: applying a pattern when there is no pain yet (AbstractFactory for one type) → YAGNI.
// Strategy + registry: adding a provider only adds a class, does not change old code
interface PaymentGateway { PaymentResult charge(Payment p); String type(); }

@Component
class PaymentService {
  private final Map<String, PaymentGateway> gateways;   // Spring injects all impls
  PaymentService(List<PaymentGateway> list) {
    this.gateways = list.stream().collect(toMap(PaymentGateway::type, g -> g));
  }
  PaymentResult pay(String type, Payment p) { return gateways.get(type).charge(p); }
}

Do not learn patterns to show off names. Use a pattern when it solves a real pain.

What to understand deeply
  • Strategy — replace if/else by type

    Use cases: pricing rule, payment provider, shipping calculator, export format, notification channel. Interface + multiple impls, registered into a Spring map. Adding a new rule = adding a class, not changing old code (Open/Closed).

    interface ShippingCalculator { Money calculate(Order o); }

@Component class StandardShipping implements ShippingCalculator { ... }
@Component class ExpressShipping implements ShippingCalculator { ... }

@Service
class CheckoutService {
    private final Map<ShippingType, ShippingCalculator> strategies;
    // Spring auto-wires when injecting Map<String, Interface>
}
  • Factory — create complex objects / pick an impl by input

    Factory method when the creation logic is complex (validate, dependency). Abstract Factory when creating a family of objects. In Spring, a @Bean method = the factory pattern. You rarely write your own Factory class in a Spring app.

  • Builder — object with many optional fields

    A 10-parameter constructor → hard to read, easy to mix up the order. A fluent Builder: Order.builder().customer(c).items(items).discount(d).build(). Lombok @Builder generates it. Suitable for complex DTO requests, config objects.

  • Adapter — wrap external API

    The domain defines a PaymentGateway port. The StripePaymentAdapter adapter implements it, calls the Stripe SDK, maps the response → domain model. Switching the vendor to Adyen = adding AdyenPaymentAdapter, the domain does not change.

  • Template Method — shared flow, different steps

    An abstract class has a process() method that calls step1(), step2(), step3() — the steps are abstract. Subclasses override the needed steps. Suitable for ETL pipelines, import/export with different formats.

  • Observer / Event — decouple side effects

    Order placed → you need to: send email, update inventory, log audit, push notification. Instead of calling 4 services in OrderService, publish OrderPlacedEvent — each listener handles its own. Spring @EventListener or Kafka.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Over-engineering

    2 cases "if type == A else B" → creating an interface + 2 strategies + factory + registry. Premature abstraction costs time and makes code hard to read. Rule: 3 cases or more + likely to grow → only then refactor to strategy. YAGNI (You Aren't Gonna Need It).

  • Confusing naming

    AbstractFactoryBuilderStrategyManager — naming after the pattern instead of after the domain. Reading it you cannot tell what the class does. Name it after the domain responsibility: ShippingCalculator, PaymentGateway, DiscountPolicy.

  • Splitting into too many classes

    1 use case = 10 classes (request, command, validator, mapper, handler, response, ...). The flow is hard to trace, debugging goes through many files. Split when there is a real reason, do not split "for the sake of clean code". A large but cohesive class is better than many scattered small classes.

  • Refactoring patterns without tests

    Refactoring if/else → strategy without test coverage. Boundary cases get missed. Before refactoring: write golden tests for the current behavior, then refactor, ensuring the tests still pass. Characterization tests for legacy code.

Example
interface DiscountPolicy {
    Money calculate(Order order);
}

class VipDiscountPolicy implements DiscountPolicy {
    public Money calculate(Order order) { ... }
}

class SeasonalDiscountPolicy implements DiscountPolicy {
    public Money calculate(Order order) { ... }
}
Interview answer tip: When asked about patterns, tie them to a real case: pricing, payment provider, notification channel, export format.
Key focus
CI/CD and release strategy
Concept

CI (Continuous Integration) automatically builds + tests on every code push. CD (Continuous Delivery/Deployment) automatically moves the build through environments to production. Release strategy is how you release safely (blue-green, canary, rolling).

Why it exists: To catch bugs early, deploy frequently and safely, and be able to roll back fast — instead of manual, rare, risk-laden deploys.

How it works
  1. Push → the pipeline builds, runs tests, scans quality/security (quality gate).
  2. Pass the gate → package an immutable artifact (image), push to the registry.
  3. Deploy through environments (dev → staging → prod), with approval if needed.
  4. Release safely: canary (gradually open up %), blue-green (switch to the new environment), always with a rollback path.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Manual deploy causes human error: automated pipeline + quality gate (test, coverage, scan).
  2. No fast rollback: blue-green/canary + keep the old artifact version to revert instantly.
  3. Slow tests make the pipeline sluggish: run in parallel, split into stages, fail-fast.
  4. Secrets leaked in pipeline logs: masked secrets / vault, do not echo.
  5. DB migration breaks prod: backward-compatible migration (expand → migrate → contract), separated from the code deploy.
# Pipeline: build -> test/scan (gate) -> image -> deploy canary -> promote
stages: [build, test, scan, package, deploy]
canary:                 # gradually open traffic, watch the error rate
  steps: [10%, 50%, 100%]
  rollback_on: error_rate > 1% || p99 > 800ms
# Rollback = point back to the previous image version (immutable artifact)

Engineers need to know which pipeline code travels through from local to production, how quality is controlled and how rollback works.

What to understand deeply
  • Standard pipeline stages

    Checkout → compile → unit test → static analysis (SonarQube) → security scan (Snyk, Trivy, OWASP DC) → package (jar) → docker build → docker scan → push registry → deploy staging → smoke test → deploy production. Each stage is a quality gate, fail = abort.

    # Github Actions example
jobs:
  build-test:
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/setup-java@v4
      - run: ./mvnw verify
      - run: ./mvnw sonar:sonar
      - uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
      - uses: aquasecurity/trivy-action@master  # scan image
  • Test pyramid

    Many unit tests (fast, isolated), a medium number of integration tests (DB, Kafka — using Testcontainers), few E2E tests. Unit tests < 5 minutes for the full suite. Integration tests < 15 minutes. E2E only runs in staging.

  • Release strategy: Rolling, Blue-Green, Canary

    Rolling (K8s default): replace old pods → new pods one at a time, traffic split evenly. Blue-Green: run 2 envs in parallel, switch traffic instantly (fast rollback). Canary: 5% new traffic, monitor 30 minutes, ramp 25% → 50% → 100%. More expensive but reduces the blast radius when there is a bug.

  • Feature flag — separate deploy from release

    Deploy code with the flag OFF → safe production deploy. Turn the flag on (by user, % of traffic) → release the feature. Roll back by turning the flag off (seconds), no redeploy needed. Tools: LaunchDarkly, Unleash, ConfigCat, or build your own with Redis.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Manual, non-repeatable deploy

    SSH into the server, copy the jar, restart with a hand-written script. Every deploy is a different beast. No logs, no audit, no rollback. Migrate to a CI/CD pipeline; deploy only by git push tag or merge to main.

  • No rollback plan

    Image tagged latest, old versions not kept → when prod fails, you do not know what to roll back to. Or the DB migration is not reversible. Every deploy must have: an immutable image, a backward-compatible DB migration, a rollback plan (kubectl rollout undo).

  • Slow tests → skipped

    A 1-hour pipeline → devs push code without waiting, merge in a hurry → bugs slip through. Optimize: parallel tests, test sharding, only run tests related to the change. Set a goal: full pipeline < 15-20 minutes.

  • Config drift between envs

    Dev has config retry.maxAttempts: 5, prod forgot to set it → default 0 → different behavior. Maintain config under Git (Kubernetes ConfigMap), the diff between envs must be minimal and documented. Tools: Helm values, Kustomize overlay, ArgoCD.

  • Image not tagged with SHA

    2 deploys with the tag "v1.0" can be 2 different images (if CI rebuilds). You cannot trace a bug to a specific commit. CI builds must tag the image with the git SHA (immutable): app:1.0-a1b2c3d. Container registry GC keeps old images for a few months.

Example
Typical pipeline:
1. Checkout code
2. Build
3. Unit tests
4. Integration tests
5. Static analysis/security scan
6. Build Docker image
7. Push image registry
8. Deploy to staging
9. Smoke test
10. Deploy production
Interview answer tip: Say: 'A good pipeline must be repeatable, observable, with a quality gate and a rollback strategy.'
Deep dive — Architecture / CI-CD
Hexagonal architecture — dependency direction enforced
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ Adapter / Driving side (input)              Frameworks here    │
│  REST controllers, gRPC, Kafka consumers, CLI, scheduler        │
└────────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────┘
                                 │ depends on ▼ (call use case ports)
┌────────────────────────────────▼────────────────────────────────┐
│ Application layer (use cases)               Pure orchestration  │
│  PlaceOrderUseCase, CancelOrderUseCase                          │
│  Transaction boundary lives HERE                                │
│  Depends only on Domain + Ports                                 │
└────────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────┘
                                 │ depends on ▼ (port interfaces)
┌────────────────────────────────▼────────────────────────────────┐
│ Domain layer                                Pure Java/Kotlin    │
│  Entities: Order, Customer                                      │
│  Value objects: Money, OrderId                                  │
│  Domain services: PricingPolicy                                 │
│  Ports (interfaces): OrderRepository, PaymentGateway            │
│  NO framework imports here                                      │
└────────────────────────────────▲────────────────────────────────┘
                                 │ implements ports ▲
┌────────────────────────────────┴────────────────────────────────┐
│ Adapter / Driven side (output)              Frameworks here    │
│  JpaOrderRepository, KafkaPaymentClient, StripeAdapter, ...     │
│  Spring/JPA/Kafka annotations live HERE                         │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Dependency Rule:  outer → inner   (never the reverse)
Why it pays off:
  • domain unit tests need no Spring, no DB, no mocks of frameworks
  • swap Postgres → DynamoDB by writing a new adapter
  • controllers stay thin (no business logic to retest)
  • ArchUnit can enforce this in CI — see code box below

The "ports & adapters" name (Cockburn) is the same as Clean Architecture
inner-vs-outer (Martin) and Onion Architecture (Palermo). Pick a vocabulary
and stick with it across the team.
War story — "the 6-month framework migration we didn't do"

Service: a 5-year-old order-service, ~120k LOC, originally Spring Boot 1.5 + JPA.

Symptom: Spring Boot 1.5 was end-of-life. Quoting the migration to 3.x came back as "6-8 person-months." A second team was asked to evaluate replacing JPA with jOOQ for performance — that estimate was "9 months minimum, very risky."

Root cause: The codebase had no architectural seam. Spring annotations and JPA imports were in 80% of the files, including domain classes. @Transactional on controllers, business logic in @Component services that mixed entity manipulation, REST calls, and Kafka publishing. Every change touched the framework layer because the domain was the framework layer.

Reframe: A new team lead proposed: don't migrate, refactor first. Six weeks of work split into incremental PRs:

  • Introduce a domain package. Move pure business types (Order, Money, OrderStatus) into it. Strip JPA annotations from these classes; create a parallel OrderJpaEntity in infrastructure with a mapper.
  • Define ports (OrderRepository, PaymentGateway) as interfaces in domain or application. Existing implementations move to infrastructure and implement the port.
  • Introduce use case classes for the top 5 flows. Controllers thin out to just I/O mapping and one use-case call.
  • Add ArchUnit tests to make the direction enforceable: noClasses().that().resideInAPackage("..domain..").should().dependOnClassesThat().resideInAPackage("..infrastructure..").

Result: The Spring Boot 3 migration took 3 weeks instead of 6 months. The JPA-to-jOOQ replacement, when it eventually happened, took 2 weeks — only the infrastructure adapter changed. The domain didn't know.

Lesson: Architecture isn't about elegance, it's about optionality. The cost of putting framework concerns inside business code is invisible until you need to change frameworks — and then it's enormous. Hexagonal architecture is the price you pay so that "swap this dependency" is a 2-week project, not a quarter.

Module structure + ArchUnit enforcement + CI gate
// === Project layout (Maven multi-module) ===
order-service/
├── domain/                       <-- no Spring, no JPA, no Jackson
│   └── src/main/java/com/x/order/domain/
│       ├── Order.java            (pure)
│       ├── OrderId.java          (record)
│       ├── Money.java            (value object)
│       ├── OrderRepository.java  (port interface)
│       └── PaymentGateway.java   (port interface)
│
├── application/                  <-- only domain + Spring's @Service/@Transactional
│   └── src/main/java/com/x/order/application/
│       ├── PlaceOrderUseCase.java
│       ├── CancelOrderUseCase.java
│       └── dto/PlaceOrderCommand.java
│
├── infrastructure/               <-- adapters (Spring, JPA, Kafka, HTTP clients)
│   └── src/main/java/com/x/order/infrastructure/
│       ├── persistence/
│       │   ├── OrderJpaEntity.java
│       │   ├── OrderJpaRepository.java       <-- Spring Data
│       │   └── JpaOrderRepository.java       <-- implements domain.OrderRepository
│       ├── messaging/KafkaOrderPublisher.java
│       └── http/StripePaymentAdapter.java
│
└── api/                          <-- REST controllers, gRPC, schedulers
    └── src/main/java/com/x/order/api/
        ├── OrderController.java
        └── dto/OrderResponse.java

// === ArchUnit test — enforced in CI ===
@AnalyzeClasses(packages = "com.x.order",
                importOptions = ImportOption.DoNotIncludeTests.class)
class HexagonalArchitectureTest {

    @ArchTest
    static final ArchRule domain_has_no_framework_dependencies =
        noClasses().that().resideInAPackage("..domain..")
            .should().dependOnClassesThat().resideInAnyPackage(
                "org.springframework..", "jakarta.persistence..",
                "com.fasterxml.jackson..", "org.hibernate..");

    @ArchTest
    static final ArchRule application_does_not_depend_on_infrastructure =
        noClasses().that().resideInAPackage("..application..")
            .should().dependOnClassesThat().resideInAPackage("..infrastructure..");

    @ArchTest
    static final ArchRule api_does_not_call_repositories =
        noClasses().that().resideInAPackage("..api..")
            .should().dependOnClassesThat().resideInAPackage("..infrastructure.persistence..");

    @ArchTest
    static final ArchRule use_cases_named_correctly =
        classes().that().resideInAPackage("..application..")
                 .and().areAnnotatedWith(org.springframework.stereotype.Service.class)
            .should().haveSimpleNameEndingWith("UseCase");

    @ArchTest
    static final ArchRule layered_architecture =
        layeredArchitecture().consideringAllDependencies()
            .layer("Domain").definedBy("..domain..")
            .layer("Application").definedBy("..application..")
            .layer("Infrastructure").definedBy("..infrastructure..")
            .layer("API").definedBy("..api..")
            .whereLayer("Domain").mayNotAccessAnyLayer()
            .whereLayer("Application").mayOnlyAccessLayers("Domain")
            .whereLayer("Infrastructure").mayOnlyAccessLayers("Domain", "Application")
            .whereLayer("API").mayOnlyAccessLayers("Application", "Domain");
}

# === CI gate (GitHub Actions, fragment) ===
jobs:
  verify:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - uses: actions/setup-java@v4
        with: { java-version: '21', distribution: 'temurin' }
      - run: ./mvnw -B verify          # runs ArchUnit + unit + IT
      - run: ./mvnw -B sonar:sonar -Dsonar.qualitygate.wait=true
      - uses: aquasecurity/trivy-action@master
        with:
          image-ref: ghcr.io/org/order-service:${{ github.sha }}
          exit-code: '1'
          severity: 'HIGH,CRITICAL'
      - uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          tags: |
            ghcr.io/org/order-service:${{ github.sha }}
            ghcr.io/org/order-service:1.4.${{ github.run_number }}
          push: true
Interview scenario — "design a service with the team"

You're joining a team that's about to build a new order service from scratch. How would you set it up architecturally?

I'd push for hexagonal with three Maven modules: domain, application, infrastructure-and-api (sometimes split, sometimes not, depending on team size). Domain has zero framework dependencies — pure Java/Kotlin records and classes. Application has the use cases and the transaction boundary; it depends on domain interfaces only. Infrastructure implements those interfaces with JPA/Kafka/whatever. The key isn't the names — it's the dependency direction, enforced by ArchUnit in CI from day one.

Isn't that overkill for a small team?

It's the cheapest insurance you'll buy. The cost of having three packages is two PRs of refactoring at the start. The cost of not having them, six months in, is "we can't upgrade Spring Boot because everything imports it." I've seen that exact bill — six person-months for a migration that should have been three weeks. The hexagonal cost is paid up front and is small; the non-hexagonal cost is paid later and grows. ArchUnit catches violations on day-one PRs so the discipline doesn't drift.

When have you seen this architecture be wrong?

When it becomes ritual rather than design. Three signs: every use case has exactly one method called execute(), mappers exist solely to copy fields from one record to an identical record, and the team can't articulate which test covers which layer. At that point the architecture is theater. The fix is usually to consolidate — merge use cases that share state, drop pointless DTOs that duplicate domain types, and let some classes be both "service" and "entity" if they really are one thing. The goal is decoupling that pays off, not symmetry that doesn't.

How would you set up the CI/CD pipeline for it?

Quality gates in order, each able to fail the build: compile → unit tests → ArchUnit (architecture rules) → integration tests (Testcontainers for Postgres/Kafka) → static analysis (SonarQube with quality gate) → image build → image vulnerability scan (Trivy, fail on HIGH/CRITICAL) → push to registry with SHA-tagged image. Deploy is a separate job tied to environment promotions: develop → staging on every main merge, staging → prod on manual approval (or auto if you have canary + automatic rollback). Image tags are immutable — git SHA — never latest.

Rollback strategy?

Two layers. Infrastructure: kubectl rollout undo deployment/order-service rolls back to the previous SHA in seconds. That works because we never deploy latest — the previous SHA is a real, immutable image in the registry. Feature: every risky feature is behind a flag (LaunchDarkly or Unleash). If the deploy goes out but a feature misbehaves, we flip the flag off and the rest of the deploy stays. That separation — "deploy" is when code lands, "release" is when users see it — is the single biggest reliability win I know.

11. Domain-Driven Design (Strategic + Tactical)

DDD is not about drawing pretty UML. A strong engineer uses DDD to split the system along business language, keep business rules alive inside the domain model, and decide the right boundary for transaction/service/team.

#11
Key focus
Strategic DDD — Ubiquitous Language, Bounded Context, Context Map
Concept

Strategic DDD focuses on splitting a large system along business lines. Ubiquitous Language is the shared language used by both dev and business. Bounded Context is the boundary within which one model/language has a consistent meaning. Context Map describes how the contexts connect.

Why it exists: In a large system, the same word ("Customer") carries a different meaning in each department. Bounded Context avoids cramming one giant model that serves everywhere (an ambiguous model that breaks whenever anyone changes it).

How it works
  1. Classify subdomains: core (creates competitive advantage), supporting, generic.
  2. Each bounded context has its own model + language, usually corresponding to one service.
  3. Draw the context map: Upstream/Downstream, Customer-Supplier, Conformist, ACL relationships.
  4. Same word in a different context → different model (Customer in Sales ≠ in Billing), connected through events.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. The same word "Customer" means different things (Sales vs Billing): split into bounded contexts, each side with its own model, synced via events.
  2. Big ball of mud — one model for everywhere: split contexts by subdomain so changing one part does not break another.
  3. Integrating legacy/vendor: ACL translates the model both ways, never letting the external model leak into the domain.
  4. Many teams stepping on the same module: context = team boundary (Conway's law).
  5. Not distinguishing core vs generic: invest DDD into core (creates competitive advantage), buy/simplify generic (auth, payment).

Before writing a single line of code, A strong engineer must establish: which model applies where, whether two teams saying "Customer" mean the same thing, and who owns which subdomain.

What to understand deeply
  • Ubiquitous Language — shared language for dev/PO/QA/business

    If the business calls it "place an order" then the code must be placeOrder(), not createOrderRecord(). Class, method, event, table, and API path names all use the right business words. When the language diverges, dev understands one thing and business another → business bugs of the "correct code, wrong requirement" kind.

    // Wrong — technical language
orderService.updateStatus(orderId, "PAID");

// Right — business language
order.markPaid(paymentReference);
  • Bounded Context — the scope where one model has a single meaning

    "Customer" in the Sales context (lead, deal, contact) differs from "Customer" in the Billing context (account, invoice address, tax id) and from "Customer" in Support (ticket history). Same name, different model. Each bounded context has its own team, codebase, DB schema, and language. Bounded context = service boundary in microservices.

  • Context Map — relationships between bounded contexts

    Not every context is equal. 7 common patterns:

    • Shared Kernel — 2 contexts share a small piece of code/model; changes require both teams to agree. High coupling risk.
    • Customer/Supplier — the downstream (customer) can influence the upstream's (supplier's) roadmap.
    • Conformist — the downstream accepts the upstream's model as-is, no negotiation.
    • Anti-Corruption Layer (ACL) — the downstream wraps a translator so the internal model is not "polluted" by the upstream model. The most important pattern when integrating legacy/3rd party.
    • Open Host Service + Published Language — the upstream exposes a stable API/event format for many downstreams.
    • Separate Ways — no integration, each context does its own thing.
  • Core / Supporting / Generic subdomain — invest in the right place

    Core: where competitive advantage is created (e.g., the pricing engine of an e-commerce marketplace, a bank's fraud scoring) → experienced engineers, in-house code, heavy investment. Supporting: dedicated auxiliary business (e.g., catalog, custom CRM) → can be outsourced or built to a "good enough" level. Generic: business everyone has, such as auth, email, common billing → use a vendor/SaaS, do not build it yourself.

Production issue / pitfall
  • "Customer" shared across the whole system

    One Customer class with 40 fields bearing every need: sales, billing, support, marketing. Each new feature adds 2-3 fields. Updating field A accidentally breaks feature B. Fix: split into SalesCustomer, BillingAccount, SupportContact — each bounded context with its own model, synced via events.

  • No ACL when integrating legacy/3rd party

    The domain model calls the Stripe/Salesforce SDK directly, using the vendor's objects as domain objects. The vendor changes a field → the domain breaks. The vendor goes down → domain tests fail too. Fix: define a PaymentGateway port in the domain, write a StripePaymentAdapter in infrastructure as a translator. The domain does not know Stripe exists.

    // domain port
interface PaymentGateway {
    PaymentResult charge(Money amount, PaymentMethod method);
}

// infrastructure ACL adapter
class StripePaymentAdapter implements PaymentGateway {
    public PaymentResult charge(Money amount, PaymentMethod method) {
        var stripeReq = toStripeRequest(amount, method);   // translate in
        var stripeRes = stripeClient.charges().create(stripeReq);
        return toDomain(stripeRes);                        // translate out
    }
}
  • Microservices split by table/CRUD, not by bounded context

    user-service, user-address-service, user-preference-service — a distributed monolith where every business change requires editing 3 services and deploying them together. Fix: merge back into an Identity bounded context, or split by real capability (Sales / Billing / Fulfillment).

  • Investing equally in every subdomain

    The most experienced team sits building the email-sending and user-management system — which is generic. Meanwhile the core domain (pricing/matching/fraud) is left to juniors + default libraries. Fix: classify subdomains clearly, concentrate strong engineers on core, and use vendors for generic.

Example context map for an e-commerce system
┌────────────┐    OHS+PL    ┌────────────┐
│  Catalog   │─────────────▶│   Search   │   (Catalog publishes ProductPublished events)
└────────────┘              └────────────┘
       │
       │  conformist
       ▼
┌────────────┐    ACL       ┌────────────┐
│   Order    │─────────────▶│  Stripe    │   (Payment integrates via Anti-Corruption Layer)
└────────────┘              └────────────┘
       │  customer/supplier
       ▼
┌────────────┐
│  Fulfill   │
└────────────┘
Interview answer tip: When asked "how do you split microservices?", answer: "I start from bounded contexts, not from tables. I draw a context map to identify core/supporting/generic subdomains, then use an ACL to protect the core from legacy and vendors."
Key focus
Tactical DDD — Aggregate, Value Object, Domain Event, Repository, Factory
Concept

Tactical DDD is the building blocks inside a context: Aggregate (a cluster of objects that change together, with a single root), Value Object (identified by value, immutable — Money, Address), Domain Event (something that has happened), Repository (a gateway to store/read aggregates), Factory (creates valid aggregates).

Why it exists: To place business logic in the right spot (rich domain model), protect invariants, and express the business clearly instead of scattering logic across services (anemic model).

How it works
  1. The aggregate root is the only gateway for modifying the cluster; protect invariants inside the business methods.
  2. Value Objects are immutable, compared by value (Money(100,"USD")) → avoid primitive obsession.
  3. Business behavior emits a Domain Event (OrderConfirmed) → other parts react.
  4. The Repository stores/reads by aggregate root; the Factory creates aggregates in a valid state from the start.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Anemic model (getters/setters only): rules scattered across services → put the behavior into the aggregate (confirm/pay/cancel).
  2. Aggregate too large: high lock contention, hard to scale → shrink it, reference other aggregates by id.
  3. Primitive obsession (String money, String email): immutable Value Objects (Money, Email) that self-validate.
  4. Modifying multiple aggregates in one TX: split them out, link via domain events (eventual consistency).
  5. Repository leaking internals: return only the aggregate root, do not expose the internal collection for direct modification.

Tactical DDD is the "toolkit" for coding a behavior-rich (rich) domain model, keeping invariants in the right place, and clearly separating transaction boundaries.

What to understand deeply
  • Aggregate & Aggregate Root — consistency boundary

    An aggregate is a cluster of entities + value objects with shared invariants, protected by a single entry: the aggregate root. The outside interacts only through the root, never touching the children. 1 aggregate = 1 transaction: changing multiple aggregates in the same TX is a code smell — it suggests the aggregate is too large or the boundary is wrong.

    // Order is the aggregate root; OrderItem is a child entity
public final class Order {
    private final OrderId id;
    private final CustomerId customer;     // reference another aggregate BY ID
    private final List<OrderItem> items;
    private OrderStatus status;
    private Money total;

    public void addItem(ProductId p, int qty, Money price) {
        if (status != OrderStatus.DRAFT) throw new DomainException("Order locked");
        items.add(new OrderItem(p, qty, price));
        recalcTotal();   // invariant: total = sum(items)
    }

    public void confirm() {
        if (items.isEmpty()) throw new DomainException("Empty order");
        this.status = OrderStatus.CONFIRMED;
    }
}
  • Value Object — immutable, equality by value

    No id, no lifecycle. Two value objects are equal when every field is equal. Behavior lives with the value object: Money.add(), Money.multiply() — not a utility class. Typical examples: Money, OrderId, Address, DateRange, Email.

    public record Money(BigDecimal amount, Currency currency) {
    public Money {
        Objects.requireNonNull(amount);
        Objects.requireNonNull(currency);
        if (amount.scale() > currency.getDefaultFractionDigits())
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Scale mismatch");
    }
    public Money add(Money other) {
        if (!currency.equals(other.currency))
            throw new DomainException("Currency mismatch");
        return new Money(amount.add(other.amount), currency);
    }
}
  • Domain Event — something that has happened in the domain

    Name it in past tense: OrderConfirmed, PaymentCaptured, InventoryReserved. Different from a "command" (an order that can fail). An event is a fact, it has already happened. Publish the event after the aggregate changes, not in the constructor. Persist the event via the outbox pattern to guarantee at-least-once (see topic 4: Microservices).

  • Repository — collection-like, returns only aggregate roots

    A repository "pretends" to be an in-memory collection of aggregates. Methods: save(Order), findById(OrderId), remove(Order). There is no findOrderItemById — an item is accessed only through the root. A repository is an interface in the domain, with its implementation in infrastructure (JPA, JDBC, MongoDB).

  • Factory — create complex aggregates, guaranteeing invariants from the start

    Used when creation needs many steps/dependencies or must guarantee invariants before the object is exposed. Unlike the GoF Factory (choosing an implementation by type), a DDD factory focuses on the creation of an aggregate. It can be a static method on the aggregate (Order.placeNew(...)) or a dedicated class.

  • Domain Service — logic that belongs to no entity

    When logic spans multiple aggregates or does not naturally belong to a single aggregate, use a domain service (stateless, business language). E.g.: PricingService.priceFor(cart, customer) — it is neither Cart's nor Customer's. Different from an application service: the application service orchestrates the use case + transaction; the domain service holds only pure business logic.

  • Specification — package a business rule into an object

    When the same rule (e.g., "VIP customer") is used in 3 places: query, validation, branching → extract it into VipCustomerSpecification.isSatisfiedBy(customer). Easy to test, easy to reuse, named in the right business language.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Anemic Domain Model — the entity is just a DTO

    The entity has only getters/setters, with every rule scattered across services. orderService.updateStatus(order, "PAID") does not check paymentRef, another service sets "CONFIRMED" skipping the payment step → data inconsistency. Fix: invariants belong to the aggregate, expose business methods (order.markPaid(ref)), no public setters.

  • Aggregate too large — "God Aggregate"

    The Customer aggregate holds Orders, Invoices, Tickets, Addresses, Preferences. Each update loads 50MB, locks all of the customer's data, high contention. Fix: split into smaller aggregates, reference by id, sync via domain events. Rule of thumb: a small aggregate is better than a large one.

  • Changing multiple aggregates in the same transaction

    A @Transactional method loads Order + Inventory + Customer + Loyalty, updates all 4, and saves. High lock contention, scaling per aggregate hits limits, and you cannot split the service later. Fix: change only 1 aggregate per TX, publish a domain event, other aggregates become eventually consistent via outbox + handler.

  • Referencing an object instead of an id between aggregates

    order.setCustomer(customerEntity) — JPA loads Customer together with Order, n+1, unintended cascades, and it breaks when you split the service. Fix: order.setCustomerId(customerId). Customer is a different aggregate, referenced only by id.

  • Mutable Value Object — using setters

    money.setAmount(...) → 2 places hold the reference, one changes it → the other changes too. Equality and hashCode are unstable, and used as a HashMap key it disappears from the map (see topic 1: HashMap pitfall). Fix: final fields, no setters, every "change" returns a new object.

  • Domain Event uses the wrong tense

    CreateOrderEvent, PayOrderEvent — sounds like a command, not an event. Correct: OrderPlaced, OrderPaid. The reader immediately sees this is a fact that has happened, which cannot be refused or retried as a "cancel".

Example — the Place Order use case fully assembled
// === domain ===
public final class Order {                       // aggregate root
    private final OrderId id;
    private final CustomerId customerId;         // ref by id
    private final List<OrderItem> items;
    private OrderStatus status;
    private final List<DomainEvent> events = new ArrayList<>();

    public static Order placeNew(CustomerId c, List<OrderItem> items) {  // factory
        if (items.isEmpty()) throw new DomainException("Empty order");
        var order = new Order(OrderId.newId(), c, items, OrderStatus.CONFIRMED);
        order.events.add(new OrderPlaced(order.id, c, order.total()));   // past tense
        return order;
    }
    public List<DomainEvent> pullEvents() { var c = List.copyOf(events); events.clear(); return c; }
}

public interface OrderRepository {               // port, collection-like
    Optional<Order> findById(OrderId id);
    void save(Order order);
}

// === application ===
@Service
public class PlaceOrderUseCase {
    private final OrderRepository orders;
    private final DomainEventPublisher events;   // outbox-backed in infra

    @Transactional                                // 1 use case = 1 TX = 1 aggregate write
    public OrderId execute(PlaceOrderCommand cmd) {
        var order = Order.placeNew(cmd.customerId(), cmd.items());
        orders.save(order);
        events.publishAll(order.pullEvents());    // will be written to the outbox in the same TX
        return order.id();
    }
}
Interview answer tip: If asked "when do you use a large vs small aggregate?", answer: "I prefer small aggregates. The larger the aggregate, the higher the lock contention and the harder it is to split a service. I only merge when there is an invariant that must be preserved immediately (e.g., Order + OrderItems). Otherwise I reference by id and sync via domain events."
Key focus
DDD in production — Transaction boundary, ACL, CQRS, when NOT to use DDD
Concept

DDD in production is knowing how to apply the concepts under real constraints: the aggregate boundary is exactly the transaction boundary and the scaling boundary; the ACL isolates external systems; CQRS separates the read/write models; and most importantly — knowing when NOT to use DDD.

Why it exists: DDD is a trade-off: it increases expressiveness but also increases complexity. Forcing it onto simple CRUD only bloats the code with no benefit.

How it works
  1. One aggregate = one transaction. Small aggregate → less contention, easier to split into a service.
  2. Link multiple aggregates via domain events (eventual consistency), not one giant TX.
  3. An ACL wraps every legacy/vendor integration; CQRS only when the read pattern differs widely from the write.
  4. Do NOT use DDD when the business is just "validate + save" (form/CRUD, short lifecycle).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Drawing aggregates from UML: wrong. Draw by the invariant that must be atomic — because the aggregate boundary = transaction = scaling boundary.
  2. Read differs widely from write (dashboard, search): CQRS — a separate flat read model, synced via events.
  3. The domain needs history/audit (ledger, banking): consider Event Sourcing (state = fold(events)); but querying is hard, and it is not mandatory to go alongside DDD.
  4. Vendor integration: an ACL adapter so swapping the vendor does not touch the domain.
  5. Simple CRUD, short lifecycle: do NOT use DDD — Spring Data + DTO is enough, do not over-engineer.
// CQRS: the write side emits events, the read model updates separately for fast queries
order.confirm();                       // write side -> emit OrderConfirmed

@EventListener                          // read side (flat projection)
void on(OrderConfirmed ev) {
    orderListReadRepo.upsert(new OrderListItemDto(
        ev.orderId(), ev.customerId(), ev.total(), "CONFIRMED", ev.occurredAt()));
}

DDD is a trade-off. A strong engineer must know when it answers a real pain, and when it merely adds useless complexity.

What to understand deeply
  • Aggregate boundary = transaction boundary = scaling boundary

    The same decision: 1 aggregate is serialized/locked within 1 TX. The smaller the aggregate → the higher the throughput, and the easier to shard/split into a service later. Do not draw aggregates from "entities in UML", draw by which invariant must be atomic.

  • Cross-aggregate consistency = eventual via domain events

    Order confirmed → Inventory reserved → Payment captured. 3 aggregates, 3 services, 3 TXs. Linked by domain events via outbox + Kafka (see topic 4 Microservices, topic 5 Kafka). The consumer must be idempotent. A saga orchestrates if rollback/compensation is needed.

  • Anti-Corruption Layer when integrating legacy/vendor

    Every external integration (Stripe, Salesforce, legacy mainframe, 3rd-party API) must go through an ACL adapter. The domain defines a port, the ACL adapter in infrastructure translates the model both ways. Swapping the vendor = replacing the adapter, the domain does not change (see topic 10 §1 Hexagonal).

  • CQRS — split the read model when read requirements differ widely from write

    Write model: a behavior-rich aggregate, optimized for consistency. Read model: a flat projection optimized for querying (dashboard, search, list). Synced via domain events. Not every service needs CQRS — only when the read pattern is diverse and the write model slows down reads.

    // write side
order.confirm();   // emit OrderConfirmed

// projection side
@EventListener
void on(OrderConfirmed ev) {
    orderListReadRepo.upsert(new OrderListItemDto(
        ev.orderId(), ev.customerId(), ev.total(), "CONFIRMED", ev.occurredAt()));
}
  • Event Sourcing — state = fold(events)

    Instead of storing the current state, store every event that has happened. State is rebuilt by replay. Free audit, time-travel debugging possible. Trade-off: querying is hard (needs a separate projection), event schema migration is hard, and it does not fit every domain. Commonly seen in banking, ledger, audit-heavy domains. Not mandatory to go alongside DDD.

  • When NOT to use DDD

    Simple CRUD apps (admin panels, internal tools), few domain rules, short lifecycle → DDD only adds boilerplate. A team that is not yet familiar → half-baked adoption creates "DDD theater": there are domain/application/infrastructure folders but the entity is still anemic and the repository still returns a JPA entity. Better to start with a simple service + JPA entity and refactor to DDD when the business logic genuinely becomes complex.

Production issue / pitfall
  • "DDD theater" — all the folders, no rich model

    4 packages domain/application/infrastructure/api, but Order has only getters/setters and all the logic is in OrderService. The layers are merely cosmetic. Fix: pull invariants back into the aggregate, enforce with ArchUnit + code review, and make sure entities have real business methods.

  • Applying DDD to every service, even generic ones

    Email service, file upload service, audit log service — generic subdomains that need no complex aggregate/value object/domain event. Forcing DDD onto them → 3x the boilerplate, no added value. Fix: classify subdomains first, apply DDD only to core (and part of supporting).

  • Distributed transaction between aggregates

    Using 2PC/XA to keep Order + Inventory consistent in the same TX → fragile, poor performance, does not scale. Fix: accept eventual consistency, use outbox + idempotent consumer + saga for compensation (see topic 4 §2 Saga).

  • Event payload containing the entire aggregate state

    A 50KB event containing the whole Order + Items + Customer. The consumer needs only 3 fields, but coupling is tight and the payload bloats. Fix: the event contains id + changed fields + minimal business context. If the consumer needs more, it queries again (CQRS read model) or uses the "claim check" pattern.

  • Unnecessary CQRS

    A simple CRUD service, 5 endpoints, 1 dashboard → splitting read/write models, 2 DB schemas, event projection just because it is "best practice". Doubles the complexity for no reason. Fix: start with a single model, add a read projection only when a query genuinely hurts.

When you should use DDD — quick checklist
✅ Complex domain, many business rules, rules change over time
✅ More business bugs than technical bugs
✅ Many teams working in parallel, need clear boundaries
✅ System lives for many years, refactoring is normal
✅ A domain expert is available to collaborate on building the ubiquitous language

❌ Simple CRUD, few rules, rarely changing
❌ Prototype/throwaway system
❌ A new team, not yet familiar → start with service + JPA, refactor later
❌ Adopting it to "look pro" without real pain
Interview answer tip: When asked "does your project use DDD?", do not say "yes" or "no". Answer: "I apply DDD in the core domain (e.g., pricing/order), with aggregates, value objects, domain events. In supporting/generic I still use a simple service + JPA. I use an ACL for every integration with vendors and legacy. I do not apply CQRS/Event Sourcing unless there is real pain."
Mock interview
Mock — Domain-Driven Design

Walk me through how you'd decompose an e-commerce platform with DDD.

I start strategic, not tactical. First I sit with product and identify subdomains: core is what differentiates us — say, pricing/promotion and search ranking. Supporting is catalog, orders, fulfillment. Generic is auth, payments, notifications. Then I draw a context map: Catalog publishes ProductPublished events as Open Host Service; Search is a downstream Conformist consuming those events; Order integrates Stripe via an Anti-Corruption Layer so a Stripe SDK change never reaches my domain. Each bounded context becomes a service with its own model, DB, and team. Customer in Sales is a lead with deal pipeline; in Billing it's an account with invoice address and tax id — same word, different aggregates, synced by events. I only go tactical (aggregates, value objects) inside each context, after the boundary is right.

How do you decide aggregate boundaries?

By invariant, not by UML. An invariant is "what must be true after every transaction." If Order.total = sum(items) must always hold, then Order and OrderItems are one aggregate — they must be saved atomically. But OrderHistory or OrderAuditLog don't share an invariant with the order, so they're separate aggregates referenced by id. Rule of thumb: aggregates as small as possible, as large as necessary. Small aggregates mean less lock contention, easier sharding, and they let me split a service later without a rewrite.

What happens when a use case needs to change two aggregates?

I don't do it in one transaction. The use case modifies one aggregate, publishes a domain event, and the second aggregate reacts asynchronously. Concretely: Order confirms in TX1, emits OrderConfirmed via the outbox in the same TX. A handler picks it up, calls Inventory's ReserveStockUseCase in TX2. If reserve fails, a saga publishes OrderReservationFailed and Order reacts by cancelling — that's the compensation. Eventually consistent, but each step is local and idempotent. No 2PC, no XA, scale linearly.

Anemic vs rich domain model — what's the real cost of anemic?

The cost is invariants leaking. With an anemic Order, someone writes order.setStatus("PAID") in service A without checking payment, then service B sets it back to CONFIRMED ignoring a refund, and now you've got a row that violates business rules but passes every type check. Bugs of that shape are the worst to debug because no single place "owns" the rule. Rich model means order.markPaid(paymentRef) is the only way status reaches PAID, and it validates the precondition. The invariant lives with the data — that's the whole point.

When have you decided NOT to use DDD?

An internal admin tool — 8 CRUD screens, two roles, lifetime under a year. I used Spring Data + DTOs, no aggregates, no events. Adding DDD would have tripled the code for zero benefit. The signal is: if you can describe every business rule as "validate + save," you don't have a domain — you have a form. DDD is for when rules outlive any single screen and start contradicting each other across features. Use it where the domain actually has weight; don't use it as a religion.

How does DDD relate to the Outbox pattern from microservices?

They're complementary. DDD says "one aggregate per transaction; cross-aggregate consistency is eventual via domain events." Outbox is how you publish those events reliably. In the same TX that saves the aggregate, I insert a row into outbox_event with the domain event payload. A separate publisher reads outbox and writes to Kafka with the aggregate_id as the partition key — same aggregate, same partition, ordering preserved. Consumers must be idempotent because outbox is at-least-once. Without outbox, "save then publish" is two operations that can drift. Outbox makes the domain event a first-class part of the transaction.

12. System Design at Scale

Designing systems that scale, stay available under load, and degrade gracefully when a dependency fails.

#12
Key focus
Caching strategies
Concept

Caching keeps a copy of expensive-to-produce data in faster storage (in-process memory, Redis, CDN) so repeated reads skip the slow source.

Why it exists: Reads usually dominate traffic and the database is the scarcest resource. A cache cuts latency and offloads the source — the hard part is keeping it consistent and surviving misses under load.

Analogy: a cache is a sticky note on your desk: instant to read, but worthless (or harmful) if you forget to update it when the filing cabinet changes.

How it works
  1. Read path: check cache → hit returns immediately; miss → load from source, populate cache, return.
  2. TTL + eviction: entries expire by TTL; LRU/LFU eviction bounds memory when full.
  3. Write path: on update you either refresh the entry (write-through) or invalidate it (cache-aside).
  4. Miss storms: a single-flight lock or stale-while-revalidate stops thousands of threads recomputing the same key at once.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Cache-aside (lazy loading): the default. App reads cache, on miss loads DB and populates. Simple, but writes must invalidate or you serve stale data.
  2. Cache stampede / thundering herd: a hot key expires and 10k requests hit the DB simultaneously. Fix with a per-key lock (single-flight), stale-while-revalidate, or jittered TTLs.
  3. Write-through vs write-behind: write-through updates cache+DB synchronously (fresh, slower writes); write-behind buffers writes to DB async (fast, risk of loss on crash).
  4. Hot key: one celebrity key saturates a single Redis node → add a local near-cache (Caffeine) in front, or replicate the key.
  5. Cache penetration: repeated lookups for non-existent keys bypass the cache and hammer the DB → cache the negative result briefly or use a bloom filter.
// Cache-aside with single-flight to prevent stampede
public Product get(String id) {
    Product p = redis.get(id);
    if (p != null) return p;                 // hit
    return locks.computeIfAbsent(id, k -> {  // only one loader per key
        Product fresh = db.findById(k);
        redis.setex(k, ttlWithJitter(), fresh);
        return fresh;
    });
}

Effective caching is not "I put it in Redis." It is about the consistency tradeoff, the invalidation strategy, and what happens to the database the moment a popular key expires.

What to understand deeply
  • Cache-aside is the default; invalidation is the hard half

    Reading is trivial; the bug surface is writes. Decide per entity: invalidate on write, short TTL as a safety net, or write-through when staleness is unacceptable.

  • TTL with jitter + bounded size

    Always set a TTL (no TTL = stale forever and an unbounded memory leak). Add random jitter so keys do not all expire on the same second and cause a synchronized stampede.

  • Layered caching

    Browser/CDN → in-process (Caffeine, nanosecond, per-pod) → distributed (Redis, shared, network hop) → DB. Each layer cuts load on the next; near-cache absorbs hot keys.

  • Know what you must NOT cache stale

    Authz decisions, balances, inventory counts on the buy path — cache these only with very short TTL or not at all; serving a stale "in stock" oversells.

Production issue / pitfall
  • No TTL on entries

    Memory grows forever and data goes stale silently. Every entry needs an expiry plus an eviction policy.

  • Per-user data under a shared key

    Caching a response keyed only by URL when it varies by user leaks one user's data to another. Include the user/tenant in the key.

  • Caching huge blobs

    Serialization + network cost of a 2MB object can exceed re-computing it; cache small, hot, expensive-to-derive values.

  • Treating Redis as the source of truth

    Without a persistence/replication plan, a Redis restart drops everything. It is a cache; the DB owns the data.

Example
// Stampede-safe read with stale-while-revalidate
Product get(String id) {
    Entry e = redis.getEntry(id);
    if (e != null && !e.expired()) return e.value();          // fresh hit
    if (e != null && tryLock(id)) refreshAsync(id);            // serve stale, refresh in bg
    if (e != null) return e.value();                          // stale but fast
    return loadAndCache(id);                                  // cold miss
}
Interview answer tip: Lead with the consistency tradeoff and stampede handling. Say: 'I use cache-aside with jittered TTLs and a single-flight lock so a hot key expiring doesn't collapse onto the database.'
Key focus
Rate limiting and load balancing
Concept

Rate limiting caps how many requests a client may make per time window; load balancing spreads incoming requests across many backend instances. Both control and distribute load.

Why it exists: A service has finite capacity. Rate limiting protects it from abuse and overload (and enforces fairness/billing tiers); load balancing turns N instances into one scalable, fault-tolerant endpoint.

Analogy: rate limiting is the bouncer counting people per minute; the load balancer is the host seating them across many tables so no waiter is overwhelmed.

How it works
  1. LB picks a backend: round-robin, least-connections, or hash-based, and removes unhealthy instances via health checks.
  2. Limiter checks a counter for the client key (user/API-key/IP): under limit → allow and increment; over → reject with 429 + Retry-After.
  3. Distributed correctness: the counter lives in a shared store (Redis) so all instances enforce one global limit, not N separate ones.
  4. Backpressure: when overloaded, shed load fast (429) instead of queueing unboundedly until you run out of memory.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Token bucket vs leaky bucket: token bucket allows bursts up to bucket size then refills at a steady rate; leaky bucket forces a smooth constant outflow. Token bucket is the common API choice.
  2. Fixed-window boundary spike: a fixed 60s window lets a client send 2× the limit across the boundary (end of one window + start of next). Use a sliding window (log or weighted counter).
  3. Per-instance vs global: 10 instances each allowing 1000/min = 10000/min effective. Centralize counters in Redis with an atomic INCR+EXPIRE (or a Lua script).
  4. Consistent hashing: for sharded caches/LBs, hashing keys onto a ring (with virtual nodes) means adding/removing a node remaps only ~1/N keys instead of all of them.
  5. L4 vs L7: L4 balances TCP (fast, opaque); L7 understands HTTP so it can route by path/header, terminate TLS, and retry idempotent requests.
-- Atomic sliding-window-ish token bucket in Redis (Lua, runs server-side)
-- KEYS[1]=bucket key  ARGV: rate, capacity, now, requested
local tokens = tonumber(redis.call('hget', KEYS[1], 'tokens') or ARGV[2])
local last   = tonumber(redis.call('hget', KEYS[1], 'ts') or ARGV[3])
tokens = math.min(ARGV[2], tokens + (ARGV[3]-last)*ARGV[1])   -- refill
if tokens < tonumber(ARGV[4]) then return 0 end               -- reject -> 429
redis.call('hmset', KEYS[1], 'tokens', tokens-ARGV[4], 'ts', ARGV[3])
return 1

the key signal here is distributed correctness (one global limit across instances) and naming the algorithm tradeoff — not just adding a Spring filter.

What to understand deeply
  • Token bucket is rate + burst

    Two parameters: refill rate (steady-state) and capacity (max burst). Implement it atomically in Redis so concurrent requests can't double-spend tokens.

  • Respond with 429 + Retry-After + limit headers

    Tell clients how long to wait and their remaining quota (X-RateLimit-Remaining). Good clients back off; you avoid retry storms.

  • Consistent hashing for scale-out

    A hash ring with virtual nodes keeps key→node mapping stable as the fleet changes, so a deploy doesn't cold-miss the entire cache.

  • Health checks + connection draining

    The LB must detect a dead instance fast and drain in-flight connections on deploy so you don't drop live requests.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Rate limiting per instance, not globally

    Each pod counts independently, so the real limit is N× what you intended. Use a shared store.

  • Keying limits by IP behind a proxy/NAT

    Thousands of users share one egress IP; you throttle them all. Limit by authenticated user/API key, and read the real client IP from trusted headers.

  • No backpressure / unbounded queue

    Accepting everything and queueing leads to OOM and cascading timeouts. Shed load with 429 once saturated.

  • Retries without jitter

    Synchronized client retries after a blip create a thundering-herd retry storm. Use exponential backoff + jitter (and a circuit breaker).

Example
// App-side guard backed by the Redis bucket
if (!rateLimiter.tryAcquire(apiKey, cost)) {
    return ResponseEntity.status(429)
        .header("Retry-After", "2")
        .header("X-RateLimit-Remaining", "0")
        .body(Problem.of("rate_limited", "Too many requests"));
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I'd use a token bucket in Redis so the limit is global across instances, return 429 with Retry-After, and limit by API key not IP. For the LB, L7 with health checks and consistent hashing for the cache tier.'
Key focus
Scaling data: replication, sharding, CAP
Concept

When one database node hits its ceiling you scale state with replication (copies for reads and failover) and sharding/partitioning (splitting data across nodes by a key). CAP says that during a network partition you must choose consistency or availability.

Why it exists: Stateless app servers scale trivially by adding instances; data does not, because it must stay consistent. Scaling the data tier is the hardest and most failure-prone part of scaling a system.

Analogy: replication is photocopying the ledger so many people can read it; sharding is splitting the ledger by account range so no single book gets too heavy to write.

How it works
  1. Scale up first: a bigger box is the simplest win and has a real ceiling — reach for it before distributing.
  2. Read replicas: async copies absorb read traffic; the cost is replica lag (a replica is slightly behind the primary).
  3. Shard writes: split rows across nodes by a shard key (hash or range); each shard owns a slice of the data and its writes.
  4. Route + fan-out: a router sends each query to the owning shard; queries spanning shards need scatter-gather and become expensive.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Read-heavy workload: add read replicas and route reads there — but a user who just wrote may read their own stale data from a lagging replica. Route read-your-writes to the primary or pin briefly.
  2. Write-heavy workload: shard by tenant/user id to spread writes; the shard key choice makes or breaks it.
  3. Hot shard (celebrity problem): one giant tenant overloads its shard while others idle → sub-partition that tenant or give it a dedicated shard.
  4. Rebalancing on growth: hash mod N remaps almost everything when N changes → use consistent hashing or pre-split key ranges.
  5. Cross-shard transactions: avoid them; model so each transaction stays in one shard, and use saga/outbox for multi-shard workflows (see Microservices).
// Shard router: same key -> same shard, read/write split
int shard = Math.floorMod(consistentHash(tenantId), SHARDS);
DataSource ds = write ? primaries[shard] : replicas[shard].pickOne();
// cross-shard report = scatter-gather over all shards, then merge (expensive)

Don't hand-wave 'just add a database.' Talk about replica lag, shard-key selection, and what happens when you need to add a shard.

What to understand deeply
  • Replication: sync vs async, lag, failover

    Sync replication is durable but slow; async is fast but loses recent writes on failover and exposes replica lag. Know read-your-writes consistency.

  • Partitioning: hash vs range

    Hash spreads evenly but kills range scans; range keeps scans cheap but creates hotspots (e.g., everything 'today'). Pick per query pattern.

  • CAP / PACELC in practice

    Under a partition: CP (reject writes to stay consistent) or AP (accept, reconcile later). PACELC adds: even without a partition, you trade latency vs consistency.

  • Shard key selection

    High cardinality, even distribution, and aligned with your most common query so you avoid cross-shard fan-out.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Sharding too early

    Distributed data is a huge operational tax. Exhaust vertical scaling and read replicas first.

  • A bad shard key

    Low cardinality or skewed keys create hot shards and force cross-shard queries — the worst of both worlds.

  • Read-after-write on a replica

    Writing to primary then reading from a lagging replica returns stale data; a classic 'it works on my machine' production bug.

  • 2PC across shards

    Distributed two-phase commit is slow and locks across the network; prefer eventual consistency via events.

Example
// Read-your-writes: pin recent writers to the primary
boolean recentlyWrote = writeMarker.exists(userId);   // short TTL flag
DataSource ds = recentlyWrote ? primary : replica.pickOne();
return jdbc(ds).query(...);
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Vertical scale, then read replicas (mind the lag and read-your-writes), then shard by a high-cardinality key aligned to my queries, using consistent hashing so adding a shard doesn't remap everything.'
Key focus
Worked design: a URL shortener (the method)
Concept

A system design round asks you to design a service end to end under scale. What is graded is the method: clarify requirements, estimate scale, define API and data, sketch components, deep-dive the hard part, and discuss tradeoffs.

Why it exists: It tests whether you can reason about scale, consistency, and failure — and drive an ambiguous problem — not whether you memorized one architecture diagram.

Analogy: it's like an architect interview: they don't want a pre-drawn house, they want to watch you ask about the plot, budget, and soil before you draw.

How it works
  1. Requirements: functional (shorten, redirect, custom alias, analytics) and non-functional (QPS, latency, durability, read:write ratio).
  2. Estimate: back-of-envelope — writes/day, storage/year, read QPS — so every later decision (cache, shard) is justified by a number.
  3. API + data model: a couple of endpoints and the table/columns.
  4. Components → deep-dive → tradeoffs: LB, app, cache, DB, queue; then attack the hard part (id generation, hot reads) and name what you traded.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. ID generation: base62 of an auto-increment (short, sequential, guessable), Snowflake id (distributed, time-ordered), or random+collision-check (unguessable, needs a uniqueness check). State the tradeoff.
  2. Read:write is ~100:1: redirects dominate → cache hot short-codes in Redis and at the CDN edge; the DB is the source of truth, not the hot path.
  3. Storage estimate: 100M writes/day × ~500B ≈ 50GB/day → shard by hash of the short code.
  4. Custom alias collisions: enforce a unique constraint and return 409 on conflict.
  5. Analytics (click counts): never increment a counter synchronously on the redirect path — emit an event to Kafka and aggregate asynchronously.
  6. Expiry/cleanup: TTL column + a background job to purge expired links.
POST /urls            {url, alias?, ttl?} -> 201 {shortCode}
GET  /{shortCode}     -> 301 Location: <longUrl>   (served from cache)

// id generation (sketch): sequential id -> base62, unguessable variant adds salt
String code = base62(idGen.next());          // e.g. 7 chars covers ~3.5T urls
// hot read path: redis.get(code) -> fallback db -> populate; analytics via Kafka

Drive the conversation. The candidates who fail are the ones who jump straight to boxes-and-arrows without clarifying requirements or estimating scale.

What to understand deeply
  • Requirements + estimation discipline

    Pin the read:write ratio and QPS early; these numbers justify every component. 'Read-heavy 100:1' is why you add a cache — say it.

  • ID generation tradeoffs

    Sequential is short but enumerable; Snowflake is distributed and ordered; random needs a collision check. Pick and defend one.

    long id = (epochMillis << 22) | (nodeId << 12) | seq;   // Snowflake-style 64-bit id
  • Split read and write paths

    Reads: cache + CDN, stateless app, replicated store. Writes: durable DB + async analytics off the hot path.

  • Failure + scaling story

    Stateless app behind an LB (scale horizontally), sharded DB, replicated cache, no single point of failure.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Drawing before clarifying

    Designing without requirements means you design the wrong system. Spend the first minutes on questions.

  • Skipping estimation

    Without numbers you can't justify a cache, a shard, or a queue — it reads as cargo-culting.

  • Synchronous analytics on the hot path

    A DB write per redirect destroys your read throughput; make it async.

  • No failure discussion

    Not naming single points of failure or what happens when the cache/DB/region dies is an automatic advanced-level ding.

Example
// Capacity sketch you'd say out loud:
// 100M writes/day  -> ~1.2k write QPS, ~120k read QPS (100:1)
// 50GB/day -> ~18TB/yr -> shard by hash(shortCode)
// redirect p99 target < 50ms -> Redis + CDN, DB only on miss
Interview answer tip: Narrate the structure explicitly: requirements → estimate → API/data → components → deep-dive → tradeoffs. Interviewers grade the journey far more than the final diagram.
Deep dive — System Design at Scale
War story — the Black Friday cache stampede

Symptom: At 00:00 the homepage went down for 6 minutes; the database showed 100% CPU and a wall of 'too many connections'.

Cause: The hot 'featured products' key had a flat 5-minute TTL. It expired exactly as a marketing push hit, and ~40k req/s all missed the cache and slammed Postgres at once — a textbook stampede.

Fix: Single-flight lock per key so only one request rebuilds it, stale-while-revalidate so others serve the slightly old value, and TTL jitter so keys never expire in lockstep again.

Lesson: A cache miss is a load amplifier. The dangerous moment isn't steady state — it's the instant a popular key expires under load.

// Before:  on miss -> everyone queries DB
// After:
Product featured() {
    var e = redis.getEntry(KEY);
    if (e != null && !e.expired()) return e.value();
    if (e != null) { if (tryLock(KEY)) refreshAsync(KEY); return e.value(); } // serve stale
    return computeUnderLock(KEY);   // cold start: single loader only
}
Interview scenario — "Design a rate limiter for our public API"

Design a rate limiter for our public API. We run a dozen instances behind a load balancer.

First the requirements: per-API-key limits with tiers (say 1000 req/min free, higher for paid), enforced globally across all instances, and it must fail fast — a limited request should cost almost nothing. I'd use a token bucket: a refill rate plus a burst capacity, because APIs want to allow short bursts but bound the sustained rate.

Why not just count requests per instance?

Because a dozen instances each counting locally means the real limit is twelve times what I intended, and it drifts as we autoscale. The counter has to be shared. I'd put it in Redis and do the check-and-decrement atomically in a Lua script so concurrent requests on the same key can't double-spend tokens. One network round-trip, server-side atomicity.

What does the client see when throttled?

A 429 with Retry-After and X-RateLimit-Remaining headers so well-behaved clients back off instead of hammering. And I'd add jitter to the retry guidance — if everyone retries at exactly Retry-After we just recreate the spike. For abusive clients past a threshold I'd shed at the edge/L7 before it even reaches the app.

Single point of failure — what if Redis is down?

I fail open with a conservative per-instance fallback limit, not closed — rate limiting protects the service but shouldn't take it down itself. I'd also run Redis with replication and keep the limiter logic tolerant of a brief blip. The tradeoff is explicit: during a Redis outage limits are approximate, which I'd rather accept than reject all traffic.

13. Modern Java (Loom, Records, Sealed)

Java 17-21+ features that change how you write backend code: virtual threads, records, sealed types, pattern matching, and functional style done right.

#13
Key focus
Virtual threads (Project Loom, Java 21)
Concept

Virtual threads are lightweight threads scheduled by the JVM rather than the OS. You can run millions of them; when one blocks on I/O the JVM parks it and frees the underlying OS (carrier) thread for other work.

Why it exists: The classic thread-per-request model is easy to read but capped by OS threads (a few thousand). Loom keeps that simple blocking style while scaling to massive concurrency — without rewriting everything in reactive/async callbacks.

Analogy: virtual threads are green threads done right: the JVM is the scheduler, and a platform thread is just a CPU core to borrow while you're actually running.

How it works
  1. You start one via Thread.ofVirtual() or Executors.newVirtualThreadPerTaskExecutor().
  2. It mounts on a carrier (platform) thread to execute.
  3. On blocking I/O the JVM unmounts it and parks it — the carrier is immediately free to run another virtual thread.
  4. On completion it remounts on any free carrier and resumes. Net effect: blocking code scales like async, no callbacks.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Thread-per-request servers: Tomcat/Jetty on virtual threads handle huge concurrency without a large fixed pool — each request gets its own cheap thread.
  2. Pinning: inside a synchronized block or a native call the virtual thread cannot unmount, so it pins the carrier and blocks it. Replace synchronized around I/O with ReentrantLock.
  3. Don't pool them: virtual threads are cheap and disposable. Use a per-task executor, not a fixed pool — pooling defeats the purpose.
  4. ThreadLocal blowup: millions of virtual threads each holding ThreadLocals costs real memory; prefer scoped values or pass context explicitly.
  5. CPU-bound work doesn't benefit: Loom helps I/O-bound concurrency; CPU work is still limited by cores. Don't expect speedups for number-crunching.
  6. Bounding a dependency: virtual threads remove the natural pool limit, so cap calls to a fragile downstream with a Semaphore.
// One cheap thread per task; blocking calls are fine here
try (var exec = Executors.newVirtualThreadPerTaskExecutor()) {
    for (var id : ids) exec.submit(() -> client.fetch(id));   // millions OK
}
// Cap a fragile downstream even though threads are unlimited
Semaphore gate = new Semaphore(50);
gate.acquire(); try { return paymentApi.charge(req); } finally { gate.release(); }

Virtual threads are not 'faster threads.' The key story is mounting/unmounting, pinning, and the fact that you stop managing pool sizes and start managing downstream concurrency.

What to understand deeply
  • Carriers, mounting, unmounting

    A small pool of platform carriers runs many virtual threads. Blocking unmounts; the scheduler keeps carriers busy. This is why blocking code suddenly scales.

  • Pinning: causes and fix

    synchronized and native frames pin the VT to its carrier. Around blocking I/O, switch to ReentrantLock so the VT can unmount.

    // Pins the carrier during I/O:
synchronized (lock) { remote.call(); }
// Does not pin:
lock.lock(); try { remote.call(); } finally { lock.unlock(); }
  • Structured concurrency for fan-out

    StructuredTaskScope makes parallel sub-tasks a unit: all succeed, or one failure cancels the rest, with a deadline.

    try (var scope = new StructuredTaskScope.ShutdownOnFailure()) {
    var u = scope.fork(() -> userClient.find(id));
    var o = scope.fork(() -> orderClient.recent(id));
    scope.joinUntil(Instant.now().plusMillis(800)); scope.throwIfFailed();
    return new Dashboard(u.get(), o.get());
}
  • Scoped values over ThreadLocal

    ScopedValue passes context (traceId, principal) down the call tree immutably without per-thread ThreadLocal memory cost.

Production issue / pitfall
  • synchronized around blocking I/O

    Pins carriers; under load carriers starve and throughput collapses — the opposite of what you migrated for.

  • Pooling virtual threads

    A fixed virtual-thread pool reintroduces the limit you were trying to remove. Use newVirtualThreadPerTaskExecutor.

  • Expecting CPU speedups

    Virtual threads add concurrency, not cores. CPU-bound code gains nothing; it may even regress from scheduling overhead.

  • Unbounded fan-out

    Without a Semaphore, a spike spawns unbounded concurrent calls and overwhelms a downstream that has its own limits.

Example
// Fan-out with deadline + bounded downstream
try (var scope = new StructuredTaskScope.ShutdownOnFailure()) {
    Subtask<User>  u = scope.fork(() -> userClient.find(id));
    Subtask<Score> s = scope.fork(() -> scoreClient.fetch(id));
    scope.joinUntil(Instant.now().plusSeconds(1));   // overall timeout
    scope.throwIfFailed();
    return new Dashboard(u.get(), s.get());
}
Interview answer tip: Explain mounting/unmounting and name pinning (synchronized/native) plus its fix (ReentrantLock). Then add: 'I stop sizing thread pools and instead bound downstream concurrency with a semaphore.'
Key focus
Records and sealed classes
Concept

A record is an immutable, transparent data carrier — the compiler generates the constructor, accessors, and value-based equals/hashCode/toString from its components. A sealed type restricts exactly which classes may implement or extend it.
