Why it exists: Records kill the boilerplate of value objects and DTOs and give correct equality for free. Sealed types let the compiler know the complete set of subtypes, which enables exhaustive switch and lets you model a closed set of cases safely (algebraic data types).

Analogy: a record is a labeled tuple; a sealed interface is a closed menu — the compiler knows every dish, so it can warn you when you forget to handle one.

How it works
  1. Declare a record record Money(long amount, String currency){} → final fields, canonical constructor, accessors, value equality, toString — all generated.
  2. Add a compact constructor to validate or normalize before the fields are set.
  3. Declare a sealed type sealed interface PaymentResult permits Approved, Declined, Error.
  4. switch becomes exhaustive over the sealed type — the compiler errors if you miss a permitted case, so adding a case forces you to handle it everywhere.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. DTO / API response: a record is concise and immutable — perfect for request/response payloads.
  2. Value object (DDD): Money, Address, EmailAddress as records with a compact constructor that enforces invariants at creation.
  3. Closed set of outcomes/states: model PaymentResult = Approved | Declined | Error as a sealed interface of records.
  4. Exhaustive handling: switch over the sealed type with no default; when a new case is added the compiler flags every switch that must change.
  5. Not for JPA entities: Hibernate needs a no-arg constructor and mutability/proxies — keep records for DTOs and value objects, use classes for @Entity.
public record Money(long minor, String currency) {
    public Money {                                   // compact constructor
        if (minor < 0) throw new IllegalArgumentException("negative");
        currency = currency.toUpperCase();           // normalize
    }
}
sealed interface PaymentResult permits Approved, Declined, Error {}
record Approved(String ref) implements PaymentResult {}
record Declined(String reason) implements PaymentResult {}
record Error(Throwable cause) implements PaymentResult {}

Tie records to value objects and DTOs, and sealed types to modeling a closed domain so the compiler enforces exhaustiveness — that's the design-level answer.

What to understand deeply
  • Record semantics

    Components define final fields + accessors + value equals/hashCode. The compact constructor validates/normalizes; the canonical constructor is generated.

  • Sealed = algebraic data types

    permits lists every subtype, so a switch can be exhaustive. This turns 'forgot a case' from a runtime bug into a compile error.

  • Records + pattern matching

    Records deconstruct in switch/instanceof, so you read fields directly: case Approved(var ref) -> ...

  • When NOT to use a record

    Mutable state, JPA @Entity, or a framework that needs setters/no-arg ctor — use a normal class.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Record as a JPA @Entity

    Hibernate can't proxy/populate an immutable record with no setters — mapping breaks or behaves oddly. Use a class for entities.

  • Mutable components

    record Team(List<Player> players) is not truly immutable — callers can mutate the list. Defensively copy in the compact constructor.

  • Forcing behavior/mutation into records

    Records model values, not lifecycle. If it needs to change state over time, it's not a record.

Example
PaymentResult r = gateway.charge(payment);
String msg = switch (r) {                            // exhaustive, no default
    case Approved a -> "OK " + a.ref();
    case Declined d -> "Declined: " + d.reason();
    case Error e    -> "Retry: " + e.cause().getMessage();
};
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Records for DTOs and value objects (immutability + correct equals for free), sealed interfaces to model a closed set of outcomes so my switch is compiler-checked exhaustive.'
Key focus
Pattern matching and switch expressions
Concept

Pattern matching tests a type and binds a variable in one step; switch became an expression that returns a value and can match on types, deconstruct records, and apply guards.

Why it exists: It replaces verbose instanceof-and-cast chains and long if/else ladders with concise, exhaustive, expression-style code — eliminating cast errors and switch fall-through bugs.

Analogy: instead of 'check the label, then carefully unwrap the box,' pattern matching unwraps the box the moment the label matches.

How it works
  1. instanceof pattern: if (o instanceof Order ord) binds ord with no cast.
  2. switch as an expression: var x = switch(v){ ... } returns a value; arms use -> with no fall-through.
  3. type patterns in switch: case Circle c -> ... dispatch on subtype.
  4. record deconstruction + guards: case Point(int x, int y) when x == y -> ... matches shape and condition together.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Replacing instanceof + cast ladders: the pattern binds the variable, so the cast (and its ClassCastException risk) disappears.
  2. Type dispatch over a sealed type: a switch is exhaustive and self-documenting, replacing an if/else-if chain.
  3. Deconstructing records: pull fields out directly in the case label instead of calling accessors inside the body.
  4. Killing fall-through bugs: arrow switch has no implicit fall-through, so a forgotten break can't cause a wrong branch.
  5. Mapping domain events to actions: a switch expression returns the result, keeping the mapping in one readable place.
Object o = ...;
String s = switch (o) {
    case Integer i when i > 0 -> "positive int " + i;   // guard
    case Integer i            -> "non-positive " + i;
    case String str           -> "text " + str.length();
    case Point(int x, int y)  -> "point " + (x + y);      // record deconstruction
    case null                 -> "null";
    default                   -> "other";
};

Pair pattern matching with sealed types: that combination is what gives you compiler-checked exhaustiveness, which is the real win over old switch.

What to understand deeply
  • instanceof pattern binds, no cast

    One construct tests and binds, so you never write the redundant cast that could throw.

    if (event instanceof OrderPaid p) ledger.credit(p.amount());
  • switch expression vs statement

    An expression returns a value (use yield for blocks) and the compiler checks exhaustiveness over sealed types.

  • Record deconstruction patterns

    Match structure and extract components in one label: case Result(var code, var msg) -> ...

  • Guarded patterns (when)

    Refine a type match with a boolean condition for fine-grained branching without nested ifs.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Assuming exhaustiveness on non-sealed types

    Only sealed hierarchies (and enums) get compiler-checked exhaustiveness; otherwise you still need a default.

  • Pattern matching where polymorphism is cleaner

    If behavior naturally belongs on the type, a virtual method can beat a big external switch. Choose deliberately.

  • Side effects inside switch-expression arms

    An expression should compute a value; hiding mutations in arms makes it hard to reason about.

Example
sealed interface Event permits Created, Paid, Cancelled {}
String audit(Event e) {
    return switch (e) {                              // exhaustive over sealed
        case Created c   -> "created " + c.id();
        case Paid p      -> "paid " + p.amount();
        case Cancelled c -> "cancelled " + c.reason();
    };
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'instanceof patterns remove casts, switch expressions remove fall-through, and over a sealed type the compiler forces me to handle every case — adding a new event type fails the build until I handle it.'
Key focus
Streams, Optional and functional, done right
Concept

Streams express data transformations declaratively (filter/map/reduce); Optional models a value that may be absent so you avoid null. Both are powerful and both are commonly misused.

Why it exists: Used well, pipelines read like the intent and absence is explicit instead of a lurking NullPointerException. The key skill is knowing the limits — when a plain loop or a batch is clearer or faster.

Analogy: a stream is an assembly line (lazy stages, one terminal product); Optional is a box that is honestly labeled 'might be empty' so you never blindly reach in.

How it works
  1. Source → intermediate → terminal: a collection/stream feeds lazy ops (filter, map) that do nothing until a terminal op (collect, reduce, findFirst) pulls the data through.
  2. Laziness + short-circuiting: findFirst/anyMatch stop early; nothing runs without a terminal op.
  3. Optional chains: wrap a nullable, then map/filter, and end with orElse/orElseThrow to get a value or a default.
  4. Collectors shape the result: groupingBy, partitioningBy, toMap (with a merge function for duplicate keys).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Transform/aggregate collections: filter+map+collect with groupingBy/toMap reads better than a manual loop with accumulator maps.
  2. Express 'may be missing': return Optional<User> instead of null — but never as a field or method parameter.
  3. Parallel streams: only for large, CPU-bound, splittable data; never for blocking I/O (it floods the shared common ForkJoinPool).
  4. N+1 hidden in a stream: a DB/remote call inside map() runs once per element — batch the calls outside the stream instead.
  5. Don't force it: a simple counting loop or early-return search is often clearer than a contorted stream; readability wins.
Map<String, List<Order>> byStatus = orders.stream()
    .filter(o -> o.total() > 0)
    .collect(Collectors.groupingBy(Order::status));

String name = findUser(id)                 // returns Optional<User>
    .map(User::name)
    .orElse("anonymous");                  // no NPE, explicit default

The differentiator is restraint: show you know when streams and Optional help and when they hurt, not that you can chain ten operators.

What to understand deeply
  • Lazy vs terminal, short-circuiting

    Intermediate ops are lazy and fuse; the terminal op drives execution. findFirst/anyMatch can stop early — useful for large sources.

  • Collectors you actually need

    groupingBy, partitioningBy, and toMap with a merge function (duplicate keys throw without one).

    // duplicate keys -> supply a merge function, else IllegalStateException
Map<String,Order> latest = orders.stream()
    .collect(Collectors.toMap(Order::customer, o -> o, (a,b) -> newer(a,b)));
  • Optional rules

    Use it as a return type only. Never an Optional field or parameter; never call get() without checking — use orElseThrow.

    Optional<User> findUser(Id id);                 // good: return type
user.flatMap(User::manager).map(User::email).orElseThrow();
  • When NOT to stream

    Hot-path performance, heavy side effects, or a trivial loop — a for-loop is clearer and sometimes faster.

Production issue / pitfall
  • Optional as a field or parameter

    It's meant for return values; as a field it adds overhead and breaks serialization, as a param it just shifts the null check.

  • parallelStream() with blocking I/O

    It runs on the shared common pool and starves the whole JVM's parallel work; use a dedicated executor or virtual threads instead.

  • Stateful lambdas / side effects in map()

    Mutating shared state inside a pipeline breaks under parallelism and is hard to debug; keep stages pure.

  • toMap without a merge function

    Duplicate keys throw IllegalStateException at runtime — always provide a merge function when collisions are possible.

Example
// Batch instead of N+1 inside a stream
Set<Long> ids = orders.stream().map(Order::customerId).collect(toSet());
Map<Long,Customer> cs = customerRepo.findAllById(ids).stream()   // one query
    .collect(toMap(Customer::id, c -> c));
orders.forEach(o -> enrich(o, cs.get(o.customerId())));
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Streams for declarative transforms, Optional as a return type for absence — but I drop to a loop on hot paths, batch to avoid N+1, and never run blocking work on a parallel stream.'
Deep dive — Modern Java
War story — virtual threads that didn't get faster

Symptom: We migrated a gateway service to virtual threads expecting higher throughput. Instead p99 latency got worse under load and throughput barely moved.

Cause: A shared response cache used a synchronized block, and inside it we made a remote call on a miss. synchronized pins the virtual thread to its carrier, so under load the handful of carriers were all pinned waiting on I/O — carrier starvation.

Fix: Replaced synchronized with a ReentrantLock and moved the remote call outside the lock, then bounded the downstream with a Semaphore. Carriers could unmount on I/O again; throughput went up ~4×.

Lesson: Virtual threads only win if blocking calls can actually unmount. synchronized and native frames pin them — audit hot locks before declaring victory.

// Before (pins carrier on the miss path):
synchronized (cacheLock) { return cache.computeIfAbsent(k, x -> remote.fetch(x)); }
// After (no pinning; remote call outside the lock):
var v = cache.get(k);
if (v == null) { v = remote.fetch(k); lock.lock(); try { cache.putIfAbsent(k, v); } finally { lock.unlock(); } }
return v;
Interview scenario — "Virtual threads or reactive (WebFlux)?"

When would you reach for virtual threads versus a reactive stack like WebFlux?

Default to virtual threads for I/O-bound, request-per-task services — they give you reactive-level scalability while keeping plain blocking, debuggable, top-to-bottom code with normal stack traces and try/finally. Reactive earns its complexity when I genuinely need streaming backpressure, compose a lot of async event sources, or I'm on a stack already built around it.

So is reactive obsolete now?

No — but its main selling point, scaling a thread-per-request model past the OS-thread ceiling, is now available without the cognitive cost. Reactive still shines for true stream processing and end-to-end backpressure. For typical CRUD-plus-remote-calls microservices, virtual threads are simpler and just as scalable, and simpler is a feature.

What's the catch with virtual threads in production?

Pinning, mostly. synchronized around blocking I/O or native calls pins the carrier and quietly kills the scalability you migrated for. So I audit hot locks and switch them to ReentrantLock, I don't pool virtual threads, and because they remove the natural pool limit I add a Semaphore to bound calls to any fragile downstream. I also watch for ThreadLocal-heavy libraries and prefer scoped values.

How would you roll it out safely?

Behind a flag, one service first, with load tests comparing throughput and p99 before and after. I'd add a JFR/jstack check for pinned events under load. If pinning shows up I fix the lock and re-test. It's a low-risk change precisely because the programming model stays the same — the risk is concentrated in a few locks, not the whole codebase.

14. Coding Test / Algorithms (DSA)

For the coding-test round. Do not memorize 500 LeetCode problems — learn the handful of patterns that 80% of interview questions reduce to, and how to talk through complexity.

#14
Start here
Complexity & a problem-solving framework
Concept

Big-O describes how runtime/memory grows as input size n grows — it ignores constants and small terms, and answers "does this still work when n is a million?".

Why it exists: The coding round is rarely about getting an answer; it is about getting an efficient one and being able to justify it. Interviewers grade your reasoning out loud as much as the final code.

Analogy: finding a name in a phone book — flipping page by page is O(n); opening to the middle and halving each time is O(log n).

How to approach any problem (say this out loud)
  1. Clarify: input size, value range, duplicates, sorted or not, empty/negative cases. Repeat the problem back.
  2. Brute force first: state the naive O(n²)/O(2ⁿ) solution so you have a baseline and never freeze.
  3. Find the bottleneck: which step is expensive? That points at the pattern (hash to avoid a scan, two pointers to avoid nested loops, sort to enable binary search).
  4. Pick a data structure: the right structure usually is the algorithm (HashMap, heap, stack, set).
  5. Walk a tiny example, then code, then test edge cases: empty, single element, all-equal, max size.
Complexity cheat-sheet for common operations
  1. HashMap / HashSet get/put/contains: O(1) average — the workhorse for turning a scan into a lookup.
  2. Sort: O(n log n) — often worth paying once to unlock two pointers or binary search.
  3. Binary search on a sorted array: O(log n).
  4. Heap (PriorityQueue) push/pop: O(log n); peek O(1) — for top-K and streaming min/max.
  5. ArrayList get by index: O(1); contains / remove from middle: O(n). LinkedList random access: O(n).
  6. Nested loop over n: O(n²) — fine for n ≤ ~1000, a red flag for n ≥ 10⁵.
What to understand deeply
  • Read n to guess the target complexity

    n ≤ 20 → exponential/backtracking is fine. n ≤ 10³ → O(n²). n ≤ 10⁵–10⁶ → you need O(n log n) or O(n). n ≥ 10⁸ → O(n) or O(log n) only.

  • Time vs space trade-off

    Most "make it faster" moves spend memory (a HashMap, a prefix-sum array, memoization) to delete a loop. Name the trade-off explicitly.

  • Amortized vs worst case

    ArrayList add is amortized O(1) but a single resize is O(n); HashMap is O(1) average but O(n) if every key collides.

Common pitfalls in the test
  • Jumping straight to code

    Silence then a wrong solution scores worse than a narrated brute force you then optimize.

  • Ignoring integer overflow

    int overflows around 2.1×10⁹. Use long for sums/products, and write lo + (hi - lo) / 2 not (lo + hi) / 2.

  • Off-by-one and empty input

    The classic bug. Always test n = 0 and n = 1 before saying "done".

Interview answer tip: "My brute force is O(n²) because of the nested scan. The bottleneck is the inner lookup, so I'll trade O(n) memory for a HashMap and bring it to O(n) time." Naming brute force → bottleneck → trade-off is the key signal.
Top pattern
Two pointers & sliding window
Concept

Two indices walking an array/string instead of a nested loop. A sliding window is the two-pointer variant where the pair bounds a contiguous range that grows and shrinks.

Why it exists: It collapses an O(n²) "check every pair / every subarray" into O(n) when the data is sorted or the property is monotonic (extending the window can only help, shrinking can only fix a violation).

How it works
  1. Opposite ends: on a sorted array, move left++ when the sum is too small, right-- when too big (two-sum, container-with-most-water, palindrome check).
  2. Fast/slow: same direction at different speeds (cycle detection, removing duplicates in place, finding the middle of a list).
  3. Sliding window: expand right to grow, shrink left while the window is invalid; track the best window seen (longest substring without repeats, min subarray ≥ target).
When to reach for it
  • "Subarray / substring" + "contiguous"

    Almost always a sliding window. "Longest", "shortest", "at most K distinct", "sum equals" are tells.

  • Sorted array + "pair/triplet with sum"

    Opposite-end two pointers; for triplets, fix one element and two-point the rest → O(n²).

Pitfalls
  • Window only works for non-negative contributions

    "Subarray sum = k" with negatives needs a prefix-sum + HashMap, not a window — shrinking is no longer safe.

  • Forgetting to update the answer at the right moment

    For "longest valid" update after expanding; for "shortest valid" update after each shrink.

Example — longest substring without repeating characters (O(n))
int longestUnique(String s) {
  Map<Character,Integer> last = new HashMap<>();
  int left = 0, best = 0;
  for (int right = 0; right < s.length(); right++) {
    char c = s.charAt(right);
    if (last.containsKey(c) && last.get(c) >= left) {
      left = last.get(c) + 1;          // shrink past the duplicate
    }
    last.put(c, right);
    best = Math.max(best, right - left + 1);
  }
  return best;
}
Interview answer tip: "It's a contiguous-substring problem, so I'll slide a window: expand right, and when a character repeats I jump left past its last position. Each index is visited at most twice → O(n)."
Top pattern
Hashing — turn a scan into a lookup
Concept

A HashMap/HashSet gives O(1) average membership and counting. The pattern: while scanning once, ask "have I seen the complement / this value / this prefix before?".

Why it exists: It is the single most common way to drop a nested loop. The same idea ties directly to the Java-Core HashMap topic — a good equals/hashCode is what makes it O(1).

How it works
  1. Complement lookup: Two-Sum — for each x, check if target - x was already seen.
  2. Frequency map: count occurrences for anagrams, majority element, top-K, first non-repeating char.
  3. Seen-set: detect duplicates, cycle in a linked structure, or visited nodes in a graph.
  4. Prefix-sum + map: count/locate subarrays summing to k even with negatives — store running sum → index/count.
What to understand deeply
  • Group-by key design

    Anagrams group by sorted string or a 26-count signature; "lines on the same slope" group by a reduced fraction key.

  • getOrDefault / merge / computeIfAbsent

    map.merge(k, 1, Integer::sum) is the idiomatic frequency counter; computeIfAbsent(k, x -> new ArrayList<>()) for adjacency lists.

Pitfalls
  • Mutable keys

    Mutating an object after using it as a key corrupts the map — keys must be immutable in the equals/hashCode fields.

  • Autoboxing surprises

    Integer caches only -128..127; never compare boxed values with ==. Use .equals or primitives.

Example — Two Sum in O(n)
int[] twoSum(int[] nums, int target) {
  Map<Integer,Integer> seen = new HashMap<>();   // value -> index
  for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
    Integer j = seen.get(target - nums[i]);
    if (j != null) return new int[]{j, i};
    seen.put(nums[i], i);
  }
  return new int[0];
}
Interview answer tip: "Brute force checks every pair in O(n²). Instead I scan once and remember each value in a HashMap, asking if the complement is already there — O(n) time, O(n) space."
Top pattern
Binary search (on arrays and on the answer)
Concept

Repeatedly halve a sorted/monotonic search space until one candidate remains — O(log n). The deeper version: binary-search the answer when you can cheaply check "is X feasible?".

Why it exists: log n is dramatically faster than n for large inputs, and many "minimize the maximum / find the smallest X that works" problems hide a monotonic predicate.

How it works
  1. Classic: lo=0, hi=n-1; compare mid to target, discard half each step.
  2. Lower/upper bound: find the first index ≥ target (insertion point) or last ≤ target — the basis of Arrays.binarySearch, TreeMap.ceilingKey/floorKey.
  3. Binary search on answer: guess a value, run an O(n) feasibility check, move lo/hi based on the result (e.g. "min days to ship", "min eating speed").
When to reach for it
  • Data is sorted, or you can sort it

    Or it's rotated-sorted, or the value space is monotonic ("smallest capacity such that we finish in time").

  • Use the library when allowed

    Collections.binarySearch, TreeMap/TreeSet give floor/ceiling in O(log n) for free.

Pitfalls
  • Infinite loops

    Wrong boundary updates (lo = mid instead of mid + 1) loop forever. Pick a template and reuse it exactly.

  • Overflow on mid

    Always lo + (hi - lo) / 2.

Example — lower bound (first index with nums[i] >= target)
int lowerBound(int[] nums, int target) {
  int lo = 0, hi = nums.length;            // [lo, hi)
  while (lo < hi) {
    int mid = lo + (hi - lo) / 2;
    if (nums[mid] < target) lo = mid + 1;  // discard left half incl. mid
    else hi = mid;                         // mid still a candidate
  }
  return lo;                               // insertion point
}
Interview answer tip: "The array is sorted so I binary-search in O(log n). For 'smallest X that works' problems I binary-search the answer: each guess gets an O(n) feasibility check, so O(n log range) overall."
Pattern
Stack, queue & monotonic stack
Concept

A stack (LIFO) remembers "what's still open / unresolved"; a queue (FIFO) processes "in arrival order". A monotonic stack keeps its elements sorted to answer "next greater/smaller" in O(n).

Why it exists: Matching, nesting, and "nearest bigger element" problems have a natural last-in-first-out structure; a plain stack solves them in one pass instead of nested scans.

How it works
  1. Validation/matching: push openers, pop and check on closers (valid parentheses, expression evaluation).
  2. Monotonic stack: before pushing, pop everything smaller (or larger) — those popped just found their "next greater" (daily temperatures, stock span, largest rectangle in histogram).
  3. Deque as both: ArrayDeque is the go-to for stack and queue, and for the sliding-window-maximum monotonic deque.
What to understand deeply
  • Use ArrayDeque, not Stack/LinkedList

    Legacy java.util.Stack is synchronized and slow; ArrayDeque is the recommended LIFO/FIFO.

  • Each element pushed/popped once

    That's why a monotonic stack is O(n) even though there's an inner while-loop.

Pitfalls
  • Peeking/popping an empty stack

    Check isEmpty() first — a stray closer with nothing to match is the common bug.

  • Storing values when you need indices

    Many monotonic-stack problems need indices to compute distances/widths.

Example — valid parentheses
boolean isValid(String s) {
  Deque<Character> st = new ArrayDeque<>();
  Map<Character,Character> pair = Map.of(')','(', ']','[', '}','{');
  for (char c : s.toCharArray()) {
    if (pair.containsValue(c)) st.push(c);                  // opener
    else if (st.isEmpty() || st.pop() != pair.get(c)) return false;
  }
  return st.isEmpty();
}
Interview answer tip: "Nesting/matching screams stack. For 'next greater element' I keep a monotonic stack — each index is pushed and popped once, so it's O(n) despite the inner loop."
Top pattern
Trees & graphs — BFS / DFS
Concept

BFS explores level by level using a queue; DFS goes deep first using recursion or a stack. A tree is just a graph with no cycles, so the same two traversals cover most tree and graph questions.

Why it exists: Almost any "reachability / shortest steps / connected components / level / order" problem is a graph traversal in disguise.

How it works
  1. BFS: queue + visited set; first time you reach a node is the fewest edges path → shortest path on an unweighted graph, level-order tree traversal.
  2. DFS: recurse/stack + visited set; natural for "explore a whole component", cycle detection, and tree in/pre/post-order.
  3. Grid as a graph: cells are nodes, 4/8 neighbours are edges → number of islands, flood fill, rotting oranges (multi-source BFS).
  4. Topological sort: DFS post-order or Kahn's BFS on a DAG → build/task ordering, course schedule.
BFS vs DFS — when to pick which
  • Shortest path / minimum steps (unweighted)

    BFS — the level you first reach a node is its distance.

  • Connectivity, cycles, "all paths", tree depth

    DFS — simpler to write recursively. For weighted shortest path use Dijkstra (a heap-based BFS).

Pitfalls
  • Forgetting the visited set

    Graphs have cycles → infinite loops. Mark visited when you enqueue in BFS, not when you dequeue, to avoid duplicates.

  • Deep recursion stack overflow

    n ≈ 10⁵ deep DFS can blow the JVM stack — switch to an explicit stack or BFS.

Example — number of islands (grid DFS)
int numIslands(char[][] g) {
  int count = 0;
  for (int r = 0; r < g.length; r++)
    for (int c = 0; c < g[0].length; c++)
      if (g[r][c] == '1') { count++; sink(g, r, c); }
  return count;
}
void sink(char[][] g, int r, int c) {
  if (r < 0 || c < 0 || r >= g.length || c >= g[0].length || g[r][c] != '1') return;
  g[r][c] = '0';                                   // mark visited in place
  sink(g, r+1, c); sink(g, r-1, c); sink(g, r, c+1); sink(g, r, c-1);
}
Interview answer tip: "I model it as a graph: nodes and edges. Shortest unweighted path → BFS with a queue; connectivity or all-paths → DFS. Either way I keep a visited set so cycles don't loop forever."
Pattern
Recursion & backtracking
Concept

Recursion solves a problem in terms of smaller copies of itself. Backtracking is recursion that builds a candidate step by step and undoes the last choice when it leads to a dead end.

Why it exists: For "generate all / find every valid configuration" (subsets, permutations, combinations, N-Queens, word search) it explores the decision tree systematically and prunes impossible branches early.

How it works (the template)
  1. Base case: candidate is complete → record it (a copy!) and return.
  2. Choose: pick the next option, add it to the current path.
  3. Explore: recurse with the choice applied.
  4. Un-choose (backtrack): remove the choice so the next iteration starts clean.
  5. Prune: skip branches that can't possibly lead to a valid/better answer.
What to understand deeply
  • Complexity is exponential by nature

    Subsets 2ⁿ, permutations n!. Acceptable only for small n — pruning is what makes it finish.

  • Avoid duplicates

    Sort first and skip equal siblings, or pass a start index so you don't reuse earlier elements.

Pitfalls
  • Adding the path by reference

    You must add new ArrayList<>(path) — otherwise every result points at the same list that ends up empty.

  • Forgetting to undo

    Missing the remove/backtrack step silently corrupts later branches.

Example — all subsets
List<List<Integer>> subsets(int[] nums) {
  List<List<Integer>> res = new ArrayList<>();
  backtrack(nums, 0, new ArrayList<>(), res);
  return res;
}
void backtrack(int[] nums, int start, List<Integer> path, List<List<Integer>> res) {
  res.add(new ArrayList<>(path));            // every node is a valid subset
  for (int i = start; i < nums.length; i++) {
    path.add(nums[i]);                        // choose
    backtrack(nums, i + 1, path, res);        // explore
    path.remove(path.size() - 1);             // un-choose
  }
}
Interview answer tip: "It's 'generate all valid configurations', so I backtrack: choose → recurse → un-choose, snapshotting the path at base cases. I prune dead branches early because the raw search is exponential."
Hard but common
Dynamic programming
Concept

DP solves problems with overlapping subproblems and optimal substructure by solving each subproblem once and reusing the answer — either top-down (recursion + memo) or bottom-up (fill a table).

Why it exists: It turns an exponential brute-force recursion into polynomial time by never recomputing the same state. The art is defining the state and the transition.

How to derive a DP
  1. Define the state: what minimal info identifies a subproblem? e.g. dp[i] = best answer using the first i items.
  2. Write the recurrence: express dp[i] from smaller states (the "choice" at step i).
  3. Base cases: the smallest states you know directly.
  4. Order / memoize: bottom-up fill in dependency order, or top-down recurse with a cache.
  5. Optimize space: if dp[i] only needs dp[i-1], keep two rows / a few variables.
Classic shapes to recognize
  • 1-D sequence: Fibonacci, climbing stairs, house robber, longest increasing subsequence.
  • 2-D grid / two strings: unique paths, edit distance, longest common subsequence.
  • Knapsack / subset-sum: coin change, partition equal subset — "pick or skip with a capacity".
Pitfalls
  • Wrong state definition

    If the recurrence needs info your state doesn't carry, the table is wrong. Get the state right before coding.

  • Iteration order

    Bottom-up must compute dependencies first; 0/1 vs unbounded knapsack differ only by inner-loop direction.

Example — coin change (fewest coins for amount)
int coinChange(int[] coins, int amount) {
  int[] dp = new int[amount + 1];
  Arrays.fill(dp, amount + 1);          // "infinity" sentinel
  dp[0] = 0;
  for (int a = 1; a <= amount; a++)
    for (int c : coins)
      if (c <= a) dp[a] = Math.min(dp[a], dp[a - c] + 1);
  return dp[amount] > amount ? -1 : dp[amount];
}
Interview answer tip: "I look for overlapping subproblems. I define the state as 'fewest coins to make amount a', the transition is 'take one coin c then dp[a−c]', and I fill bottom-up. Often I can shrink the table to O(amount) space."
Pattern
Heap / priority queue & top-K
Concept

A heap (Java's PriorityQueue) always gives you the smallest (or largest) element in O(1) peek and O(log n) push/pop, without keeping everything fully sorted.

Why it exists: When you only need the K best or a running min/max — not a full sort — a heap does it in O(n log k) and works on streams where you can't see all data at once.

How it works
  1. Top-K largest: keep a min-heap of size K; push each element, pop when size > K → the heap holds the K largest. (Top-K smallest → max-heap.)
  2. Merge K sorted lists/streams: heap of the current head of each list, repeatedly pop the min and push its successor.
  3. Running median / scheduling: two heaps, or pull the soonest/cheapest task (Dijkstra, task scheduler).
  4. Quickselect alternative: for an in-memory "Kth largest" you can also partition in O(n) average — mention both.
What to understand deeply
  • Custom ordering

    new PriorityQueue<>(Comparator.reverseOrder()) for a max-heap; comparingInt for objects.

  • Size-K heap beats full sort

    O(n log k) vs O(n log n) — a big win when k ≪ n, and it streams.

Pitfalls
  • Heap is not sorted iteration

    Iterating a PriorityQueue does not yield sorted order — only poll() does.

  • Wrong heap direction

    For "K largest" you want a min-heap so the smallest of your K is the one you evict.

Example — Kth largest element (O(n log k))
int kthLargest(int[] nums, int k) {
  PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();   // smallest on top
  for (int x : nums) {
    minHeap.offer(x);
    if (minHeap.size() > k) minHeap.poll();                 // drop the smallest
  }
  return minHeap.peek();                                     // Kth largest
}
Interview answer tip: "I don't need a full sort, just the K largest, so I keep a size-K min-heap — O(n log k) and it works on a stream. If everything's in memory, quickselect gives O(n) average."
Foundations
Sorting & comparators
Concept

Sorting (O(n log n)) is often the cheap pre-step that unlocks two pointers, binary search, greedy or interval-merging. Knowing how to express custom order in Java is the practical skill the test checks.

Why it exists: Many problems become trivial once data is ordered — merge intervals, meeting rooms, "k closest", greedy scheduling all start with a sort.

How it works
  1. Know the library: Arrays.sort uses dual-pivot quicksort for primitives (not stable) and Timsort for objects (stable, O(n log n) worst case).
  2. Comparator composition: comparingInt(...).thenComparing(...).reversed() for multi-key order.
  3. Sort then sweep: sort by start, then merge overlapping intervals in one pass.
  4. Know the trade-offs: merge sort = stable, O(n) extra space; quicksort = in-place, O(n²) worst case; counting/radix = O(n) for small integer ranges.
What to understand deeply
  • Stable vs unstable

    Stability preserves the order of equal keys — matters when sorting by a secondary key in two passes.

  • Comparator contract

    Must be consistent (anti-symmetric, transitive); a broken one throws "Comparison method violates its general contract". Never subtract ints that can overflow — use Integer.compare.

Pitfalls
  • Overflow in a comparator

    (a, b) -> a - b overflows for large/negative values → use Integer.compare(a, b).

  • Sorting when you don't need to

    If you only need the min/max or top-K, a single pass or heap beats an O(n log n) sort.

Example — merge overlapping intervals
int[][] merge(int[][] iv) {
  Arrays.sort(iv, Comparator.comparingInt(a -> a[0]));   // by start
  List<int[]> out = new ArrayList<>();
  for (int[] cur : iv) {
    if (!out.isEmpty() && cur[0] <= out.get(out.size()-1)[1]) {
      out.get(out.size()-1)[1] = Math.max(out.get(out.size()-1)[1], cur[1]);
    } else {
      out.add(cur);
    }
  }
  return out.toArray(new int[0][]);
}
Interview answer tip: "Sorting is my O(n log n) setup move — it unlocks a linear sweep for interval merging or greedy. I write custom order with Comparator.comparingInt(...).thenComparing(...) and use Integer.compare to avoid overflow."
Deep dive — Coding Test / Algorithms
War story — the "optimal" solution that timed out

Symptom: A candidate's solution passed the small tests but TLE'd (time-limit exceeded) on the large hidden case with n = 2×10⁵.

Cause: An inner list.contains() turned an otherwise O(n) loop into O(n²) — contains on an ArrayList is a linear scan, so 4×10¹⁰ operations.

Fix: Swap the ArrayList membership check for a HashSet. Same algorithm shape, O(1) lookups, back to O(n) and well under the limit.

Lesson: Always sanity-check the complexity of library calls inside loops — contains, string concatenation in a loop, or list.remove(0) quietly add a factor of n. Estimate operations against n before submitting.

// Before — O(n^2): contains scans the whole list each time
if (seen.contains(x)) ...;     // List<Integer> seen
// After — O(n): O(1) membership
if (seenSet.contains(x)) ...;  // HashSet<Integer> seenSet
Interview scenario — "Walk me through how you'd attack a new problem"

You're given an array and a target; here's the problem. Where do you start?

First I clarify: input size, are values sorted, can they be negative, are there duplicates, what should I return if there's no answer? Then I state a brute force out loud so we have a baseline and I'm not stuck on a blank screen — usually the O(n²) "check every pair".

Good — now make it faster.

I find the bottleneck: the inner lookup. I can trade memory for speed with a HashMap and ask whether the complement was already seen, which gets me to O(n) time and O(n) space. If the array were sorted I'd instead use two pointers and stay O(1) extra space — I'd name that trade-off and let you steer.

How do you know your code is correct before running it?

I dry-run a tiny example by hand, then deliberately test the edges: empty array, single element, all equal, the no-answer case, and the maximum size for complexity. I also watch for integer overflow — long for sums, and lo + (hi - lo) / 2 in binary search.

What if you don't see the optimal solution in time?

I ship the working brute force, say clearly what its complexity is and where the bottleneck is, and describe the direction I'd optimize — a hash, a sort, a heap. A correct slower solution plus an honest optimization plan beats a broken "clever" one, and it keeps the conversation going.

15. Security & Identity

Backend engineers must treat authentication, authorization and secret handling as first-class design concerns, not an afterthought bolted on at the end.

#15
Key focus
AuthN vs AuthZ, OAuth2 & OIDC, JWT vs opaque tokens
Concept

Authentication (AuthN) proves who you are; Authorization (AuthZ) decides what you are allowed to do. OAuth2 is an authorization-delegation framework; OIDC layers identity on top of it; the token can be a self-contained JWT or an opaque reference.

Why it exists: So that a service can let a user log in once and then delegate scoped access to other services/clients without ever sharing the password, and so that "logged in" and "allowed to perform this action" stay two separate decisions.

Analogy: AuthN = the bouncer checking your ID at the door; AuthZ = the wristband that says which rooms you may enter. The same person is one identity but many permissions.

How it works
  1. Roles: resource owner (the user), client (the app), authorization server (issues tokens), resource server (the API holding the data).
  2. Authorization Code + PKCE: client redirects the user to the auth server with a code_challenge (SHA-256 of a secret verifier), gets back a short-lived code, then exchanges code + code_verifier for an access token — so a stolen code is useless without the verifier.
  3. OIDC adds the id_token: a JWT that proves identity to the client; the access_token still authorizes API calls. OAuth2 alone is for access, not identity.
  4. Validation: JWT → verify signature against the issuer JWKS, check exp/iss/aud. Opaque → call the auth server's introspection endpoint to ask "is this still valid?".
Common real-world scenarios
  1. SPA / mobile login: public clients cannot keep a client secret → use Authorization Code + PKCE, never the deprecated Implicit flow.
  2. "Log out now" requirement: a long-lived JWT cannot be revoked before exp → either keep JWT lifetime short (5-15 min) + refresh tokens, or use opaque tokens with introspection.
  3. Reading the user's email from a JWT vs an id_token: the access token is for the API; identity claims belong in the id_token. Don't treat the access token as proof of identity.
  4. Multi-audience token reused on the wrong service: a token issued for payments-api accepted by reports-api because nobody checked aud → privilege bleed.
  5. Clock skew between services: a freshly issued token is rejected as "not yet valid" (nbf) → allow a small leeway (30-60s) in validation.

A strong engineer can explain the OAuth2 roles precisely, why PKCE exists, the difference between an access token and an id_token, and the revocability trade-off between JWT and opaque tokens — not just "we use JWT".

What to understand deeply
  • AuthN and AuthZ are two separate decisions

    A request can be perfectly authenticated and still unauthorized. Treating "has a valid token" as "may do this" is the root of most access-control bugs. Validate the token first (identity), then evaluate permissions against the target resource.

    // Authenticated != authorized
                  // Step 1 (AuthN): token signature + exp + iss + aud are valid
                  // Step 2 (AuthZ): does THIS user own THIS order?
if (!order.getOwnerId().equals(principal.userId())) {
    throw new AccessDeniedException("Not your order");
}
  • Authorization Code + PKCE, step by step

    PKCE (Proof Key for Code Exchange) binds the token request to the same client that started the flow. The client generates a random code_verifier, sends code_challenge = SHA256(verifier) on the redirect, then proves possession of the verifier at the token endpoint.

    verifier  = random(43..128 chars)
challenge = base64url(sha256(verifier))
// 1. /authorize?...&code_challenge=challenge&code_challenge_method=S256
// 2. user logs in, server returns ?code=abc
// 3. POST /token  code=abc & code_verifier=verifier
//    server recomputes sha256(verifier) == challenge ? issue tokens
  • JWT: self-contained, stateless — and hard to revoke

    A JWT is header.payload.signature. The resource server verifies the signature with the issuer's public key (fetched from the JWKS endpoint, cached by kid) and checks exp/iss/aud/nbf — no network call per request. The cost: you cannot easily invalidate it before expiry, so keep lifetimes short and rely on refresh-token rotation.

  • Opaque token: revocable, but a hop per request

    An opaque token is a random reference with no embedded claims. The resource server calls the auth server's introspection endpoint (RFC 7662) to validate and resolve scopes. Revocation is instant, but every request adds latency — mitigate with a short-TTL introspection cache. Choose opaque when instant revocation matters more than statelessness.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Accepting alg: none or not pinning the algorithm

    A classic JWT attack: the attacker sets the header alg to none, or swaps RS256→HS256 and signs with the public key as the HMAC secret. Always pin the expected algorithm and verify against the JWKS — never let the token dictate how it is verified.

  • Not validating aud / iss

    A token issued for service A is replayed against service B. If B only checks the signature (same auth server) but not the audience, it accepts a token never meant for it. Always assert iss equals your trusted issuer and aud contains your service id.

    JwtDecoder decoder = ...;
decoder.setJwtValidator(JwtValidators.createDefaultWithIssuer(issuer));
// plus a custom audience validator that requires aud == "payments-api"
  • Treating "valid token" as "authorized"

    The token says who, not what they may touch. Skipping per-object checks turns every endpoint into an IDOR. AuthZ must run against the concrete resource, not just the route — see Broken Access Control below.

  • Long-lived JWTs with no revocation strategy

    A 24h access token means a compromised token is usable for 24h even after the user is disabled. Either shorten lifetimes drastically + rotate refresh tokens with reuse detection, or move to opaque tokens. "We'll just blacklist it" reintroduces the state you were trying to avoid.

Example
// Decoded JWT payload the resource server must check
{
  "iss": "https://auth.example.com/",   // must equal trusted issuer
  "aud": "payments-api",                 // must contain THIS service
  "sub": "user-123",                     // the principal
  "scope": "payments:read payments:write",
  "exp": 1718539200,                     // reject if now > exp (+skew)
  "iat": 1718538300,
  "nbf": 1718538300                      // reject if now < nbf (-skew)
}

// Validation order: signature (JWKS) -> exp/nbf -> iss -> aud -> scopes
// Only AFTER all pass do you trust 'sub' and 'scope'.
Interview answer tip: Say: 'AuthN proves identity, AuthZ checks permission — separate decisions. For public clients I use Authorization Code + PKCE. JWTs are stateless but hard to revoke, so I keep them short-lived with rotating refresh tokens; opaque tokens are revocable but cost an introspection hop. And I always validate signature, exp, iss and aud — never just the signature.'
Key focus
Service-to-service security: mTLS, secrets & token propagation
Concept

Inside a microservices system, services must prove which service they are (mTLS), fetch credentials safely (secrets management), and pass the user's context downstream without over-sharing (token propagation & downscoping). Network position is not identity — this is zero trust.

Why it exists: "It came from inside the cluster, so it's safe" is how one compromised pod turns into a full breach. Mutual authentication, scoped credentials and rotating secrets shrink the blast radius of any single compromise.

Analogy: A company badge (mTLS) proves you're an employee at every door, not just the lobby; a key card is scoped to your floor (downscoped token); the master key lives in a vault, not under the mat (secrets manager).

How it works
  1. mTLS: both client and server present X.509 certificates; each verifies the other against a trusted CA → mutual proof of identity, encrypted channel.
  2. Secrets: the app fetches DB passwords / API keys from a secret manager (Vault, cloud SM, k8s Secret) at startup or on demand — never from source or baked images.
  3. Token propagation: the gateway authenticates the user, then services forward the user context; downstream calls receive a downscoped token (fewer scopes, narrower audience) via token exchange.
  4. Zero trust: every hop authenticates and authorizes the caller and the user — no service is trusted merely for being internal.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. An internal endpoint with no auth: "only called by other services" — until an SSRF or a compromised pod calls it directly. Require mTLS or a service token on every internal API.
  2. DB password committed to application.yml: ends up in git history and the container image. Move to a secret manager and rotate the leaked credential immediately.
  3. Forwarding the full user token everywhere: the notification service now holds a token with payments:write it never needs → downscope before propagating.
  4. Service mesh enables mTLS but apps still listen on plaintext: the sidecar is bypassed on localhost → bind only to the mesh interface / use STRICT mTLS mode.
  5. Static API keys that never rotate: a key leaked two years ago still works → prefer short-lived dynamic credentials (Vault dynamic secrets, IAM roles).

A strong engineer designs the trust boundaries explicitly: which calls require mTLS, where secrets come from and how they rotate, and what scopes a token carries by the time it reaches the third service downstream.

What to understand deeply
  • mTLS gives services a verifiable identity

    One-way TLS only authenticates the server. mTLS makes the client present a certificate too, so service B knows it's really service A (identity bound to the cert's SAN/SPIFFE ID), not just "something on the network". A mesh (Istio/Linkerd) or SPIFFE/SPIRE can issue and rotate these certs transparently.

  • Secrets: never hardcode, always rotate

    Secrets in code/config/images leak through git, logs and registries. Fetch them from Vault / AWS Secrets Manager / GCP Secret Manager / k8s Secrets. Dynamic, short-lived secrets (e.g. Vault generating a per-pod DB credential with a 1h TTL) beat long-lived static ones because there is little to leak and rotation is automatic.

    // Anti-pattern
spring.datasource.password: SuperSecret123   // in git forever

// Better: injected from a secret manager, rotated
spring.datasource.password: ${DB_PASSWORD}   // sourced from Vault / k8s Secret
// Best: dynamic short-lived credential issued per pod, auto-rotated
  • Token propagation & downscoping

    When service A calls B which calls C, blindly forwarding the original user token gives C more power than it needs. Use token exchange (RFC 8693) to mint a downstream token with a narrower aud and fewer scopes, so a leak at C cannot be replayed against A's resources.

  • Zero trust: position is not identity

    A flat internal network where every service trusts every other means one foothold = everything. Authenticate the caller (mTLS / service token) and the user (propagated token) on every hop, and authorize each call. "Behind the firewall" is not an authorization decision.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Implicit trust of internal callers

    An admin endpoint protected only by "it's on the internal subnet". A SSRF in any front-line service, or a single compromised pod, can now call it. Internal != trusted — require authentication on internal traffic too.

  • Secrets in env vars dumped to logs / crash reports

    A startup log prints the full config, or an error handler serializes the environment to an APM tool → the DB password lands in a third-party log store. Mask secret keys in logging and never serialize raw config objects.

  • Long-lived service credentials with no rotation

    A shared static API key embedded in every service. Rotating it means a coordinated redeploy, so nobody does it, so it lives for years. Use per-service identities and automated rotation so revoking one doesn't break the fleet.

  • mTLS terminated at the edge then plaintext inside

    TLS ends at the load balancer and east-west traffic is plaintext, so anyone who can sniff the pod network reads tokens and PII. Enforce mesh mTLS in STRICT mode and verify sidecars actually intercept the traffic.

Example
// Spring WebClient that forwards a DOWNSCOPED token, not the raw user token
WebClient toNotificationSvc(OAuth2AuthorizedClient downscoped) {
    return WebClient.builder()
        .baseUrl("https://notifications.internal")
        // mTLS handled by the service-mesh sidecar (STRICT mode)
        .defaultHeader(HttpHeaders.AUTHORIZATION,
            "Bearer " + downscoped.getAccessToken().getTokenValue())
        .build();
}

// The downscoped token's claims: narrower aud + only the scope it needs
// {
//   "aud": "notifications-api",        // NOT payments-api
//   "scope": "notifications:send",     // dropped payments:* scopes
//   "exp": <short>
// }
// A leak here cannot touch the payments service.
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I assume zero trust between services. Service identity comes from mTLS (mesh or SPIFFE), secrets come from a vault and rotate — never from code — and when I propagate a user token downstream I downscope it so a leak at the third service has a tiny blast radius. Being inside the cluster is never an authorization decision.'
Key focus
OWASP Top 10 in practice
Concept

The OWASP Top 10 is the industry's list of the most common, most damaging web-app security risks. For a backend engineer it is a practical checklist: broken access control, injection, SSRF, insecure deserialization, security misconfiguration and sensitive data exposure are the ones you meet weekly.

Why it exists: The same handful of mistakes cause most breaches. Knowing the concrete defensive pattern for each one turns "security" from a vague worry into specific code review checks.

Analogy: It's a pre-flight checklist. Pilots don't rediscover how to crash each flight; they run the same list that catches the known killers.

How it works
  1. Broken Access Control (incl. IDOR): enforce AuthZ on the object (does this user own this record?), not just on the route.
  2. Injection: use parameterized queries / JPA parameters; never string-concatenate user input into SQL/JPQL/shell.
  3. SSRF: validate and allowlist any outbound URL the user can influence; block internal ranges and the cloud metadata endpoint.
  4. Deserialization / misconfiguration / data exposure: never deserialize untrusted data, lock down defaults and actuator, encrypt data and keep secrets/PII out of logs.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. IDOR: GET /invoices/{id} returns any invoice because the handler loads by id without checking the owner → user 1 reads user 2's invoice by changing the number.
  2. SQL injection via search: "... WHERE name LIKE '%" + q + "%'" → q = "'; DROP TABLE...". Use bound parameters.
  3. SSRF via "fetch image from URL": attacker passes http://169.254.169.254/... and the server returns cloud credentials.
  4. Open actuator / debug endpoints in prod: /actuator/env exposes secrets, /heapdump leaks tokens.
  5. Tokens / passwords / PII in logs: request logging dumps the Authorization header → credentials sit in the log store readable by half the company.

A strong engineer doesn't recite the list — they know the one concrete defense for each: authorize the object not the route, bind parameters, allowlist outbound URLs, refuse untrusted deserialization, harden defaults, and never log secrets.

What to understand deeply
  • Broken Access Control / IDOR — authorize the object

    The #1 risk. Loading findById(id) and returning it is an IDOR unless you also assert the caller may see that object. Enforce ownership/role on the concrete resource, ideally in the query itself so you cannot forget.

    // Vulnerable: route is authenticated, object is not authorized
Invoice inv = repo.findById(id).orElseThrow();
return inv; // any logged-in user reads any invoice

// Safe: scope the query to the owner
Invoice inv = repo.findByIdAndOwnerId(id, principal.userId())
                  .orElseThrow(() -> new AccessDeniedException("not found"));
  • Injection — parameterize, never concatenate

    Any user input concatenated into a query is injectable. Use bound parameters (JPA named params, PreparedStatement, Panache ("name = ?1", name)). The same rule applies to JPQL, native SQL, NoSQL filters and shell commands.

    // Vulnerable
em.createQuery("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.email = '" + email + "'");

// Safe: bound parameter, never interpolated
em.createQuery("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.email = :email")
  .setParameter("email", email);
  • SSRF — allowlist outbound URLs

    If a user can influence a URL the server fetches (webhooks, "import from URL", image proxy), an attacker aims it at internal services or the cloud metadata endpoint 169.254.169.254. Resolve the host, reject private/link-local/loopback ranges, and allowlist permitted destinations — denylists are bypassable.

  • Insecure deserialization & misconfiguration

    Never deserialize untrusted bytes with Java native serialization — it can instantiate arbitrary gadget chains → RCE. Prefer JSON with strict, explicit types. Separately, ship secure defaults: no default credentials, no verbose stack traces to clients, actuator endpoints locked down, debug off in prod.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • AuthZ only at the controller, not the data

    A @PreAuthorize("hasRole('USER')") guards the route, but the method then loads any id. Role checks are not ownership checks — combine route-level role guards with object-level ownership checks every time.

  • "Safe" string building that isn't

    Dynamic ORDER BY or table names can't be bound as parameters, so developers concatenate them — and inject there instead. Validate such values against a fixed allowlist of column/table names, never pass them through raw.

    // Injectable even though the WHERE is parameterized
String sql = "SELECT * FROM orders ORDER BY " + sortColumn;
// Fix: allowlist
if (!Set.of("created_at","total").contains(sortColumn)) throw ...;
  • Sensitive data exposure via logs & errors

    Logging full requests/responses, stack traces with PII, or tokens in URLs (query strings get logged everywhere). Mask Authorization headers, keep PII/secrets out of logs, return generic error messages to clients, and encrypt data at rest and in transit.

  • Security misconfiguration drifting back in

    A new dependency re-opens /actuator, a debug flag survives to prod, CORS gets set to * "to unblock the frontend". These regress silently — add automated security tests / config scanning so a misconfig fails the build, not just a pentest months later.

Example
// SSRF defense: allowlist + reject internal ranges before fetching
URI uri = URI.create(userSuppliedUrl);
InetAddress addr = InetAddress.getByName(uri.getHost());

if (addr.isLoopbackAddress() || addr.isLinkLocalAddress()
        || addr.isSiteLocalAddress() || addr.isAnyLocalAddress()) {
    throw new SecurityException("Blocked internal address");
}
if (!ALLOWED_HOSTS.contains(uri.getHost())) {
    throw new SecurityException("Host not allowlisted");
}
// 169.254.169.254 (cloud metadata) is link-local -> already blocked
return httpClient.send(HttpRequest.newBuilder(uri).build(), ...);
Interview answer tip: Pick one risk and go deep: 'Broken access control is the one I review hardest. A valid token isn't authorization, so I scope queries by owner — findByIdAndOwnerId — rather than findById, so an IDOR is impossible by construction. Then injection: always bound parameters; and SSRF: allowlist outbound URLs and block link-local so 169.254.169.254 can't leak cloud creds.'
Deep dive — Security
Architecture flow: OAuth2 Authorization Code + PKCE through an API gateway
Browser / SPA (public client)            Authorization Server (OIDC)
      │                                          │
      │ 1. generate verifier; challenge=S256(v)  │
      │ 2. /authorize?code_challenge=...&S256 ──▶ │
      │                                          │  user logs in (AuthN)
      │ 3. redirect ?code=abc  ◀──────────────── │  + consent (scopes)
      │                                          │
      │ 4. POST /token  code=abc & verifier=v ──▶ │  recompute S256(v)==challenge?
      │ 5. ◀── access_token (JWT) + id_token + refresh_token
      │
      │ 6. GET /api/orders   Authorization: Bearer <JWT>
      ▼
┌─────────────────────────┐        fetch JWKS (cache by kid)
│      API Gateway        │ ─────────────────────────────────▶ Auth Server
│  • verify signature     │ ◀───────────────────────────────── /jwks
│  • check exp/iss/aud    │
│  • basic scope check    │
└────────────┬────────────┘
             │  forward (often downscoped) Bearer token
             ▼
┌─────────────────────────┐
│   Resource Server       │  ZERO TRUST: re-validate, don't blindly trust gateway
│  • verify JWT again      │
│  • @PreAuthorize scope   │  AuthZ on scope/role
│  • object ownership chk  │  AuthZ on the resource (anti-IDOR)
└────────────┬────────────┘
             ▼
        200 OK (only the caller's data)

Failure modes:
  • alg:none / wrong aud      -> reject at gateway AND service (pin alg, check aud)
  • token valid but not owner -> 403 at object check, not just route
  • gateway-only validation   -> compromised gateway = full bypass (re-verify downstream)
War story — the invoice that wasn't yours

Service: billing-api, B2B SaaS, ~2k tenants, REST endpoint GET /api/invoices/{id}.

Symptom: A customer support ticket: "I can see another company's invoice PDF." Reproduced in 30 seconds by incrementing the invoice id in the URL. No alerts had fired — every request was a valid, authenticated 200.

Investigation: The endpoint had @PreAuthorize("hasRole('USER')") and a valid JWT, so AuthN and route-level AuthZ both passed. The handler did invoiceRepository.findById(id) and returned it. There was no check that the invoice's tenant matched the caller's tenant. Access logs showed one client had been walking the id space for two days.

Root cause: Classic IDOR / broken access control. AuthZ was enforced on the route ("is a logged-in user") but never on the object ("does this user's tenant own this invoice"). A valid token had been mistaken for authorization.

Fix (immediate): Change the query to scope by tenant: findByIdAndTenantId(id, principal.tenantId()), returning 404 (not 403, to avoid confirming existence). Hotfixed and deployed within the hour; rotated nothing because no credentials leaked, but notified affected tenants per policy.

Fix (structural): A repository convention — entities carrying a tenant always expose only tenant-scoped finders; raw findById on multi-tenant entities is banned and enforced by an ArchUnit test. Added authorization integration tests that assert cross-tenant access returns 404.

Lesson: Authentication is not authorization, and route-level role checks are not object-level ownership checks. Enforce AuthZ on the data — ideally in the query — so the insecure path cannot be written by accident.

Production-grade: Spring Security resource server (JWT + issuer/audience + method security)
@Configuration
@EnableWebSecurity
@EnableMethodSecurity   // turns on @PreAuthorize
public class ResourceServerConfig {

    @Value("${security.issuer-uri}")  private String issuerUri;
    @Value("${security.audience}")    private String audience;

    @Bean
    SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        http
            .authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
                .requestMatchers("/actuator/health").permitAll()
                .anyRequest().authenticated())
            .oauth2ResourceServer(oauth -> oauth
                .jwt(jwt -> jwt.jwtAuthenticationConverter(scopeConverter())))
            // stateless API: no session, send 401 not a login redirect
            .sessionManagement(s -> s.sessionCreationPolicy(STATELESS))
            .csrf(csrf -> csrf.disable()); // bearer tokens, not cookies
        return http.build();
    }

    @Bean
    JwtDecoder jwtDecoder() {
        // signature verified against the issuer's JWKS, fetched + cached by kid
        NimbusJwtDecoder decoder =
            (NimbusJwtDecoder) JwtDecoders.fromIssuerLocation(issuerUri);

        OAuth2TokenValidator<Jwt> withIssuer =
            JwtValidators.createDefaultWithIssuer(issuerUri); // exp, nbf, iss
        OAuth2TokenValidator<Jwt> withAudience = jwt ->
            jwt.getAudience().contains(audience)
                ? OAuth2TokenValidatorResult.success()
                : OAuth2TokenValidatorResult.failure(
                    new OAuth2Error("invalid_token", "Missing audience", null));

        decoder.setJwtValidator(new DelegatingOAuth2TokenValidator<>(withIssuer, withAudience));
        return decoder;
    }

    private JwtAuthenticationConverter scopeConverter() {
        JwtGrantedAuthoritiesConverter scopes = new JwtGrantedAuthoritiesConverter();
        scopes.setAuthorityPrefix("SCOPE_");
        scopes.setAuthoritiesClaimName("scope");
        JwtAuthenticationConverter c = new JwtAuthenticationConverter();
        c.setJwtGrantedAuthoritiesConverter(scopes);
        return c;
    }
}

@RestController
@RequestMapping("/api/invoices")
class InvoiceController {

    private final InvoiceRepository repo;

    InvoiceController(InvoiceRepository repo) { this.repo = repo; }

    // AuthZ #1: scope check (what the token is allowed to do)
    @PreAuthorize("hasAuthority('SCOPE_invoices:read')")
    @GetMapping("/{id}")
    Invoice get(@PathVariable String id, @AuthenticationPrincipal Jwt jwt) {
        String tenantId = jwt.getClaimAsString("tenant_id");
        // AuthZ #2: object ownership (anti-IDOR) - scope the query by tenant
        return repo.findByIdAndTenantId(id, tenantId)
                   .orElseThrow(() -> new ResponseStatusException(NOT_FOUND));
    }
}
Interview scenario — "Design auth for a microservices system"

You have a web app, a mobile app, an API gateway and a dozen backend services. How do you design authentication and authorization?

Central OIDC authorization server issues tokens; clients log in via Authorization Code + PKCE — that covers both the SPA and mobile since neither can safely hold a client secret. The auth server returns an id_token for the client's own use and an access token (a JWT) for calling APIs. The gateway terminates the user-facing edge: it validates the JWT signature against the issuer JWKS and checks exp, iss and aud before routing.

Does the gateway validating the token mean the services can trust it blindly?

No — that's zero trust. If a service trusts the gateway implicitly, a single compromised gateway or any path that bypasses it is a total breach. Each service re-validates the JWT itself; it's cheap because it's just signature plus claim checks against cached JWKS. The gateway is a first line, not the only line.

How do services authenticate to each other, and how does the user context flow downstream?

Service identity comes from mTLS — a service mesh issues and rotates the certs, so service A cryptographically knows it's talking to service B, not just something on the network. For the user context I propagate the token, but downscoped: when service A calls B I use token exchange to mint a token with a narrower audience and only the scopes B needs. So if B is compromised, the token it holds can't be replayed against the payments service.

JWT or opaque tokens? And how do you handle revocation and logout?

JWTs for the stateless, high-throughput path because services validate locally with no per-request hop. The cost is revocation — you can't kill a JWT before exp — so I keep access tokens short, 5 to 15 minutes, and use refresh tokens with rotation and reuse detection. If a refresh token is replayed, the whole family is revoked. If the business genuinely needs instant revocation everywhere, I'd switch those flows to opaque tokens with introspection and cache the result for a few seconds.

Where does authorization actually happen, and how do you stop IDOR?

Two layers. Coarse-grained at the service via scopes/roles — @PreAuthorize on the method. Fine-grained at the object: I scope queries by owner or tenant, findByIdAndTenantId, so a valid token for the wrong resource returns 404 by construction. A token proves who, never what they may touch. I back that with authorization integration tests asserting cross-tenant access fails, and an ArchUnit rule banning raw findById on tenant-scoped entities.

And secrets — the DB passwords, signing keys, downstream API keys?

Never in code or images. They come from a secret manager — Vault or the cloud equivalent — ideally as short-lived dynamic credentials so there's little to leak and rotation is automatic. The token-signing keys live on the auth server and are exposed only as public keys via JWKS, rotated with a kid so services pick up new keys without downtime. And I keep secrets out of logs and error responses, which is where they tend to leak in practice.

16. Testing Strategy

Engineers are judged on testability and confidence-per-second, not raw coverage % — the skill is picking the right test at the right level.

#16
Key focus
The Test Pyramid — unit vs integration vs E2E, what to mock
Concept

The Test Pyramid is a shape that tells you the proportion of each test type: many fast unit tests at the base, fewer integration tests in the middle, very few slow end-to-end (E2E) tests at the top. A test double is any fake object you swap in for a real collaborator.

Why it exists: Tests cost time to run and maintain. The pyramid maximizes confidence per second — push as much verification as possible down to the cheap, fast, reliable layer, and reserve the expensive slow layer for the few things only it can prove.

Analogy: Like quality control in a factory — you check each part on the line (unit), check sub-assemblies fit together (integration), and only fully assemble & road-test a handful of finished cars (E2E). You don't road-test every bolt.

How it works
  1. Unit: one class/method in isolation, no I/O, milliseconds. Thousands of them, run on every save.
  2. Integration: your code + one real dependency (DB, broker, HTTP) wired together. Hundreds, run in CI.
  3. E2E: the whole system deployed, driven through its real entry point. A handful, slow and flaky-prone.
  4. Doubles: stub (canned answers), mock (verifies calls), fake (working lightweight impl), spy (real object that records calls).
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Inverted pyramid (ice-cream cone): mostly Selenium/E2E, almost no unit tests → suite takes 40 min, flakes constantly, nobody trusts a red build. Push logic down into fast unit tests.
  2. Mock the payment gateway / SMS provider: external boundary you don't own and can't call for free in CI → stub it. Verify your code sends the right request shape.
  3. Mocking Clock and UUID: a test that asserts "expires in 24h" fails at midnight. Inject a Clock and an id generator so time and randomness are deterministic.
  4. Over-mocked service test: every collaborator mocked, the test asserts the mock was called — it passes forever even after the real logic breaks. False confidence.
  5. Mocking the repository when behavior depends on the DB: a query with a real UNIQUE constraint / native SQL → the mock can't reproduce constraint violations; use a real DB (Testcontainers).

A strong engineer doesn't aim for a coverage number. They decide, per behavior, the cheapest level that can actually prove it correct — and know that the most dangerous test is one that passes whether the code works or not.

What to understand deeply
  • Solitary vs sociable unit tests

    A solitary test mocks all collaborators and isolates one class — fast, but couples the test to the implementation's call structure. A sociable test lets the class use real collaborators (pure domain objects) and only stubs the true edges (I/O). Prefer sociable for the domain: it tests behavior, not wiring, so it survives refactors.

    // Solitary — every collaborator mocked, tests the wiring
when(discountPolicy.apply(any())).thenReturn(Money.of(10));
when(taxCalculator.tax(any())).thenReturn(Money.of(2));
// asserts mocks were called in order — brittle

// Sociable — real domain objects, only the DB edge is faked
var order = new Order(realDiscountPolicy, realTaxCalculator);
order.addLine(item, 3);
assertThat(order.total()).isEqualTo(Money.of(257));
  • Know your four doubles — stub ≠ mock

    A stub provides input (state verification: assert the result). A mock verifies output calls (behavior verification: verify(x).send(...)). A fake is a real but lightweight implementation (in-memory repo). A spy wraps a real object and records calls. Reaching for verify() when an assertion on the result would do is the #1 cause of brittle tests.

  • What to mock: things at the boundary you don't control

    Mock external HTTP APIs (payment, KYC), the message broker, the system clock (Clock), randomness (UUID, Random), and the current user/principal. These are non-deterministic or cost money. Inject them as dependencies so a test can pin them.

    // Inject Clock — never call Instant.now() directly in logic
public Token issue(User u) {
    return new Token(u.id(), clock.instant().plus(Duration.ofHours(24)));
}
// test: Clock.fixed(...) → exact, repeatable expiry assertion
  • What NOT to mock: your own domain & the DB when behavior depends on it

    Don't mock value objects, entities, or domain services — they have no I/O, run in microseconds, and mocking them only tests that you wrote the test. Don't mock the repository when the behavior under test is the query itself (joins, constraints, pagination, native SQL). A mocked findByEmail proves nothing about whether the SQL is correct.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Over-mocking → "tests pass but prod breaks"

    When every dependency is a mock, the test encodes your assumptions about how the dependency behaves. If the real dependency behaves differently (different error, different null handling), the mock keeps lying and the green build ships a bug. Mocks freeze a contract that nobody re-verifies against reality.

  • Mocking the repository hides DB-only failures

    A unit test mocks orderRepo.save() to return the entity. In production the DB rejects it on a UNIQUE / NOT NULL / FK constraint, or a lazy association throws LazyInitializationException outside the transaction. The mock can't reproduce any of this — only a real DB integration test can.

    // Mock says "all good"
when(orderRepo.save(any())).thenReturn(order);
// Real Postgres: ERROR: duplicate key value violates
//   unique constraint "uq_order_idempotency_key"
  • Asserting on interactions instead of outcomes

    A test full of verify(mock, times(1))... couples to how the code works. Rename a method or reorder two calls and a dozen tests go red even though behavior is identical. Verify interactions only for true side-effects (an email was sent); for everything else assert the observable result.

  • The inverted pyramid kills velocity

    Heavy E2E + thin unit layer means every bug investigation starts from a 40-minute, flaky suite. Mean-time-to-feedback explodes, developers stop running tests locally, and red builds get rubber-stamped. Coverage % can still look high while confidence is low.

Example
// Solitary unit test (JUnit 5 + Mockito) — boundary mocked, logic real
@ExtendWith(MockitoExtension.class)
class SubscriptionServiceTest {

    @Mock PaymentGateway gateway;          // external boundary → mock
    @Mock SubscriptionRepository repo;     // (better: a fake or a Testcontainers test)

    @Test
    void chargesAndActivatesOnSuccess() {
        var clock = Clock.fixed(Instant.parse("2026-06-16T00:00:00Z"), ZoneOffset.UTC);
        var service = new SubscriptionService(gateway, repo, clock);
        when(gateway.charge(any())).thenReturn(PaymentResult.ok("txn_1"));

        Subscription sub = service.subscribe(userId, Plan.PRO);

        // assert the OUTCOME, not "the mock was called"
        assertThat(sub.status()).isEqualTo(ACTIVE);
        assertThat(sub.renewsAt()).isEqualTo(Instant.parse("2026-07-16T00:00:00Z"));
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Don't recite "unit, integration, E2E." Say: 'I mock what I don't own and what's non-deterministic — clock, randomness, external HTTP. I do NOT mock my own domain or the DB when the query is the thing under test. Over-mocking gives a green build that ships bugs, so I optimize for confidence per second, not coverage %.'
Key focus
Integration testing with Testcontainers
Concept

Testcontainers is a library that boots real dependencies — Postgres, Kafka, Redis — inside throwaway Docker containers for the duration of your tests, then tears them down automatically. Your test talks to the genuine engine, not a substitute.

Why it exists: In-memory fakes (H2, embedded Kafka) behave differently from production. They hide dialect quirks, constraint enforcement, and migration failures. Testcontainers gives you production-fidelity at the cost of a few seconds of startup, so integration tests catch real bugs instead of fake-engine bugs.

Analogy: Like rehearsing a play on the actual stage with the real lighting rig, instead of in someone's living room. The living room "works" but the trapdoor on the real stage is exactly what trips you in front of the audience.

How it works
  1. @Testcontainers on the class activates the JUnit 5 extension that manages container lifecycle.
  2. @Container static PostgreSQLContainer<> pg = ... — a static field starts once per class; an instance field starts per test method.
  3. @DynamicPropertySource injects the container's randomized JDBC url/user/password into Spring's Environment before the context starts.
  4. Spring Boot 3.1+: annotate the container @ServiceConnection and Spring auto-wires the DataSource — no manual property plumbing.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. H2 "PostgreSQL mode" passes, prod fails: jsonb, arrays, ON CONFLICT, window functions, LATERAL joins behave differently or don't exist in H2. A Postgres container catches it in CI.
  2. Flyway/Liquibase migration test: run migrations against a fresh container to prove they apply cleanly on an empty DB — catches a forgotten DDL change before it hits staging.
  3. Kafka consumer logic: a real KafkaContainer verifies serialization, partition assignment, and offset commit semantics that an embedded mock glosses over.
  4. Slow suite from container churn: 200 test classes each starting Postgres → minutes wasted. Reuse a singleton container so it starts once for the whole run.
  5. Constraint / unique-index behavior: a concurrent insert idempotency test needs a real UNIQUE index to throw — H2 enforces it differently or not at all.

A strong engineer treats "it passes on H2" as no evidence at all. They run integration tests on the same engine and version as production, and they tune container reuse so fidelity doesn't cost developer patience.

What to understand deeply
  • Static container = once per class, and why it matters

    A static @Container starts before any test and stops after all tests in the class — paying the ~2s startup once. An instance field restarts per method and balloons the suite. For a whole module, go further with the singleton container pattern: a manually-started static container in a base class, never stopped (Ryuk cleans it up at JVM exit).

    abstract class IntegrationTest {
    static final PostgreSQLContainer<?> PG =
        new PostgreSQLContainer<>("postgres:16-alpine");
    static { PG.start(); } // started once, shared by all subclasses
}
  • @DynamicPropertySource vs @ServiceConnection

    The container's port is random, so you can't hardcode the JDBC url. @DynamicPropertySource registers getJdbcUrl()/user/password lazily, before context startup. Since Spring Boot 3.1, @ServiceConnection does this automatically for known containers — less boilerplate, fewer typos.

    @DynamicPropertySource
static void props(DynamicPropertyRegistry r) {
    r.add("spring.datasource.url", PG::getJdbcUrl);
    r.add("spring.datasource.username", PG::getUsername);
    r.add("spring.datasource.password", PG::getPassword);
}
  • Container reuse for speed (opt-in, dev machines)

    .withReuse(true) plus testcontainers.reuse.enable=true in ~/.testcontainers.properties keeps the container alive between test runs on a developer's machine — huge feedback-loop win. Disable in CI where each run should be pristine. Always reset state (truncate / @Sql) between tests since the container is shared.

  • Why a real DB catches what H2 hides
