    SQL dialect (Postgres jsonb, RETURNING, partial indexes), constraint timing (deferred FKs), isolation-level behavior, sequence/identity generation, and migration scripts all differ. An in-memory DB silently rewrites or ignores them, so the bug ships. Same engine + same major version is the rule.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • State leaking between shared-container tests

    A reused/static container keeps data from the previous test → order-dependent flakiness. Fix: @Sql cleanup, TRUNCATE ... RESTART IDENTITY, or @Transactional rollback per test (careful: rollback hides commit-time constraint checks).

  • Wrong image version → false confidence

    Testing on postgres:13 while prod runs 16, or using postgres:latest (non-deterministic). Pin the exact production major version, and a digest if you need reproducibility.

  • No Docker in CI / slow cold starts

    CI runner without a Docker daemon → every test errors. And cold-pulling images each build adds minutes. Fix: ensure Docker-in-Docker or a socket mount, cache images, and prefer -alpine slim images.

  • Per-method container start blows up the suite

    Forgetting static on the @Container field restarts Postgres for every @Test — a 30-test class goes from 4s to 60s+. The class-level lifecycle is almost always what you want.

    // Bug: restarts per test (no 'static')
@Container PostgreSQLContainer<?> pg = new PostgreSQLContainer<>("postgres:16");
// Fix:
@Container static PostgreSQLContainer<?> pg = new PostgreSQLContainer<>("postgres:16");
Example
@SpringBootTest
@Testcontainers
class OrderRepositoryIT {

    @Container
    static PostgreSQLContainer<?> postgres =
        new PostgreSQLContainer<>("postgres:16-alpine");

    @DynamicPropertySource
    static void datasourceProps(DynamicPropertyRegistry registry) {
        registry.add("spring.datasource.url", postgres::getJdbcUrl);
        registry.add("spring.datasource.username", postgres::getUsername);
        registry.add("spring.datasource.password", postgres::getPassword);
        registry.add("spring.flyway.enabled", () -> "true");
    }

    @Autowired OrderRepository repository;

    @Test
    void rejectsDuplicateIdempotencyKey() {
        repository.save(new Order("idem-123"));
        // real Postgres UNIQUE index — a mock could never prove this
        assertThatThrownBy(() -> repository.save(new Order("idem-123")))
            .isInstanceOf(DataIntegrityViolationException.class);
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I run integration tests on a real Postgres via Testcontainers, not H2 — H2's "Postgres mode" is a lie that hides jsonb, constraints, and migration bugs. I use a static/singleton container with @DynamicPropertySource (or @ServiceConnection on Boot 3.1+) and reuse on dev machines for fast feedback, pristine containers in CI, pinned to the production major version.'
Key focus
Contract testing & mutation testing
Concept

Contract testing verifies that a service consumer and producer still agree on the message shape — without deploying both and running slow cross-service E2E. Mutation testing measures how effective your tests are by deliberately introducing bugs and checking whether a test catches them.

Why it exists: E2E across many services is slow, flaky, and needs everything deployed at once. Contracts let each side test alone yet stay compatible. And line coverage lies — it proves code ran, not that anything was asserted; mutation testing measures the assertions, not the execution.

Analogy: A contract is the agreed shape of a plug and socket — each factory tests its half against the spec, no need to ship both to one room. Mutation testing is a fire drill: deliberately start a small fire to check the alarm actually goes off.

How it works
  1. Consumer-driven contract: the consumer writes its expectation of the response → a contract (a Pact JSON / Spring Cloud Contract DSL).
  2. The contract is published (e.g. to a Pact Broker); the producer runs provider verification to prove its real responses satisfy it.
  3. can-i-deploy: CI asks the broker whether this version is compatible with everything currently deployed before shipping.
  4. Mutation testing (PIT): mutate the bytecode (flip > to >=, return null, remove a call), re-run tests; a killed mutant means a test failed = good.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Producer renames a JSON field: total_amount → amount. Provider verification against the consumer's contract goes red in the producer's CI before deploy, not at 2 AM in prod.
  2. Replacing brittle cross-service E2E: 12 microservices, a full E2E suite that flakes weekly → swap most of it for contract tests each service runs in isolation.
  3. 90% coverage, weak tests: mutation testing shows a 45% mutation score — half the "covered" code has no real assertion. The number reveals theatre.
  4. Boundary bug survives: code uses >= but tests never probe the boundary; PIT mutates it to > and the surviving mutant pinpoints the missing test case.
  5. can-i-deploy gate in the pipeline: a deploy is blocked because a not-yet-updated consumer still depends on the old contract — caught automatically.

A strong engineer knows coverage is an execution metric, not a quality metric, and reaches for mutation testing to prove the test suite has teeth — while using contracts to get cross-service safety without the cost and flakiness of full E2E.

What to understand deeply
  • Consumer-driven: the consumer's needs define the contract

    The consumer only encodes the fields it actually uses. The producer is free to add fields (backward-compatible) but must not remove or retype a consumed one without the verification failing. This catches breaking changes at the producer, in isolation, with no running consumer.

    // Pact consumer test (defines the expectation)
builder.given("user 42 exists")
  .uponReceiving("a request for user 42")
  .path("/users/42").method("GET")
  .willRespondWith().status(200)
  .body(newJsonBody(o -> {
      o.numberType("id", 42);
      o.stringType("email", "a@x.com"); // only fields the consumer uses
  }).build());
  • Contracts ≠ E2E — and that's the point

    A contract test never starts both services together. The consumer test runs against a mock that is the contract; the producer test replays the contract against its real controller. Compatibility is proven without orchestrating a whole environment — fast, deterministic, parallelizable.

  • Mutation score vs line coverage

    Line coverage = "did this line execute during a test." A test with zero assertions can hit 100% coverage. Mutation score = "of all the deliberate bugs PIT injected, what fraction did a test catch (kill)." It directly measures assertion strength — the metric that actually correlates with finding regressions.

    // 100% line coverage, 0% mutation score:
@Test void runsWithoutError() {
    calculator.discount(order); // executes, asserts NOTHING
}
// PIT mutates discount() → every mutant SURVIVES → test is worthless
  • Mutators and where to spend the budget

    PIT applies mutators: negate conditionals, change boundaries (<↔<=), return defaults (null/0/empty), remove void calls. A surviving mutant is a precise pointer to a missing assertion. It's expensive, so run it on critical domain logic (pricing, auth) — not getters or DTOs.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • Treating coverage % as a quality gate

    "80% coverage required" leads to assertion-free tests written purely to pass the gate — coverage theatre. The number rises, real safety doesn't. Gate on mutation score for critical packages instead, and treat coverage as a rough floor, not a ceiling.

  • Contracts that drift / aren't verified in CI

    Contracts written once and never run in the producer's pipeline rot silently. Without provider verification on every producer build (and can-i-deploy before release), the contract is just stale documentation that gives false safety.

  • Over-specified contracts (matching exact values)

    Asserting id == 42 instead of "is a number" makes the producer verification break on unrelated data changes. Use type/regex matchers so the contract pins shape, not incidental example values.

  • Mutation testing too slow / run on everything

    PIT runs the test suite once per mutant — minutes to hours on a large codebase. Running it on the whole project in CI stalls the pipeline. Scope it to changed classes (incremental analysis) or critical packages, and run the full pass nightly.

Example
// Producer-side verification (Spring Cloud Contract generated base)
// Contract (Groovy DSL) lives with the producer, published to consumers:
Contract.make {
    request {
        method GET()
        url '/users/42'
    }
    response {
        status OK()
        headers { contentType(applicationJson()) }
        body(
            id: 42,
            email: $(regex('[^@]+@[^@]+')) // shape, not exact value
        )
    }
}

// PIT (Maven) — measure test effectiveness, not coverage
// mvn org.pitest:pitest-maven:mutationCoverage
//   -DtargetClasses=com.shop.pricing.*
//   -DmutationThreshold=80   // fail build if mutation score < 80%
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Contract tests give me cross-service safety without slow flaky E2E — the consumer defines the shape, the producer verifies it in its own CI, and can-i-deploy gates the release. And I don't trust coverage %: a test with no assertion still shows green. I run mutation testing (PIT) on critical logic — if a mutant survives, that line had no real assertion.'
Deep dive — Testing
Architecture flow: the test pyramid mapped onto a CI pipeline
            ▲  slower, fewer, more brittle
            │
     ┌──────┴───────┐
     │     E2E       │   ~10 tests   ~5-15 min   real env, smoke only
     ├──────────────┤
     │  Contract     │   ~80 tests   ~30 s       Pact / Spring Cloud Contract
     ├──────────────┤                            (per-service, no full env)
     │ Integration   │   ~300 tests  ~2-4 min    Testcontainers: real PG/Kafka
     ├──────────────┤
     │     Unit      │   ~3000 tests ~20 s       pure logic, mocked boundaries
     └──────────────┘
            │  faster, more, more reliable
            ▼

CI pipeline (fail fast, cheapest stage first):

  push ─▶ [unit] ─▶ [integration: Testcontainers] ─▶ [contract verify]
   20s        2-4 min                                 30s
                                                        │
                                                        ▼
                                              [can-i-deploy?] ──no──▶ block
                                                        │ yes
                                                        ▼
                                              [deploy] ─▶ [E2E smoke]

  • each stage runs only if the previous is green (fail fast = cheap feedback)
  • mutation testing (PIT) runs NIGHTLY on critical packages, not per-push
  • E2E is a thin smoke layer, NOT where logic is verified
Anti-pattern (ice-cream cone): huge E2E layer, thin unit layer
  → 40-min flaky suite, nobody trusts red, coverage% high / confidence low
War story — the mock that hid an N+1

Service: billing-export-api, generates monthly invoices for ~50k customers in a nightly batch.

Symptom: Green build, 92% coverage, shipped Friday. Saturday's batch ran 6 hours instead of 8 minutes and held a DB connection the whole time, tripping the connection-leak alarm. No exception, just glacial.

Investigation: The service test mocked invoiceRepository.findForMonth() to return a hand-built list of fully-populated Invoice objects with their lineItems already in memory. Every assertion passed. Enabling Hibernate SQL logging against a real DB showed 50,001 queries: one to load invoices, then one lazy SELECT per invoice for its line items — a textbook N+1.

Root cause: Invoice.lineItems was @OneToMany(fetch = LAZY). The mock returned objects whose collections were already initialized, so the lazy proxy / N+1 behavior could not exist in the test. The mock encoded an assumption (collections pre-loaded) that production never honored. The test proved the mapping logic, never the data access.

Fix (immediate): Add a Testcontainers integration test that runs the real query against Postgres and asserts the SQL count via a datasource-proxy / QueryCountHolder assertion (≤ 2 queries). It went red instantly. Fixed with a JOIN FETCH / @EntityGraph.

Fix (structural): Rule: any code whose behavior is the query (fetch strategy, transaction boundary, pagination) must have a Testcontainers test; repositories are not unit-tested with mocks. Added an N+1 detector (query-count assertion) to the integration base class.

Lesson: A mock can only be as correct as your assumptions about the thing it replaces — and lazy loading, transaction boundaries, and N+1 are exactly the assumptions developers get wrong. Mocking the DB tests your beliefs about the DB, not the DB.

Production-grade integration test (Testcontainers + @DynamicPropertySource) + a contract stub
// ---- Shared base: singleton container started once for the whole module ----
@SpringBootTest
@Testcontainers
@Sql(scripts = "/sql/clean.sql", executionPhase = BEFORE_TEST_METHOD)
abstract class AbstractIntegrationTest {

    @Container
    static final PostgreSQLContainer<?> POSTGRES =
        new PostgreSQLContainer<>("postgres:16-alpine")
            .withReuse(true); // dev machines reuse; CI uses a fresh daemon

    @DynamicPropertySource
    static void registerProps(DynamicPropertyRegistry registry) {
        registry.add("spring.datasource.url", POSTGRES::getJdbcUrl);
        registry.add("spring.datasource.username", POSTGRES::getUsername);
        registry.add("spring.datasource.password", POSTGRES::getPassword);
        registry.add("spring.jpa.hibernate.ddl-auto", () -> "validate");
        registry.add("spring.flyway.enabled", () -> "true"); // run real migrations
    }
}

// ---- The actual integration test: proves the QUERY, with an N+1 guard ----
class InvoiceRepositoryIT extends AbstractIntegrationTest {

    @Autowired InvoiceRepository repository;
    @Autowired TestEntityManager em;

    @Test
    void loadsInvoicesWithLineItemsWithoutNPlusOne() {
        seedInvoicesWithLines(50);
        QueryCountHolder.clear();

        List<Invoice> invoices = repository.findForMonth(YearMonth.of(2026, 6));

        // real Postgres + real fetch strategy — a mock could not catch this
        assertThat(invoices).hasSize(50);
        assertThat(invoices.get(0).getLineItems()).isNotEmpty();
        long selects = QueryCountHolder.getGrandTotal().getSelect();
        assertThat(selects)
            .as("must JOIN FETCH, not N+1")
            .isLessThanOrEqualTo(2);
    }

    @Test
    void enforcesUniqueIdempotencyKeyAtDbLevel() {
        repository.saveAndFlush(new Invoice("idem-001"));
        assertThatThrownBy(() -> repository.saveAndFlush(new Invoice("idem-001")))
            .isInstanceOf(DataIntegrityViolationException.class);
    }
}

// ---- Consumer-driven contract stub (Pact + JUnit 5) ----
@ExtendWith(PactConsumerTestExt.class)
@PactTestFor(providerName = "user-service", pactMethod = "userExists")
class UserClientContractTest {

    @Pact(consumer = "billing-export-api")
    V4Pact userExists(PactDslWithProvider builder) {
        return builder
            .given("user 42 exists")
            .uponReceiving("a lookup for user 42")
                .path("/users/42").method("GET")
            .willRespondWith().status(200)
                .body(newJsonBody(o -> {
                    o.numberType("id", 42);          // shape, not exact value
                    o.stringMatcher("email", "[^@]+@[^@]+", "a@x.com");
                }).build())
            .toPact(V4Pact.class);
    }

    @Test
    void clientParsesUser(MockServer mockServer) {
        var client = new UserClient(mockServer.getUrl());
        User u = client.findById(42);
        assertThat(u.email()).contains("@"); // verify the consumer can read it
    }
}
Interview scenario — "How do you decide what to test where, and how do you handle a flaky suite?"

You join a team with 85% coverage but bugs keep reaching production. Where do you start?

Coverage tells me code ran, not that it was asserted. My first move is to run mutation testing — PIT — on the critical packages: pricing, auth, anything money-touching. If the mutation score is, say, 45% while line coverage is 85%, that gap is exactly the bugs leaking out: half the "covered" code has tests with no real assertion. I'd fix the weak tests the surviving mutants point at before writing any new ones.

A teammate wrote a service test that mocks the repository. Is that fine?

It depends what the test is proving. If the behavior under test is pure logic — a discount calculation — mocking the repo to feed it data is fine; it's a fast solitary unit test. But if the behavior is the data access — the query, the fetch strategy, a unique constraint, a transaction boundary — a mock proves nothing and actively hides N+1s and lazy-loading bugs. That class of behavior must go through a Testcontainers integration test against real Postgres. The mock only encodes our assumptions about the DB.

Why Testcontainers and not just H2 for speed?

Because H2's "Postgres compatibility mode" is a lie for exactly the things that break in prod — jsonb, arrays, ON CONFLICT, partial indexes, migration DDL. A green H2 test gives false confidence. Testcontainers runs the same engine and major version as prod. I pay for the startup once with a static singleton container, reuse it on dev machines for fast feedback, and use fresh containers in CI. Cross-service compatibility I cover with contract tests, not slow E2E — the consumer defines the shape, the producer verifies it in its own pipeline, and can-i-deploy gates the release.

Your integration suite is flaky — passes locally, fails 1 in 10 in CI. How do you handle it?

First, I do not add a blanket retry — that hides the defect and erodes trust in the suite. I quarantine the flaky tests out of the gating job so they stop blocking everyone, but I track them as bugs, not "known flakes." Then I hunt the usual root causes: shared mutable state between tests — a static container not cleaned between methods, a singleton holding data; test-ordering dependence — one test relying on another's side effect; and the real clock — code calling Instant.now() instead of an injected Clock, so an assertion straddles a second boundary or midnight.

Give me a concrete one you've actually fixed.

A token-expiry test asserted "expires in exactly 24h" using Instant.now() in both the code and the assertion. They were computed microseconds apart, so 99% of the time they matched — but when the run crossed a second tick, off by one. The fix was structural: inject a Clock everywhere time is read, and pin it with Clock.fixed(...) in tests. That killed a whole category of flakiness. The principle is the same as the mocking question — non-determinism (time, randomness, ordering, shared state) is what makes tests flaky, so you make it deterministic at the boundary instead of papering over it with retries.

17. Resilience & Fault Tolerance

In a distributed system, failure is the normal case — design so that one slow or broken dependency cannot take the whole system down.

#17
Key focus
Timeouts, retries (backoff + jitter), idempotency
Concept

A timeout caps how long you wait for a remote call. A retry re-attempts a failed call. Backoff + jitter spreads retries out over time; an idempotency key lets the server recognize a duplicate request and not perform the action twice.

Why it exists: Across a network, calls don't fail cleanly — they hang. A call with no timeout holds a thread forever; enough of them and the pool drains and the whole app "hangs" while CPU is idle. Retries recover from transient blips, but only safely when bounded, jittered and idempotent.

Analogy: Calling someone who doesn't pick up — a timeout is hanging up after 5 rings instead of holding the line all day; jitter is everyone NOT redialling at the exact same second.

How it works
  1. Timeout: every remote call gets a connect timeout + a read timeout (and a DB query timeout). When it fires, the thread is freed and the call fails fast instead of hanging.
  2. Classify the failure: retry only transient errors (connection reset, 503, timeout) on idempotent operations; never blindly retry a non-idempotent write.
  3. Backoff + jitter: wait base * 2^attempt plus a random jitter so clients don't all retry on the same tick (avoids a synchronized retry storm).
  4. Retry budget + idempotency key: cap total retries to a small % of traffic; attach an idempotency key so a duplicated write is deduplicated server-side.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. HTTP client with no read timeout: downstream gets slow, every worker thread blocks on the socket, the pool drains → the app looks "hung" while CPU is ~0%. Set connect + read timeouts on every client.
  2. Retrying a payment POST: the charge succeeded but the response timed out; a blind retry charges the customer twice. Use an idempotency key so the gateway returns the stored result.
  3. Retry storm: a downstream hiccups, every client retries 3× at the same instant → 3× load lands on a recovering service → it falls over again. Add jitter + a retry budget + a circuit breaker.
  4. Retrying a 400/409: retrying a non-transient client error wastes capacity and never succeeds. Only retry on transient codes (timeouts, 502/503/504, connection errors).
  5. Inner + outer retries stacked: the HTTP client retries 3×, the service retries 3×, the gateway retries 3× → 27× amplification. Retry at exactly one layer.

A strong engineer treats a missing timeout as a production incident waiting to happen, retries only what is transient and idempotent, and assumes that any retry can and will be duplicated.

What to understand deeply
  • No timeout is the root cause of most outages

    Defaults lie: many HTTP/JDBC clients default to an infinite read timeout. One slow downstream then converts into "all my threads are blocked." Set both a connect timeout (TCP handshake) and a read timeout (waiting for bytes), and a DB statement/query timeout. The total budget for a request must be larger than the sum of its inner timeouts, or the outer one fires first and masks the real failure.

    // Every client gets explicit timeouts
OkHttpClient client = new OkHttpClient.Builder()
    .connectTimeout(Duration.ofMillis(500))
    .readTimeout(Duration.ofSeconds(2))
    .callTimeout(Duration.ofSeconds(3)) // hard ceiling for the whole call
    .build();
  • Retry only transient + idempotent operations

    GET/PUT/DELETE are naturally idempotent; POST usually is not. Retry on connection errors, timeouts and 502/503/504 — never on 4xx (the request itself is wrong). For a non-idempotent write, make it idempotent first (idempotency key) before allowing any retry.

  • Exponential backoff WITH jitter — not without

    Plain exponential backoff still synchronizes: every client that failed at T retries at T+1s, T+3s, T+7s together. Adding randomness ("full jitter": sleep = random(0, base * 2^attempt)) de-correlates clients so the recovering downstream sees a smooth trickle, not a wall.

    // Full jitter
long cap = 8_000, base = 200;
long expo = Math.min(cap, base * (1L << attempt));
long sleep = ThreadLocalRandom.current().nextLong(0, expo + 1);
  • Retry budgets stop amplification

    Without a budget, retries multiply load exactly when the system is weakest. A retry budget caps retries to e.g. 10% of requests; once exceeded, calls fail fast instead of retrying. This bounds the extra load a struggling downstream can ever receive from you.

  • Idempotency keys make retries safe

    The client generates a unique key per logical operation and sends it on every attempt. The server stores (key → result); a repeated key returns the stored result without re-executing. This is what makes "retry the charge" safe.

    // Server side: dedup on the key
var existing = idempotencyStore.find(key);
if (existing != null) return existing.result();   // replay, do not re-charge
PaymentResult r = gateway.charge(req);
idempotencyStore.save(key, r, Duration.ofHours(24));
return r;
Production issues / pitfalls
  • Blind retries amplify load → retry storm

    A downstream returns a few errors; the client library silently retries 3× with no jitter. Now a 1% error blip becomes 3× traffic, all synchronized, on a service that was already struggling → it tips over completely. The retry was the outage, not the original blip.

  • Retries layered at every tier

    Feign retries, the service retries, the API gateway retries. 3 × 3 × 3 = 27 attempts per user click. Pick exactly ONE layer to retry (usually closest to the call) and disable retries everywhere else — including the hidden defaults in HTTP clients.

  • Double-charge from a retried non-idempotent POST

    The charge succeeded but the 200 response was lost to a timeout; the client retries and charges again. Without an idempotency key the customer pays twice and reconciliation is a nightmare.

    // Bad: retry a raw charge
@Retryable // on a POST /charge with no idempotency key
PaymentResult charge(ChargeRequest r) { ... } // can double-charge

// Good: caller supplies a stable key, server dedups
PaymentResult charge(ChargeRequest r, String idempotencyKey) { ... }
  • Outer timeout shorter than inner timeout

    Request budget is 1s but the read timeout is 2s → the request always aborts before the call could finish, every call looks like a timeout, and retries pile on. Budget timeouts top-down: outer > sum of inner.

  • "Timeout" that doesn't cancel the work

    orTimeout() on a CompletableFuture completes the future but the underlying thread/socket may keep running, still holding a connection. Pair timeouts with real cancellation (interrupt, close, gRPC deadline) so resources are actually released.

Example
// Transient-only retry with exponential backoff + full jitter + budget
public <T> T callWithRetry(Supplier<T> call) {
    int maxAttempts = 3;
    for (int attempt = 0; ; attempt++) {
        try {
            return call.get();
        } catch (TransientException e) {           // timeout / 503 / conn reset only
            if (attempt >= maxAttempts - 1 || !budget.tryConsume()) {
                throw e;                            // budget exhausted → fail fast
            }
            long expo = Math.min(8_000L, 200L * (1L << attempt));
            long jittered = ThreadLocalRandom.current().nextLong(0, expo + 1);
            sleep(jittered);
        }
        // NOTE: never reaches here for non-transient (4xx) errors — they propagate
    }
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'Every remote call gets a timeout — no timeout is how a slow dependency hangs the whole app. I only retry transient, idempotent calls, with exponential backoff plus jitter and a retry budget, and I make writes safe with an idempotency key so a retry can never double-charge.'
Key focus
Circuit breaker, bulkhead, fallback (Resilience4j)
Concept

A circuit breaker stops calling a failing dependency and fails fast. A bulkhead isolates each dependency into its own pool/permit set so one cannot starve the others. A fallback returns a degraded-but-useful response instead of an error.

Why it exists: Piling more requests onto a dying dependency only makes it die faster and ties up your threads. Failing fast frees resources and gives the downstream room to recover; isolation contains the blast radius; fallbacks keep the user experience alive.

Analogy: A circuit breaker in your house — it trips to stop a fault from burning the building down. Bulkheads are the watertight compartments of a ship: one flooded compartment doesn't sink the vessel.

How it works
  1. CLOSED: calls pass through; the breaker records success/failure over a sliding window.
  2. → OPEN: when the failure rate (or slow-call rate) crosses the threshold, the breaker opens and short-circuits every call for waitDurationInOpenState — instant failure, no downstream contact.
  3. → HALF_OPEN: after the wait, it permits a small number of trial calls. If they succeed → back to CLOSED; if they fail → back to OPEN.
  4. Bulkhead + fallback: each dependency runs behind its own bulkhead (semaphore or thread pool); when a call is rejected, open, or timed out, the fallback method supplies a cached/default value.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Recommendations service is down: the product page must still render. Circuit breaker opens, fallback returns a cached "popular items" list — degraded, not broken.
  2. One slow dependency starves the pool: without a bulkhead, slow score-api calls consume every shared thread and unrelated endpoints hang. A per-dependency bulkhead caps its concurrency so others keep flowing.
  3. Slow calls, not errors: the dependency returns 200 but takes 9s. A failure-rate breaker never trips; configure a slowCallRateThreshold so "slow" counts as failure.
  4. Breaker flaps open/closed: minimumNumberOfCalls too low → one or two failures trip it on a tiny sample. Raise the minimum sample size and tune the window.
  5. Fallback that also calls the network: the fallback hits another remote service that's also down → it fails too. Fallbacks must be local: cache, default, or empty.

A strong engineer wires breaker + bulkhead + fallback together and, crucially, gets the decorator order right — because the order changes what each layer actually counts and protects.

What to understand deeply
  • The three states and their transitions

    CLOSED → OPEN on threshold breach; OPEN → HALF_OPEN after the wait timer; HALF_OPEN → CLOSED if trial calls pass, or HALF_OPEN → OPEN if they fail. The point of OPEN is to stop calling — fail fast frees your threads and lets the downstream breathe instead of being hammered while it's down.

    resilience4j.circuitbreaker.instances.scoreApi:
  sliding-window-type: COUNT_BASED
  sliding-window-size: 50
  minimum-number-of-calls: 20      # don't judge on a tiny sample
  failure-rate-threshold: 50       # % failures → OPEN
  slow-call-rate-threshold: 80     # % slow calls → OPEN
  slow-call-duration-threshold: 2s
  wait-duration-in-open-state: 10s
  permitted-number-of-calls-in-half-open-state: 5
  • Bulkhead isolation: semaphore vs thread-pool

    A semaphore bulkhead caps the number of concurrent calls on the caller's own thread — cheap, no context switch, but it can't enforce a timeout on a blocking call. A thread-pool bulkhead runs the call on a separate bounded pool, isolating it fully and enabling a real timeout — at the cost of a thread hop and lost ThreadLocals/MDC. Use a pool bulkhead for slow blocking dependencies; semaphore for fast ones.

  • Fallback / graceful degradation must be local

    A fallback fires when the call is rejected (bulkhead full), short-circuited (breaker open), or times out. It should return something cheap and local — a cached value, a sensible default, an empty list — and it should be wired per-failure-type, not "catch Throwable and hide it."

    private Score scoreFallback(CustomerId id, Throwable t) {
    Log.warnf(t, "score fallback for %s", id);
    return cache.getIfPresent(id)      // stale-but-useful
        != null ? cache.get(id) : Score.unscored();
}
  • Decorator order: Retry ▸ CircuitBreaker ▸ RateLimiter ▸ TimeLimiter ▸ Bulkhead

    From outermost to innermost. Retry is outermost so one retry attempt is a full pass through the inner stack (and a retry can re-close a flapping path). The circuit breaker sits inside retry so it counts each real attempt — including a timeout — as a failure, and once OPEN it short-circuits before any retry burns budget. TimeLimiter wraps the bulkhead-isolated call so a slow call is converted into a countable failure. Bulkhead is innermost, right around the actual blocking call, to cap its concurrency. Get this backwards and, e.g., the breaker counts retries as separate calls, or the timeout never sees the real call.

    // Resilience4j programmatic order (innermost first to outermost)
Supplier<T> decorated = Decorators.ofSupplier(call)
    .withBulkhead(bulkhead)            // innermost: cap concurrency
    .withTimeLimiter(timeLimiter, sched)
    .withRateLimiter(rateLimiter)
    .withCircuitBreaker(circuitBreaker)
    .withRetry(retry)                  // outermost: retries the whole stack
    .decorate();
Production issues / pitfalls
  • No breaker → cascading failure

    Without a breaker, every request keeps calling the dead dependency, every thread blocks on its timeout, your pool drains, and your service goes down too — the failure cascades upstream. A breaker turns a downstream outage into fast local errors you can fall back from.

  • Breaker on failure rate but the problem is latency

    The dependency returns HTTP 200 in 9 seconds. Failures = 0%, so a failure-rate-only breaker never opens while your threads drown in slow calls. You must configure slowCallRateThreshold + slowCallDurationThreshold.

  • Wrong decorator order

    If the circuit breaker is OUTSIDE the retry, the breaker sees one "logical" call and the retries hammer the downstream behind its back; if the TimeLimiter is outside the breaker, slow calls aren't counted as breaker failures. The recommended order (Retry ▸ Breaker ▸ RateLimiter ▸ TimeLimiter ▸ Bulkhead) exists for these reasons.

  • Shared thread pool instead of per-dependency bulkhead

    All dependencies share one executor; the slowest one consumes every thread and starves the rest. This is exactly the "one slow dependency takes everything down" failure. Give each dependency its own bulkhead with a bounded limit.

  • Fallback hides real errors / does I/O

    A fallback that catches everything and returns "OK" masks incidents (your dashboards stay green during an outage), and a fallback that itself calls another service can fail or slow down too. Keep fallbacks local and keep emitting metrics for fallback rate.

Example
@CircuitBreaker(name = "scoreApi", fallbackMethod = "scoreFallback")
@Bulkhead(name = "scoreApi", type = Bulkhead.Type.SEMAPHORE)
@Retry(name = "scoreApi")            // retries the whole annotated stack
public Score fetchScore(CustomerId id) {
    return scoreClient.fetch(id);    // remote call with its own timeout
}

// Fallback signature = same args + a trailing Throwable
private Score scoreFallback(CustomerId id, CallNotPermittedException open) {
    // breaker is OPEN → don't even try; serve a default fast
    return Score.unscored();
}

private Score scoreFallback(CustomerId id, Throwable t) {
    Log.warnf(t, "score fallback for %s", id);
    return Score.unscored();         // degraded but the page still renders
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'A circuit breaker fails fast so I stop hammering a dying dependency and free my threads — CLOSED, OPEN, HALF_OPEN. I isolate each dependency with a bulkhead so one slow one can't starve the others, and I add a local fallback for graceful degradation. Order matters: retry outside the breaker, breaker counting timeouts as failures, bulkhead innermost around the real call.'
Key focus
Rate limiting, load shedding & backpressure
Concept

Rate limiting caps how many requests are allowed per unit time. Load shedding rejects work early when you're already overloaded. Backpressure is a consumer signalling a producer to slow down so queues don't grow unbounded.

Why it exists: Capacity is finite. It's far better to reject some requests cleanly (429/503) than to accept everything and collapse — a half-served system that's falling over serves nobody. These three protect against being overwhelmed, from outside (rate limit) and inside (shed/backpressure).

Analogy: A nightclub with a fixed capacity — the bouncer (rate limiter/load shedder) turns people away at the door so the people already inside aren't crushed.

How it works
  1. Rate limit: a token bucket holds N tokens, refilled at a steady rate; a request consumes a token or is rejected/queued. Allows bursts up to the bucket size.
  2. Distributed: for many instances, keep the counter/bucket in Redis (INCR+EXPIRE or a Lua token-bucket script) so the limit is shared, not per-pod.
  3. Load shed: when queue depth or latency crosses a threshold, reject low-priority work early with 429/503 instead of accepting it and dying.
  4. Backpressure: in reactive/streaming pipelines the consumer requests n items at a time; producers buffer/drop/pause (Reactor onBackpressure*, Kafka pause()/resume()) rather than overrun memory.
Common real-world scenarios
  1. Public API abuse / runaway client: one tenant sends 10k req/s. Per-key rate limiting (token bucket in Redis) caps them at the agreed quota and returns 429 with Retry-After.
  2. Protecting a fragile downstream: a legacy mainframe handles 50 req/s. A client-side rate limiter in front of it ensures you never exceed that, even under a traffic spike.
  3. Overload collapse: traffic doubles; instead of queueing everything until it OOMs, the service sheds load — rejects new requests early with 503 so in-flight work completes.
  4. Kafka consumer falling behind: processing is slower than the incoming rate; without backpressure (max.poll.records / pause), the consumer buffers unboundedly and OOMs. Bound the in-flight work.
  5. Fast producer, slow DB sink: a reactive stream reads faster than it writes → onBackpressureBuffer grows without limit → OOM. Use a bounded buffer with a drop/latest strategy or signal demand upstream.

A strong engineer separates the two intents clearly: rate limiting protects the downstream from you; load shedding protects you from incoming traffic you can't handle.

What to understand deeply
  • Token bucket vs leaky bucket

    Token bucket: tokens refill at rate R into a bucket of size B; a request spends a token. It permits bursts up to B then settles to R — good for APIs where short bursts are fine. Leaky bucket: requests enter a queue that drains at a constant rate — output is perfectly smooth regardless of input burstiness, good for protecting a downstream that demands a steady rate. Token bucket = burst-tolerant; leaky bucket = smoothing.

  • Client-side vs server-side vs distributed

    Server-side limits protect the server from clients; client-side limits protect a fragile downstream from your own service. With many instances, an in-memory limit of "100/s" becomes "100/s × pods" — you must use a shared store (Redis) for a true global limit.

    -- Redis token bucket (Lua, atomic): refill + try-consume
local tokens = tonumber(redis.call('HGET', KEYS[1], 'tokens') or capacity)
local last   = tonumber(redis.call('HGET', KEYS[1], 'ts') or now)
tokens = math.min(capacity, tokens + (now - last) * refillPerMs)
if tokens < 1 then return 0 end          -- reject → caller returns 429
redis.call('HSET', KEYS[1], 'tokens', tokens - 1, 'ts', now)
return 1                                   -- allow
  • Load shedding: reject early, fail cheap

    When overloaded, accepting a request and timing out 30s later is the worst outcome — you spent resources and still failed. Shed early: when concurrency/queue/latency exceeds a limit, immediately return 429 (rate) or 503 (overload) with Retry-After, and prioritize — shed low-value traffic (e.g. bulk/anonymous) before critical (checkout) traffic. Adaptive concurrency limits (gradient/AIMD) discover the safe limit automatically.

  • Backpressure = bounded demand, not unbounded buffers

    The fix for "consumer slower than producer" is never "a bigger buffer" — that just delays the OOM. The consumer must signal demand. In Reactor, request(n) pulls only what it can handle; onBackpressureBuffer/Drop/Latest define the overflow policy. In Kafka, tune max.poll.records and pause()/resume() partitions while you catch up. A bounded queue with a rejection policy is backpressure for thread pools.

Production issues / pitfalls
  • In-memory rate limit × N pods

    You configure "1000 req/s per key" in-process and run 8 pods → the real global limit is 8000/s and the downstream still gets crushed. Either divide the limit by replica count (fragile during scaling) or use a shared Redis limiter.

  • No load shedding → queue-of-death

    Under overload the service accepts everything; the request queue and connection pool grow until latency is 30s+ and it OOMs or the LB marks it unhealthy and removes it — sending its load to the remaining pods, which then also fall over. Shedding early breaks this spiral.

  • Unbounded buffer "fixing" backpressure

    onBackpressureBuffer() with no max, or an unbounded LinkedBlockingQueue in an executor → memory grows until OOM under a sustained burst. Always bound the buffer/queue and choose an explicit overflow policy (drop, latest, or block/CallerRuns).

    // Bad: unbounded — OOM under sustained load
flux.onBackpressureBuffer();              // no limit
new ThreadPoolExecutor(.., new LinkedBlockingQueue<>()); // unbounded queue

// Good: bounded + explicit overflow policy
flux.onBackpressureBuffer(1000, BufferOverflowStrategy.DROP_OLDEST);
new ThreadPoolExecutor(.., new ArrayBlockingQueue<>(1000),
    new ThreadPoolExecutor.CallerRunsPolicy()); // backpressure
  • 429 with no Retry-After / no jitter

    You correctly reject with 429 but give no Retry-After; clients retry instantly and in lockstep → a self-inflicted retry storm. Return Retry-After and expect clients to back off with jitter.

  • Confusing rate limiting with load shedding

    Treating them as the same control leads to limits that protect neither party well. Rate limiting is a contract about steady throughput to a downstream; load shedding is an emergency valve based on your own real-time health (queue depth, latency, CPU). You usually need both.

Example
// Resilience4j RateLimiter (protect a fragile downstream at 50/s)
resilience4j.ratelimiter.instances.legacyCore:
  limit-for-period: 50         # permits per refresh period
  limit-refresh-period: 1s
  timeout-duration: 0          # 0 = fail fast (shed) instead of queueing

@RateLimiter(name = "legacyCore", fallbackMethod = "shed")
public Quote getQuote(QuoteRequest r) {
    return legacyClient.quote(r);
}

// Load shedding: over the limit → fail fast with 429, don't queue
private Quote shed(QuoteRequest r, RequestNotPermitted ex) {
    throw new TooManyRequestsException("rate limited", Duration.ofSeconds(1));
    // mapped to HTTP 429 with Retry-After: 1
}
Interview answer tip: Say: 'I distinguish rate limiting — protecting a downstream with a token bucket, distributed via Redis when I have many instances — from load shedding, which protects me by rejecting early with 429/503 when my queue depth or latency says I'm overloaded. In streaming systems I rely on backpressure with bounded buffers, never an unbounded queue, because a bigger buffer just delays the OOM.'
Deep dive — Resilience
Architecture flow: the resilience decorator stack + circuit-breaker state machine
Request: GET /products/{id}  (needs score from score-api)
                       │
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   Retry (outermost)  │  transient only, backoff + jitter, budget
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   Circuit Breaker    │  OPEN → fail fast (no downstream call)
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   Rate Limiter       │  cap req/s to protect downstream
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   Time Limiter       │  slow call → timeout → counts as failure
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
            ┌──────────────────────┐
            │   Bulkhead (inner)   │  cap concurrency to score-api
            └──────────┬───────────┘
                       ▼
                 [ score-api ]  →  on any failure: fallback() → cached/default Score


Circuit-breaker state machine:

        failureRate ≥ threshold
        OR slowCallRate ≥ threshold
   ┌────────────┐ ───────────────────────────▶ ┌────────────┐
   │   CLOSED   │                               │    OPEN    │
   │ calls pass │                               │ fail fast, │
   │ count fails│ ◀─────────────────────────── │ no calls   │
   └────────────┘   trial calls succeed         └─────┬──────┘
         ▲                                            │ after
         │                                            │ waitDurationInOpenState
         │          trial calls fail                  ▼
         │     ┌──────────────────────────────┐ ┌────────────┐
         └──── │  back to OPEN on failure      │ │ HALF_OPEN  │
               └──────────────────────────────┘ │ N trial    │
                                                 │ calls only │
                                                 └────────────┘
  CLOSED ──(too many failures/slow)──▶ OPEN ──(timer)──▶ HALF_OPEN
  HALF_OPEN ──(trials pass)──▶ CLOSED   |   HALF_OPEN ──(trials fail)──▶ OPEN
War story — the retry storm that ate the cluster

Service: checkout-api → tax-api (one call per checkout), ~1,200 RPS peak, 40 client pods.

Symptom: 14:02 — tax-api p99 blipped from 120ms to ~3s for ~20 seconds (a GC pause on their side). By 14:03 tax-api was returning 100% errors and checkout-api error rate hit 90%. The blip should have been invisible to users.

Investigation: tax-api's inbound RPS graph showed traffic spike from 1,200 to ~4,800 the instant it got slow — then stay there. checkout-api's Feign client was configured with maxAttempts=3 and a fixed 100ms backoff, no jitter, no circuit breaker. Every request that hit the 3s slowness retried 3× — and because there was no jitter, all 40 pods retried on the same 100ms/200ms ticks.

Root cause: A small latency blip caused mass timeouts; blind synchronized retries multiplied load 3–4× and landed it in tight waves on a service that was already GC-thrashing. tax-api couldn't recover under 4× load, so it stayed down — a self-sustaining retry storm / thundering herd. The original 20-second GC pause turned into a 25-minute outage.

Fix (immediate): Drop Feign retries to maxAttempts=1 at the edge; add a Resilience4j circuit breaker on the tax-api client (failureRate 50%, slowCallRate 80% over a 50-call window, open 10s). Once it opened, checkout-api fell back to a cached tax estimate and tax-api saw only its real 1,200 RPS and recovered in seconds.

Fix (structural): Exponential backoff WITH full jitter on the single remaining retry layer; a retry budget capping retries to 10% of traffic; Retry-After on tax-api's 429s; and a load-shedding filter on tax-api that returns 503 early when its queue depth exceeds a threshold so it degrades instead of collapsing.

Lesson: Retries without jitter, a budget, and a circuit breaker don't add resilience — they convert a brief downstream blip into a cascading outage. The retry was the outage. Fail fast and shed load beat "try harder."

Production-grade Resilience4j config + annotated client (correct decorator order)
# application.yml — per-dependency instances
resilience4j:
  circuitbreaker:
    instances:
      taxApi:
        sliding-window-type: COUNT_BASED
        sliding-window-size: 50
        minimum-number-of-calls: 20          # don't trip on a tiny sample
        failure-rate-threshold: 50           # % failures → OPEN
        slow-call-rate-threshold: 80         # % slow calls → OPEN
        slow-call-duration-threshold: 2s     # a 200 in 9s is still a failure
        wait-duration-in-open-state: 10s
        permitted-number-of-calls-in-half-open-state: 5
        register-health-indicator: true
  retry:
    instances:
      taxApi:
        max-attempts: 3                      # 1 call + 2 retries
        wait-duration: 200ms
        enable-exponential-backoff: true
        exponential-backoff-multiplier: 2
        enable-randomized-wait: true         # JITTER — critical
        randomized-wait-factor: 0.5
        retry-exceptions:                    # transient only
          - java.io.IOException
          - java.util.concurrent.TimeoutException
        ignore-exceptions:                   # never retry these
          - com.acme.BadRequestException
  bulkhead:
    instances:
      taxApi:
        max-concurrent-calls: 30             # isolate concurrency to tax-api
        max-wait-duration: 0                 # full → reject fast (shed)
  timelimiter:
    instances:
      taxApi:
        timeout-duration: 2s
        cancel-running-future: true          # actually cancel, free the thread
  ratelimiter:
    instances:
      taxApi:
        limit-for-period: 800
        limit-refresh-period: 1s
        timeout-duration: 0                  # over limit → fail fast, don't queue

// Annotation order = decoration order applied OUTER → INNER:
//   @Retry ▸ @CircuitBreaker ▸ @RateLimiter ▸ @TimeLimiter ▸ @Bulkhead
// Retry outermost: one attempt = one full pass through the inner stack, with jitter.
// CircuitBreaker inside Retry: counts each real attempt (incl. timeouts) as a failure
//   and, once OPEN, short-circuits before any retry burns budget.
// TimeLimiter: turns a slow call into a countable failure (needs an async source).
// Bulkhead innermost: caps concurrency right at the blocking call.
@Retry(name = "taxApi")
@CircuitBreaker(name = "taxApi", fallbackMethod = "taxFallback")
@RateLimiter(name = "taxApi")
@TimeLimiter(name = "taxApi")
@Bulkhead(name = "taxApi", type = Bulkhead.Type.THREADPOOL)
public CompletableFuture<Tax> computeTax(CheckoutId id, Money subtotal) {
    return CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> taxClient.compute(id, subtotal));
}

// Fallback: same args + trailing Throwable. Local only — never another remote call.
private CompletableFuture<Tax> taxFallback(CheckoutId id, Money subtotal, Throwable t) {
    if (t instanceof CallNotPermittedException) {
        Log.debugf("taxApi circuit OPEN — serving estimate for %s", id);
    } else {
        Log.warnf(t, "taxApi failed — serving estimate for %s", id);
    }
    return CompletableFuture.completedFuture(Tax.estimate(subtotal)); // degrade, don't fail
}
Interview scenario — "Diagnose and prevent a cascading failure"

A downstream had a 20-second latency blip, but your whole checkout flow was down for 25 minutes. Walk me through it.

That's the classic retry-storm shape: a small blip amplified by our own retries. When the downstream got slow, our calls hit their timeout, and the client was retrying 3× with a fixed backoff and no jitter. So a brief slowness turned into 3–4× synchronized load landing on a service that was already struggling — it couldn't recover under that load, so it stayed down. The downstream's inbound RPS graph jumping to 4× the instant it got slow is the tell.

How did you confirm it was retries and not real traffic?

Two signals. First, the downstream's request rate quadrupled with no corresponding jump in our own inbound user traffic — that delta is retries. Second, the spikes were periodic, clustered on the same backoff intervals across all pods, because there was no jitter. Real traffic is smoother; a retry storm is bursty and synchronized.

What stops the bleeding fastest?

A circuit breaker on that client. Once the failure or slow-call rate crosses threshold it opens and fails fast, so we stop calling the dying dependency entirely — that instantly removes the amplified load and lets it recover. Behind the open breaker we serve a fallback: a cached or estimated value so checkout still completes, degraded. I'd also cut retries to a single layer immediately.

And the structural fixes so it never happens again?

Four. One, retries only at one layer, transient-only, with exponential backoff plus jitter and a retry budget capping retries to ~10% of traffic — so retries can never amplify load unbounded. Two, a circuit breaker per dependency that counts slow calls as failures, not just errors, since the trigger here was latency, not 5xx. Three, bulkhead isolation per dependency so one slow one can't starve the others. Four, on the downstream side, load shedding: reject early with 503 and Retry-After when queue depth is high, so it degrades gracefully instead of collapsing.

Why does the decorator order matter for any of this?

Because it changes what each layer counts and protects. Retry has to be outermost so one attempt is a full pass through the inner stack and so the breaker, sitting inside it, sees each real attempt — including timeouts — and can short-circuit before a retry even runs. The TimeLimiter has to sit where the breaker can count its timeouts as failures, and the bulkhead has to be innermost, right around the blocking call, to cap its concurrency. Put the breaker outside the retry and it never sees the retries hammering the downstream — the protection silently does nothing.

How to actually grow into A strong engineer

How to study this effectively
  • Do not just learn definitions. Always ask: which production bug relates to this topic?
  • Do not just learn annotations. Learn the mechanism behind them: proxy, transaction, lifecycle, thread, lock.
  • Do not just make code run. Also learn observability, rollback, idempotency, retry and performance.
How to apply them in real-world engineering
  • Start with a short concept.
  • Give a real project example.
  • State the trade-offs.
  • Mention a pitfall you have faced or that could happen.
  • Explain how you verify it with logs, metrics, tests or benchmarks.
